Tahaluf in Iqalah, Kitabah, and Salam: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on tahaluf after iqalah, in kitabah, and in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on mutual oath after iqalah (rescission) in sale, kitabah, and salam, and identify the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission; after the buyer returns the goods, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no mutual oath, but Muhammad disagrees.

The principle of mutual oath (tahaluf) is not applied uniformly across all contracts. In this lesson, we examine its application after iqalah (rescission of sale), in kitabah (contract of emancipation), and in salam (advance payment sale). The Hanafi founders differ on these extensions, revealing the methodological approach to analogizing the original rule from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on iqalah in sale
The passage states that if the buyer returns the sold item by iqalah and later disputes the price, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no mutual oath; Muhammad says they perform mutual oath.
Iqalah is considered a rescission (faskh) in their view, but the two argue the rule of tahaluf is limited to absolute sales before delivery.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf’s basis
Muhammad sees that the textual ruling (Sunnah) is exceptioned after delivery as well, so the analogy to sale before delivery holds. The two argue the ruling is only for the absolute sale before delivery, and iqalah after delivery does not fall under it.
The dispute hinges on whether the Sunnah ruling is limited to the scenario before delivery or extends to rescission after delivery.
3
Examine the ruling in kitabah
Abu Hanifa says no mutual oath regarding the amount of kitabah; the slave's statement is taken with oath. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say they perform mutual oath and the kitabah is rescinded.
Abu Hanifa argues the compensation is not binding on the mukatib, so it is not analogous to sale.
4
Examine the ruling in salam after iqalah
If after iqalah of a salam contract the parties dispute the capital amount, no mutual oath; the recipient's (musallam ilayhi) statement is taken with oath.
Iqalah in salam is considered a dropping (isqat), not a rescission, so the contract does not return.

والتحالف فيه يفضي إلى الفسخ ولا كذلك بعد هلاكها لارتفاع العقد فلم يكن في معناه، ولأنه لا يبالي بالاختلاف في السبب بعد حصول المقصود، وإنما يراعى من الفائدة ما العقد، وفائدة دفع زيادة الثمن ليست من موجباته. وهذا إذا كان الثمن ديناً، فإن كان عيناً يتحالفان لأن المبيع كل منهما فكان قائماً ببقاء المعقود عليه فيرده ويرد الآخر مثل الهالك إذا كان مثلياً وقيمته إن كان قيمياً بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا في جنس الثمن بأن ادعى أحدهما أنه دراهم والآخر أنه دنانير لأنهما لم يتفقا على ثمن فلا بد من التحالف للفسخ، وهنا اتفقا عليه وهو كاف للصحة. وبهذا علم أن الاختلاف في جنس الثمن كالاختلاف في قدره إلا في مسألة هي ما إذا كان المبيع هالكاً. وفي الظهيرية إبراهيم عن محمد في رجل اشترى تبناً في موضعين بكذا درهماً وقبض تبن أحد الموضعين وذهب الريح بتبن الموضع الآخر واختلفا في مقدار ما قبض وما ذهب، فإن كان ما قبض قائماً تحالفا أو ترادا وإن كان مستهلكاً فالقول قول المشتري في قياس قول أبي حنيفة . وقال محمد يتحالفان ويرد المشتري مثل ما أخذ من التبن والقول فيه قوله اهـ. وفي إيضاح الكرماني : لو اختلفا بعد هلاك الجارية في يد المشتري فادعى البائع أن الثمن عين هذا العبد أو ادعى المشتري أن الثمن عين وادعى البائع أن الثمن دين لم ينظر إلى دعوى البائع وإنما ينظر إلى دعوى المشتري لأن المبيع في جانب البائع هالك فكان القول في الثمن قول المشتري، فإن أقر بالدين فالقول قوله، وإن أقر بالعين يتحالفا لأن المبيع في جانبه قائم . ولو تحالفا وقد هلك أحد العوضين من يد الآخر رد مثله إن كان مثلياً، وقيمته إن لم يكن له مثل لأن العقد قد انفسخ فبقي مقبوضاً من غير عقد فصار كالغاصب اهـ. وفي كافي المصنف : ادعى شراء أمة قبضها وماتت بألف وبهذا الوصيف وقيمته خمسمائة وقال البائع هو مات البائع، فإن مات المشتري والسلعة في يد البائع يتحالفان عند الكل وإن كانت السلعة في يد ورثة المشتري عندهما لا يتحالفان وعلى قول محمد يتحالفان. وهلاك العاقد بمنزلة المعقود عليه. وممن ذكر مسألة التغير بالزيادة والنقص الاختيار والمنهاج والتغير بالعيب الدرر والغرر والله أعلم. واقعة حال اختلف المشتري مع الوكيل بقبض الثمن هل يجري التحالف بينهما؟ وقد كتبت الجواب : لا يجري إذ الوكيل بالقبض لا يحلف وإن ملك الخصومة عند الإمام فيدفع الثمن الذي أقر به له، وإذا حضر الموكل المباشر للعقد وطلبه بالزيادة يتحالفان حينئذ اهـ . قوله : (بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا جنس الثمن) أي بعد هلاك المبيع وهذا مقابل لقوله وأما إذا اختلفا بعد هلاك المبيع الخ» فإن هناك في الاختلاف في مقدار الثمن كما قدمناه عن المعراج فصحت المقابلة قوله : (وبهذا علم) أي بقوله بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا جنس الثمن .
المشتري بعت بألفين حلف المشتري في ثلثي الأمة وتحالفا في ثلثها وبعكسه حلف المشتري. وفيه : اختلفا في موت المبيع عند أحدهما فبرهن البائع أنه مات عند المشتري بعد القبض وبرهن أنه مات في يد البائع قبل القبض فالبينة لبائعه وإن وقفا فلا سابق والقتل كالموت، لو برهن المشتري أن البائع قتله بعد البيع بيوم فبرهن البائع أن المشتري قتله بعد البيع بيومين فالبينة للمشتري للسبق .اهـ وأما إذا اختلفا أي المولى والمكاتب في بدل الكتابة أي في قدره فعدم التحالف في قول الإمام الأعظم والقول للعبد مع يمينه وقالا : يتحالفان وتفسخ الكتابة كالبيع بجامع قبول الفسخ وله أن التحالف في المعاوضات اللازمة وبدل الكتابة غير لازم على المكاتب مطلقاً فلم تكن في معنى البيع ولأن فائدته النكول ليقضى عليه والمكاتب لا يقضى عليه به، وإن أقام أحدهما بينة قبلت وإن أقاماها فبينة المولى أولى لإثباتها الزيادة لكن يعتق بأداء قدر ما برهن عليه ولا يمنع وجوبه بدل الكتابة بعد عتقه كما لو كاتبه على ألف على أنه إن أدى خمسمائة عتق، وكما لو استحق البدل بعد الأداء. وأما إذا اختلفا أي رب السلم والمسلم إليه بعد إقالة عقد السلم في مقدار رأس المال لم يتحالفا والقول للمسلم إليه مع يمينه ولا يعود السلم لأن الإقالة في باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض لأنه إسقاط فلا يعود بخلاف البيع كما سيأتي. وينبغي أخذاً من تعليلهم أنهما لو اختلفا في جنسه أو نوعه أو صفته بعدها فالحكم كذلك ولم أره صريحاً. واعلم أن حكم رأس المال بعد الإقالة كحكمه قبلها فلا يجوز الاستبدال به بعدها إلا في مسألتين لا تحالف إذا اختلفا فيه بعدها بخلاف ما قبلها، ولا يشترط لصحتها قبضه قبل الافتراق بخلاف ما قبلها وهذه قدمناها في بابه. وقيد بالاختلاف بعدها لأنهما لو اختلفا قبلها في قدره تحالفا كالاختلاف في جنسه ونوعه وصفته كالاختلاف في المسلم فيه في الوجوه الأربعة على ما قدمناه. وقد علم من تقريرهم هنا أن الإقالة تقبل الإقالة إلا في إقالة السلم وأن الإبراء لا يقبلها وقد كتبناه في الفوائد.
قوله : (وإن اختلفا في مقدار الثمن بعد الإقالة تحالفا أي اختلف البائع والمشتري في مقداره بأن قال المشتري كان الثمن ألفاً وقال البائع خمسمائة ولا بينة لهما فإنهما يتحالفان ويعود البيع الأول. أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا كان كل من المبيع والثمن مقبوضاً ولم يرده المشتري إلى بائعه، فأما إذا رد المشتري المبيع إليه بحكم الإقالة فلا تحالف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لأنه يرى النص معلولاً بعد القبض أيضاً وهما قالا : كان ينبغي أن لا تحالف مطلقاً لأنه إنما ثبت في البيع المطلق بالسنة والإقالة فسخ في حقهما إلا أنه قبل القبض على وفق القياس فوجب القياس عليه كما قسنا الإجارة على البيع قبل القبض والوارث على العاقد والقيمة على العين فيما إذا استهلكه في يد البائع غير المشتري قوله : (وإن اختلفا قوله : (وبعكسه حلف أي لو ادعى البائع المبيع بألف وهذا الوصيف والمشتري الشراء بألفين .
Book of claims, chapter of mutual oath. The price is obligated, so they perform mutual oath just as when they differ on the type of price after destruction of the commodity. And for them, mutual oath after delivery is contrary to analogy because he [the seller] has surrendered to the buyer what he claims. And the Shariah has come with it in the case of the commodity existing, and mutual oath therein leads to rescission, and not so after its destruction because the contract is lifted, so it is not in its meaning. And because one does not care about the difference in the cause after obtaining the purpose. He only considers from the benefit what the contract [requires], and the benefit of repelling an increase in price is not among its requirements. This is when the price is a debt. If it is a specific thing, they perform mutual oath because each of them is a seller, so it remains due to the survival of the contracted object, so he returns it and the other returns the like of the destroyed item if it is fungible, and its value if it is non-fungible. Contrary to when they differ on the type of price: one claims it is dirhams and the other dinars, because they have not agreed on a price, so mutual oath is necessary for rescission. Here they have agreed on it, and that is sufficient for validity. By this it is known that the difference in the type of price is like the difference in its amount except in one case: when the sold item is destroyed. In al-Dhahiriya: Ibrahim from Muhammad: A man bought straw from two places for so many dirhams, took possession of the straw of one place, and the wind blew away the straw of the other place. They differed on the amount of what was taken and what was lost. If what was taken exists, they perform mutual oath or return. If it is consumed, then the statement is the buyer's according to the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement. Muhammad said: They perform mutual oath, and the buyer returns the like of what he took of straw, and the statement is his word. End. In Idah al-Kirmani: If they differ after the slave-girl is destroyed in the buyer's hand: the seller claims the price is this specific slave, or the buyer claims the price is a specific thing and the seller claims the price is debt, the seller's claim is not considered; only the buyer's claim is considered because the sold item is destroyed on the seller's side, so the statement regarding the price is the buyer's. If he admits debt, his statement is accepted; if he admits a specific thing, they perform mutual oath because the sold item on his side is existing. If they perform mutual oath and one of the two exchanges is destroyed in the other's hand, he returns its like if it is fungible, or its value if it has no like, because the contract is rescinded so what is held is held without a contract, thus he becomes like a usurper. End. In Kafi al-Musannif: He claimed to have bought a slave-girl, took possession, and she died for a thousand, and this boy worth five hundred. The seller said he [the buyer]... The seller died. If the buyer dies and the commodity is in the seller's hand, they perform mutual oath according to all. If the commodity is in the hands of the buyer's heirs, according to the two they do not perform mutual oath, but according to Muhammad they do. The death of the contracting party is like the destruction of the subject matter. Among those who mentioned the issue of change by increase and decrease are al-Ikhtiyar and al-Minhaj, and change by defect al-Durar and al-Ghurar. And Allah knows best. A real case: The buyer differed with the agent for receipt of the price: is mutual oath applicable between them? I have written the answer: It does not apply because the agent for receipt does not take an oath even if he possesses the right of litigation according to the Imam; he pays the price he admits. When the principal, who conducted the contract, appears and demands an increase, they perform mutual oath then. End. His statement: (Contrary to when they differ in the type of price) meaning after destruction of the sold item, and this is contrasted with his statement: 'As for when they differ after destruction of the sold item...' for there the difference is in the amount of the price as we presented from al-Mi'raj, so the contrast is correct. His statement: (And by this it is known) meaning by his statement: 'Contrary to when they differ in the type of price.'
The buyer [said]: I sold for two thousand. The buyer takes an oath for two-thirds of the slave-girl, and they perform mutual oath for one-third. In the opposite case, the buyer takes an oath. In it: They differed on the death of the sold item in one's hand. The seller proved that it died with the buyer after delivery, and he proved that it died in the seller's hand before delivery; the evidence is for the seller. If they are equal, then there is no precedence. Killing is like death: If the buyer proved that the seller killed it one day after sale, and the seller proved that the buyer killed it two days after sale, the evidence is for the buyer due to precedence. End. As for when they differ – i.e., the master and the mukatib – in the amount of the kitabah, i.e., its amount, there is no mutual oath according to the statement of the Greatest Imam. The statement is the slave's with his oath. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: They perform mutual oath and the kitabah is rescinded like a sale, due to the common factor of accepting rescission. He [Abu Hanifa] argued that mutual oath applies to binding compensations, and the compensation of kitabah is not binding on the mukatib absolutely, so it is not similar to a sale. Also, its benefit is that the one who refuses to take an oath is judged against, and the mukatib is not judged against him. If one brings evidence, it is accepted. If both bring evidence, the master's evidence is preferred because it establishes an increase, but he is freed by paying the amount proven, and it does not prevent the obligation of the kitabah after his freedom, as if he contracted kitabah for a thousand on condition that if he pays five hundred he is freed, and as if the substitute is taken after payment. As for when they differ – i.e., the owner of salam and the recipient of salam after iqalah of the salam contract – in the amount of the capital, they do not perform mutual oath. The statement is the recipient's with his oath, and the salam does not return because iqalah in the chapter of salam does not permit rescission because it is a dropping, so it does not return, unlike a sale as will come. By analogy from their reasoning, if they differ in its jins, type, or description after it, the ruling is likewise, though I have not seen it explicitly. Know that the ruling of the capital after iqalah is like its ruling before it, so substitution with it is not permitted after it except in two issues: no mutual oath when they differ in it after it, unlike before it; and its validity does not require its possession before separation, unlike before it. These we have presented in its chapter. The restriction to difference after it is because if they differ before it in its amount, they perform mutual oath like the difference in its jins, type, and description like the difference in the muslam fihi in the four aspects as we presented. And it is known from their exposition here that iqalah accepts iqalah except in iqalah of salam, and that ibra does not accept it, and we have written it in the benefits.
His statement: (And if they differ in the amount of the price after iqalah, they perform mutual oath) meaning the seller and buyer differ in its amount: the buyer says the price was one thousand and the seller says five hundred, and there is no evidence for them, then they perform mutual oath and the first sale returns. He [the author] made it absolute, but it is restricted to when both the sold item and the price are taken and the buyer has not returned the sold item to his seller. If the buyer returns the sold item to him by virtue of iqalah, then there is no mutual oath according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because he [Muhammad] sees that the ruling is exceptioned after delivery as well. The two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] said: There should be no mutual oath absolutely because it is only established in absolute sale by the Sunnah, and iqalah is a rescission in their judgment, except that before delivery it is contrary to analogy, so it must be analogized to it as we analogized hire to sale before delivery, and the heir to the contracting party, and the value to the [physical] thing in case of consumption in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer. His statement: (And if they differ) His statement: (And in the opposite case he takes an oath) meaning if the seller claims the sold item is for a thousand and this boy, and the buyer claims purchase for two thousand.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Raiq commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the fiqh of mutual oath (tahaluf) in sales. The core ruling is that when the sold commodity is destroyed and the parties differ on the price, they perform mutual oath as prescribed by the Sunnah. However, there are distinctions: if the price is a debt, the ruling applies; if it is a specific thing, they also perform mutual oath but with different consequences. The commentator explains that disagreement on the type of price (e.g., dirhams vs. dinars) is treated like disagreement on the amount, leading to mutual oath for rescission, except in one case when the sold item is destroyed, as detailed in al-Dhahiriya and Iydah al-Kirmani. The text then presents a complex case from Kafi al-Musannif involving a slave-girl and a boy, partial destruction, and death of parties, with ikhtilaf between Muhammad and the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Following that, a waqi'ah (real case) about dispute with an agent concludes that the agent does not take an oath. The commentary also addresses the phrase 'contrary to when they differ in the type of price' clarifying that it refers to after destruction. Later, the text covers mutual oath in kitabah: according to Abu Hanifa, there is no mutual oath; the slave's statement is taken with an oath, while the two say they perform mutual oath and the contract is rescinded. In salam, after iqalah, there is no mutual oath if they differ on the capital; the recipient's statement is accepted. Finally, the passage discusses iqalah in sale: if they differ on the price after iqalah, they perform mutual oath unless the buyer has returned the sold item, in which case Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no mutual oath, while Muhammad disagrees. This illustrates the Hanafi method of applying the rule of tahaluf by analogy to various contracts, with exceptions based on the nature of the contract and whether the subject matter is destroyed.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a contract by mutual consent; in this text, it is considered a new sale or a rescission depending on context.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of emancipation where a slave pays the master in installments and is freed upon completion.
سلم (salam): Advanced payment sale: buyer pays in full for goods to be delivered later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on mutual oath between a master and a mukatib (slave under kitabah) according to Abu Hanifa?
A) They perform mutual oath and the kitabah is rescinded.
B) No mutual oath; the slave's statement is taken with his oath.
C) No mutual oath; the master's statement is taken with his oath.
D) They perform mutual oath only if the kitabah is for a fixed amount.

Key Takeaway
Mutual oath after iqalah in sale is subject to intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: when the buyer returns the goods, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf reject tahaluf, while Muhammad affirms it.

Dispute on Kitaba Amount: Tahalf or Slave's Oath?
Ruling and ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on dispute over kitaba payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's position that tahalf does not apply in kitaba disputes and distinguish the Sahibayn's opposing view, applying the Hanafi principle that tahalf is only for binding transactions.

Core Idea
Kitaba payment is non-binding, so no mutual oaths; the slave's word with oath prevails.

When a master and his mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) disagree on the amount of the kitaba payment, a procedural question arises: do they take mutual oaths (tahalf) and annul the contract, as in a sale, or is the slave's word accepted? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and identifies the mufta bihi (operative) ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the master and mukatib disagree on the amount of the kitaba payment, and neither has evidence. The question is how to resolve this dispute procedurally.
The ruling on evidence (biena) is separate; here we assume no evidence.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its dalil
Abu Hanifa holds: no mutual oaths; the word of the mukatib (slave) is accepted with his oath. His dalil: tahalf is only for binding transactions (muawadat lazimah), but the kitaba payment is not binding on the mukatib – he cannot be forced to pay, only the master cannot force him to work. Therefore, the analogy to sale fails.
The key distinction: binding vs. non-binding transaction.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its dalil
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold: they do take mutual oaths and the kitaba is annulled. Their dalil: qiyas (analogy) to a sale, because both admit of annulment (qabul al-faskh).
They focus on the possibility of annulment, not bindingness.
4
Analyze the qiyas and the Hanafi response
Examine the qiyas: the illa (effective cause) advanced by the Sahibayn is 'admission of annulment'. Abu Hanifa counters that the essential characteristic of a sale is bindingness (luzum), which is missing in kitaba. Since the illa is not shared, the analogy fails.
This illustrates a difference in usul al-fiqh: what constitutes a valid illa for qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the slave's word is taken and no annulment occurs; under the Sahibayn, mutual oaths lead to annulment of the kitaba. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa, as indicated by 'al-Imam al-A'zam' and later Hanafi fatwa.
The source text prioritizes Abu Hanifa's view ('قول الإمام الأعظم').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا اختلفا أي المولى والمكاتب في بدل الكتابة أي في قدره فعدم التحالف في قول الإمام الأعظم والقول للعبد مع يمينه وقالا : يتحالفان وتفسخ الكتابة كالبيع بجامع قبول الفسخ وله أن التحالف في المعاوضات اللازمة وبدل الكتابة غير لازم على المكاتب مطلقاً فلم تكن في معنى البيع ولأن فائدته النكول ليقضى عليه والمكاتب لا يقضى عليه به، وإن أقام أحدهما بينة قبلت وإن أقاماها فبينة المولى أولى لإثباتها الزيادة لكن يعتق بأداء قدر ما برهن عليه ولا يمنع وجوبه بدل الكتابة بعد عتقه كما لو كاتبه على ألف على أنه إن أدى خمسمائة عتق، وكما لو استحق البدل بعد الأداء.
And as for when the master and the mukatib differ regarding the kitaba payment, i.e., its amount, then there is no mutual oath-taking in the saying of Imam al-A'zam, and the say is with the slave with his oath. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: They take mutual oaths and the kitaba is annulled, analogous to a sale because both admit annulment. His evidence is that mutual oath-taking is in binding transactions, and the kitaba payment is not binding on the mukatib absolutely, so it is not like a sale. Also because its benefit is to force a default so that judgment is passed against him, but the mukatib cannot be judged with it. And if one of them produces evidence, it is accepted. If both produce evidence, the evidence of the master is given precedence because it proves an increase. However, he is freed by paying the amount proven, and the obligation of the kitaba payment is not prevented after his freedom, just as if he contracted kitaba for a thousand such that if he pays five hundred he is freed, and just as if the payment is claimed after payment.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dispute between a master and his mukatib over the amount of the kitaba payment. Abu Hanifa holds that there is no mutual oath-taking (tahalf); the oath of the slave is accepted. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that they do take mutual oaths and the kitaba is annulled, analogizing it to a sale. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that mutual oaths are only for binding transactions, and the kitaba payment is not binding on the mukatib. The text then addresses when evidence is produced: if only one has evidence, it is accepted; if both do, the master's evidence is preferred because it proves a higher amount, but the slave is freed by paying that amount.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum for his freedom in installments
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitaba): The payment agreed upon in the kitaba contract that the slave must pay to the master to obtain freedom
تحالف (tahalf): A legal procedure where both parties in a contract take oaths to support their claims when neither has evidence, leading to annulment of the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what happens when a master and mukatib differ on the kitaba amount and neither has evidence?
A) The contract is annulled after both take oaths.
B) The master's word is accepted with his oath.
C) The slave's word is accepted with his oath.
D) The amount is split equally between both claims.

Key Takeaway
In a kitaba dispute without evidence, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that the slave's word is taken with his oath, because tahalf applies only to binding transactions.

Ruling on Dispute After Iqala in Salam: No Mutual Oaths
Ruling that no mutual oath after iqala in salam; say with muslim ilayh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when parties to a salam contract differ on the capital after iqala and explain why mutual oaths are not required.

Core Idea
After iqala in salam, the word of the muslim ilayh is accepted because iqala is a relinquishment, not a rescission.

When parties rescind a salam contract and later dispute the capital amount, a special rule applies. Unlike before rescission, they do not take mutual oaths. This lesson unpacks the rationale: iqala in salam is an isqat (relinquishment) that does not restore the original contract, so the ordinary procedure for disputed contracts does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute Scenario
The parties (rabb al-salam and muslim ilayh) have already performed iqala (mutual rescission) of the salam contract. After that, they disagree on the amount of the capital (ra's al-mal) that was given.
The iqala has already occurred; the dispute is about the past capital amount.
2
State the Ruling
The ruling is: no mutual oaths (tahalf) are taken, and the say is with the muslim ilayh (the receiver of the capital) upon his oath.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the Legal Reasoning
The reason is that iqala in salam is treated as isqat (relinquishment), not faskh (rescission). Therefore, it does not revive the original contract, so the normal rule for disputes about a contract's terms (which would require mutual oaths) does not apply.
Contrast with iqala in a regular sale, which is faskh and would allow mutual oaths if a dispute arises after rescission.
4
Note the Exceptions and Additional Points
The author mentions two differences from disputes before iqala: no tahalf after iqala (unlike before) and no requirement of possession before separation. Also, the same ruling applies if they differ on the capital's genus, type, or quality.
The iqala itself cannot be rescinded (iqala does not accept iqala), unlike in other contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا اختلفا أي رب السلم والمسلم إليه بعد إقالة عقد السلم في مقدار رأس المال لم يتحالفا والقول للمسلم إليه مع يمينه ولا يعود السلم لأن الإقالة في باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض لأنه إسقاط فلا يعود بخلاف البيع كما سيأتي. وينبغي أخذاً من تعليلهم أنهما لو اختلفا في جنسه أو نوعه أو صفته بعدها فالحكم كذلك ولم أره صريحاً. واعلم أن حكم رأس المال بعد الإقالة كحكمه قبلها فلا يجوز الاستبدال به بعدها إلا في مسألتين لا تحالف إذا اختلفا فيه بعدها بخلاف ما قبلها، ولا يشترط لصحتها قبضه قبل الافتراق بخلاف ما قبلها وهذه قدمناها في بابه. وقيد بالاختلاف بعدها لأنهما لو اختلفا قبلها في قدره تحالفا كالاختلاف في جنسه ونوعه وصفته كالاختلاف في المسلم فيه في الوجوه الأربعة على ما قدمناه. وقد علم من تقريرهم هنا أن الإقالة تقبل الإقالة إلا في إقالة السلم وأن الإبراء لا يقبلها وقد كتبناه في الفوائد.
And as for when the party giving salam and the party receiving salam differ after rescission of the salam contract regarding the amount of the capital, they do not take mutual oaths, and the say is with the party receiving salam with his oath, and the salam does not return because rescission in the chapter of salam does not permit reversal because it is a relinquishment, so it does not return, contrary to a sale as will come. And it is appropriate, derived from their reasoning, that if they differ in its genus, type, or quality after it, the ruling is likewise, though I have not seen it explicitly. And know that the ruling of the capital after rescission is like its ruling before it, so it is not permissible to substitute it after it except in two issues: no mutual oath if they differ after it unlike before it, and it is not a condition for its validity that it be taken before separation unlike before it, and we have presented these in its chapter. And it is restricted by the difference after it because if they differ before it in its amount, they take mutual oaths like the difference in its genus, type, and quality like the difference in the subject matter of salam (muslam fihi) in the four respects as we have presented. And it is known from their discourse here that rescission accepts rescission except in the rescission of salam, and that absolution does not accept it, and we have written it in the benefits.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when the parties to a salam contract differ after having rescinded (iqala) regarding the capital amount. The ruling is that they do not take mutual oaths; the word of the muslim ilayh (the receiver of the capital) is accepted with his oath. The reason is that iqala in salam is considered an isqat (relinquishment) and not a faskh (rescission) that would restore the original contract, unlike in a sale. The author notes that the same applies if they differ on the type or quality of the capital. Additionally, two differences from before iqala are highlighted: no tahalf after iqala (unlike before) and no requirement of possession before separation. The passage also clarifies that iqala in salam does not itself accept iqala (it cannot be rescinded).

Key Terms
إقالة (iqala): A mutual rescission of a contract; in salam, it is an isqat (relinquishment) rather than a lifting of the contract
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The party who pays the capital in a salam contract (the buyer in advance payment sale)
مسلم إليه (muslim ilayh): The party who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the specified commodity later
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or price paid in advance in a salam contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After rescission of a salam contract, if the parties differ on the capital amount, what is the ruling?
A) They take mutual oaths.
B) No mutual oaths; the word is with the muslim ilayh.
C) The contract is automatically annulled.
D) The capital is divided equally.

Key Takeaway
After iqala in salam, the word of the muslim ilayh is taken on oath because the rescission is a relinquishment, not a restoration of the contract.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Tahalf After Iqala When Item Returned
Dispute on price after iqala: tahalf and return of sale, with condition and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which tahalf applies after iqala according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and explain Muhammad al-Shaybani's opposing view.

Core Idea
Tahalf after iqala depends on whether the sold item has been returned; if returned, no oaths according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

When a buyer and seller disagree on the price after mutual rescission (iqala), the basic rule is that they take mutual oaths (tahalf) and the sale reverts. However, this rule is conditional: it only applies if the buyer still possesses the sold item. If the buyer has already returned it, a key intra-Hanafi dispute emerges between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequences of that difference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic ruling
The matn states that if buyer and seller dispute the price after iqala, they perform tahalf and the sale returns. Identify that this is an absolute statement that requires qualification.
2
Identify the condition
The commentary restricts the ruling: it applies only when both item and price have been taken possession of, and crucially, the buyer has not returned the sold item to the seller. Recognize that this condition is the point of contention.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if the buyer has returned the item, no tahalf applies. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: tahalf still applies even after return. This is an internal Hanafi difference.
4
Follow the reasoning behind each view
The majority argue that tahalf is established in absolute sale by sunnah and iqala is a faskh (annulment). Before possession, it followed analogy, but after possession the ruling is confined to the textual proof. Muhammad sees the text as general and applicable even after possession.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, no oaths are taken, so the dispute remains unresolved unless evidence surfaces. Under Muhammad, oaths are taken and the sale reverts. The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن اختلفا في مقدار الثمن بعد الإقالة (تحالفا أي اختلف البائع والمشتري في مقداره بأن قال المشتري كان الثمن ألفاً وقال البائع خمسمائة ولا بينة لهما فإنهما يتحالفان ويعود البيع الأول. أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا كان كل من المبيع والثمن مقبوضاً ولم يرده المشتري إلى بائعه، فأما إذا رد المشتري المبيع إليه بحكم الإقالة فلا تحالف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لأنه يرى النص معلولاً بعد القبض أيضاً وهما قالا : كان ينبغي أن لا تحالف مطلقاً لأنه إنما ثبت في البيع المطلق بالسنة والإقالة فسخ في حقهما إلا أنه قبل القبض على وفق القياس فوجب القياس عليه كما قسنا الإجارة على البيع قبل القبض والوارث على العاقد والقيمة على العين فيما إذا استهلكه في يد البائع غير المشتري
His saying: (And if they differ in the amount of the price after rescission, they take mutual oaths) i.e., the buyer and seller differ in its amount, such that the buyer says the price was one thousand and the seller says five hundred, and there is no evidence for them, then they take mutual oaths and the original sale returns. This is absolute, and it is restricted to when both the sold item and the price have been taken possession of, and the buyer has not returned the sold item to his seller. As for if the buyer returns the sold item to him by virtue of the rescission, then no mutual oaths according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad because he sees the text as caused after possession as well. And they two said: It should be that there is no mutual oath absolutely because it is only established in absolute sale by the sunnah, and rescission is an annulment in their view, except that before possession it is in accordance with analogy, so analogy must be made upon it, just as we analogized hire to sale before possession, and the heir to the contracting party, and the value to the object in case it is consumed in the hand of the seller by someone other than the buyer.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of dispute on the price after iqala between buyer and seller. The matn says they take mutual oaths and the sale returns, but the commentator restricts this: it applies only if both the sold item and the price have been taken possession of and the buyer has not returned the sold item to the seller. If the buyer has returned the sold item by virtue of the iqala, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, there is no mutual oath-taking, while Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. The reasoning is that the sunnah only prescribed tahalf in absolute sales, and iqala is a faskh. However, before possession, the ruling is analogical, so we draw analogy to other contracts like hire before utilization.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale; in this context, it is considered a faskh (annulment) by the two, but implicit
تحالف (tahalf): Procedure where both parties take oaths when evidence is lacking, leading to return of the original contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a buyer and seller differ on the price after iqala and the buyer has already returned the sold item, do they take mutual oaths?
A) Yes, unconditionally.
B) No, they do not.
C) Only if the price is less than 100.
D) Only if the seller initiated the dispute.

Key Takeaway
The application of tahalf after iqala is conditioned on the buyer not having returned the sold item; the majority of Hanafi imams reject tahalf in that scenario.

Defense of Ownership from an Absent Third Party in Claims
Explains when a defendant's claim of ownership from an absent party dismisses the lawsuit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a defendant's claim of purchase from an absent third party does not dismiss a proven ownership claim, and distinguish it from a claim of an act (da‘wā al-fi‘l).

Core Idea
A possessor's claim of purchase from an absent party does not dismiss a plaintiff's proven ownership.

When a plaintiff proves ownership of an item, the defendant-possessor may try to avoid liability by claiming he bought it from an absent third party. This lesson examines whether such a defense succeeds. The Hanafi position is clear: the defense fails—the plaintiff's proof prevails—because the defendant, by virtue of possession, has already submitted to litigation. However, the rule shifts when the plaintiff's claim is based on an act (e.g., usurpation) rather than absolute ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the plaintiff's claim type
Determine whether the plaintiff is claiming absolute ownership (da‘wā al-mulk al-muṭlaq) or a specific act (da‘wā al-fi‘l) like usurpation. The ruling differs.
The passage mainly deals with absolute ownership claims but includes examples of act claims.
2
State the defendant's defense
The defendant-possessor (dhū al-yad) claims he acquired the item from an absent third party (e.g., bought it, received it as a gift, deposit, etc.) and provides evidence.
The defense must be supported by proof (bayyinah).
3
Apply the core ruling
If the plaintiff has already proven his ownership, the defendant's evidence of purchase from an absent party does not dismiss the lawsuit. The judge rules for the plaintiff.
Key phrase: 'lā tandafiʿ' (it does not prevent).
4
Distinguish claims of an act
If the plaintiff's claim is based on an act (e.g., 'he usurped it'), then a defense of deposit from an absent party also does not dismiss, because the defendant became a litigant due to the act, not possession alone.
For act claims, the passive vs. active verb form matters: only active verb triggers the ruling, passive is analogous (Muhammad) or treated as claim of act (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Early Hanafi scholars allowed the defense to dismiss if proven; later scholars (muftā bihī) reject it. Also, if the defendant claims that the plaintiff himself sold the item to the absent, some sources accept the defense, others do not.
The author sides with non-dismissal, calling it 'al-ṣaḥīḥ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قال ابتعته من الغائب أو قال المدعي غصبته أو سرق مني وقال ذو اليد أودعنيه فلان وبرهن عليه (لا أي لا تندفع بيان للمسألتين وحاصل أن المدعي ادعى في العين ملكاً مطلقاً وأنكره المدعى عليه فبرهن المدعي على الملك فدفعه ذو اليد بأنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب وبرهن عليه لم تندفع عنه الخصومة يعني فيقضي القاضي ببرهان المدعي لأنه لما أن يده يد ملك اعترف بكونه خصماً فالضمير في «قال» عائد على المدعى عليه. وفي البزازية وذكر الوتار : قال في غير مجلس الحاكم إنه ملكي ثم قال في مجلسه إنه وديعة عندي أو رهن عندي من فلان يندفع إذا برهن على ما ذكره، ولو برهن عليه المدعي أنه أقر بكونه في غير مجلس الحاكم يجعله خصماً ويحكم عليه بسبق إقراره ويمنع من الدفع ا هـ . وبه علم أن دعوى الشراء عن الغائب مثال، والمراد أن ذا اليد ادعى ملكاً لنفسه، سواء أطلقه أو قيده بشراء وهبة مع قبض أو صدقة كذلك. وأطلق في الشراء فشمل الفاسد كما في أدب القضاء للخصاف، ولهذا قال في البزازية أيضاً لو قال إنه ملكي ثم برهن على الوديعة لا اهـ. وأشار المؤلف بهذه المسألة إلى ما في البزازية في يده دار زعم شراءها يسمع فلان الغائب أو صدقة مقبوضة أو هبة كذلك منذ شهر أو أمس وبرهن أولاً وبرهن آخراً من أن هذا الغائب رهنها منه منذ شهر أو أجرها منه أو أعارها منه وقبضها وبرهن، يحكم بها للمستأجر والمستعير والمرتهن ولا تندفع الخصومة عن ذي اليد ثم ذو اليد بالخيار إن شاء سلم إلى المدعي وتربص إلى انقضاء المدة أو فك الرهن وإن شاء نقض البيع. وإن اختار مع القبض الاكتفاء محل نظر لأن ما في (ذ) بناء على اختيار اشتراط التوفيق وعدم بمجرد إمكان التوفيق كما مر مراراً. (فقط): متقدمو مشايخنا جوز وادفع الدفع، وبعض متأخريهم على أنه لا يصح، وقيل يصح ما لم يظهر احتيال وتلبيس (فش) : حكم له بمال ثم رفعاً إلى قاض آخر وجاء المدعى عليه بالدفع يسمع ويبطل بحكم الأول. وفيه: لو .اهـ. قوله : (مخالف لما قدمناه أي قريباً وقد علمت جوابه . أن عدم النقض فأدى البائع الدين وفك الرهن قبل قبضه ثم البيع، وإن كان المدعي برهن الدار له أعارها أو أجرها أو رهنها من الغائب أو اشتراها الغائب منه ولم ينقد الثمن قبل أن يشتريها منه ذو اليد يقضى بها للمدعي في الوجوه كلها. أما في الإعارة فلعدم اللزوم، وأما في الإجارة فلأنه عذر في الفسخ لأنه يريد إزالتها عن ملكه وأما في الشراء فلأن له حق عدل ا هـ . وبه علم أن دعوى الرهن أو الإجارة أو الإعارة من الغائب كدعوى الملك المطلق على ذي اليد. وقيد بدعوى الشراء من الغائب من غير أن يدعي أن المدعي باعها من الغائب، فلو ادعى ذو اليد أن المدعي باع العين من الغائب ففيه اختلاف قال في القنية : ادعى عليه عبداً وأثبته بالبينة فأقام المدعى عليه البينة أنك بعته من فلان الغائب فعلى ما عليه إشارات الجامع والزيادات لا تقبل وذكر الناطفي في أجناسه أنها تقبل وتندفع الدعوى، ثم إذا قبلت فإن لم يدع تلقي الملك من المشتري فأولى أن تقبل إذا ادعاه ا.هـ. وفيها قبله: ادعى عليه داراً أنها ملكه وأثبته بالبينة ثم أقام المدعي عليه بينة أن المدعي باعها من زوجته وباعتها هي مني تسمع وإذا لم تندفع في المسألة الأولى وأقام الخارج البينة فقضى له ثم جاء المقر له الغائب وبرهن تقبل بينته لأن الغائب لم يصر مقضياً عليه وإنما قضى على ذي اليد خاصة؛ ذكره الشارح. وحاصل الثانية أن المدعي ادعى فعلاً على ذي اليد فدفعه الإيداع من الغائب وبرهن فإنها لا تندفع لأنه إنما صار خصماً بدعوى الفعل عليه إلا بيده بخلاف دعوى الملك المطلق لأنه خصم فيه باعتبار يده حتى لا تصح دعواه على غير ذي اليد وتصح دعوى الفعل. وقد بنى فعل الغصب للفاعل وفعل السرقة للمفعول فخرج ما إذا بني الأول للمفعول بأن قال غصب مني كما في البزازية وإنما قيد في السرقة للمفعول ليعلم حكم ما إذا بناه للفاعل وهو اتفاق وفي المبني للمفعول الاختلاف فقال محمد هو كبناء فعل الغصب للفاعل وهو القياس، واستحسنا وجعلاه من دعوى الفعل عليه لأن في ذكر الفاعل إشاعة الفاحشة بخلاف الغصب ولو ادعاه بالمصدر لم يذكره الشارحون وفي البزازية : ادعى أنه ملكه وفي يده غصب وبرهن ذو اليد على الإيداع قيل تندفع لعدم دعوى الفعل عليه، والصحيح أنه لا تندفع ا هـ . وأراد بالبرهان إقامة البينة فخرج الإقرار لما في البزازية معزياً إلى الذخيرة: من الأولى قوله : (لأنه يريد إزالتها عن (ملكه أي لأن ذا اليد يريد إزلة الدار عن ملك المدعي بدعواه شراءها من الغائب فلهذا كان للمدعي حق الفسخ وتسلم الدار من ذي اليد وهو صريح في أن ذلك من أعذار فسخ الإجارة .
His statement: (And if he says, 'I bought it from the absent one,' or the plaintiff says, 'He usurped it' or 'stole from me,' and the possessor says, 'So-and-so deposited it with me' and proves it (it does not prevent the claim). Explanation of the two issues: The plaintiff claimed absolute ownership of the item and the defendant denied it. The plaintiff proved ownership, then the possessor defended by saying he bought it from an absent person and proved it; the claim is not dismissed from him, meaning the judge rules according to the plaintiff's proof because since his hand is a hand of ownership, he has acknowledged being a litigant. The pronoun in 'he said' refers to the defendant. In al-Bazzaziyya and Dhikr al-Wattar: He said outside the court that it is mine, then in court he said it is a deposit with me or a pledge with me from so-and-so; it is dismissed if he proves what he mentioned. If the plaintiff proves that he confessed outside court, it makes him a litigant and he is judged based on his prior confession and prevented from defense. End. From this it is known that the claim of purchase from an absent is an example; the meaning is that the possessor claimed ownership for himself, whether absolute or restricted to purchase, gift with possession, or charity likewise. He generalized in purchase so it includes the invalid, as in Adab al-Qada' by al-Khassaf. Hence he also said in al-Bazzaziyya: If he says it is mine then proves deposit, it does not prevent. End. The author alludes by this issue to what is in al-Bazzaziyya: In his hand is a house he claims to have bought from so-and-so, or charity taken possession of, or gift likewise from a month ago or yesterday, and he first proved, and then proved that this absent person pledged it to him a month ago or rented it to him or lent it to him and took possession, and proved; the judge rules for the lessee, borrower, and pledgee, and the claim is not dismissed from the possessor. Then the possessor has the choice: if he wishes he surrenders to the plaintiff and waits until the end of the term or redeems the pledge, and if he wishes he voids the sale. If he chooses satisfaction with possession, it is debatable because what is in (Dh) is based on choosing the condition of reconciliation... (Faqat): Our early scholars allowed and dismissed the defense, and some later scholars hold that it is not valid; some say it is valid as long as no fraud or disguise appears. (Fash): If a judge ruled in favor of someone for property, then they went to another judge and the defendant came with a defense, it is heard and the first ruling is voided. In it: If he brings the defense after the ruling in some places, it is not accepted, such as if he proves after the ruling that the plaintiff confessed before the lawsuit that he had no right in the house; the ruling is not voided because reconciliation is possible... He says (al-Haqir): It appears that if he proves before the ruling in a case where reconciliation is not hidden, it should not be accepted and the judge should rule according to the school that deems possibility of reconciliation sufficient... Then he transmitted in al-Bazzaziyya: The one against whom judgment is given cannot have his claim heard after it unless he proves the invalidity of the judgment... His statement: (Contrary to what we presented earlier) and you know its answer. That the seller paid the debt and redeemed the pledge before taking possession then the sale, and if the plaintiff proved the house was lent or rented or pledged to the absent person, or the absent person bought it from him and did not pay the price before the possessor bought it from him, the judge rules for the plaintiff in all cases. As for loan, because it is non-binding; as for rent, because it is an excuse for rescission since he wants to remove it from his ownership; as for purchase, because he has the right of recovery to exact the price. If the judge gives the house to the plaintiff, if he had rented it and had not received the rent, the judge takes a surety for his person until the end of the term; if he had received the rent or claimed a pledge, it is not given to the plaintiff and is placed with a trustworthy person. From this it is known that the claim of pledge, rent, or loan from an absent person is like the claim of absolute ownership against the possessor. He restricted it to the claim of purchase from an absent without the claim that the plaintiff sold it to the absent. If the possessor claims that the plaintiff sold the item to the absent, there is a difference of opinion. In al-Qunyah: He claimed a slave against him and proved it; then the defendant proved that you sold him to so-and-so; according to the signs of al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat, it is not accepted. Al-Natafi in his Aghnas mentioned that it is accepted and the claim is dismissed. Then if it is accepted, if he does not claim receipt of ownership from the buyer, it is more appropriate to accept if he claims it. End. And in it before: He claimed a house against him as his property and proved it, then the defendant proved that the plaintiff sold it to his wife and she sold it to me; it is heard. And if it is not dismissed in the first issue, and the outsider proves and is judged, then the absent person (the one acknowledged as owner) comes and proves, his evidence is accepted because the absent was not a party to the judgment; the judgment was only against the possessor. The commentator mentioned this. The summary of the second issue: The plaintiff claimed an act against the possessor, and the possessor defended by deposit from the absent and proved; it is not dismissed because he only became a litigant by the claim of an act against him except by his hand, unlike the claim of absolute ownership because he is a litigant in it by virtue of his hand, so his claim against someone other than the possessor is not valid, while a claim of an act is valid. He built the active verb of usurpation and the passive verb of theft, so if he built the first as passive by saying 'it was usurped from me' as in al-Bazzaziyya, he specified theft as passive to know the ruling if he built it as active, which is agreed upon; in the passive there is a difference. Muhammad said it is like building the verb of usurpation as active, which is qiyas; we (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) applied istihsan and made it a claim of an act against him because mentioning the active verb spreads immorality unlike usurpation. If he claimed by infinitive, the commentators did not mention it. In al-Bazzaziyya: He claimed it is his and in his hand as usurpation, and the possessor proved deposit; some said it is dismissed because there is no claim of act against him; the correct is that it is not dismissed. End. By 'proof' he meant the evidence, so confession is excluded as in al-Bazzaziyya citing al-Dhakhira: from the first. His statement: (Because he wants to remove it from his ownership) meaning because the possessor wants to remove the house from the plaintiff's ownership by his claim of purchase from the absent, hence the plaintiff has the right to rescind and take the house from the possessor, and it is explicit that this is one of the excuses for rescinding a lease.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on Kanz al-Daqa'iq in the chapter on mutual oaths (bāb al-taḥāluf). The author explains a key rule in claim litigation: the effect of a defendant's claim that the disputed item belongs to an absent third party (ghā'ib). The passage clarifies multiple scenarios and intra-Hanafi differences. It opens by presenting the ruling: if a plaintiff claims absolute ownership of an item and proves it, but the defendant (possessor) counters by stating he bought it from an absent person and provides evidence, the defendant's claim does not dismiss the lawsuit. The judge still rules based on the plaintiff's proof because the defendant, by admitting possession, has already made himself a litigant. The author then notes that this principle applies not only to purchase but to any claim of ownership (absolute or restricted, e.g., gift with possession). Even if the defendant claims a deposit (wadīʿah) from a third party, the lawsuit is not dismissed if the plaintiff proves ownership. However, if the defendant first confessed outside court that the item was his own, then in court claimed it was a deposit, his defense is accepted only if he proves the deposit—but if the plaintiff proves the earlier confession, the defendant becomes bound by it. The passage then discusses more complex cases where the defendant claims the item was rented, pledged, or lent from the absent person. In such cases, the judge rules for the lessee, pledgee, or borrower (i.e., the defendant's claimant), but the lawsuit against the defendant (possessor) is not dismissed. The defendant has options: surrender the item to the plaintiff and wait for the term to end, redeem the pledge, or void the sale. A difference arises between early and later Hanafi scholars regarding acceptance of such defenses. Early scholars allowed dismissal if proof was provided; later scholars held it invalid, while some said it is valid unless fraud is evident. The passage also addresses post-judgment defenses: if a defendant brings a defense after the judge has ruled, it may not be accepted if it contradicts the judgment, but exceptions exist if the defense proves that the judgment was invalid. Regarding the case where the defendant claims the plaintiff himself sold the item to the absent third party: there is an intra-Hanafi difference. According to some sources (Ishārāt al-Jāmiʿ and al-Ziyādāt), such evidence is not accepted; al-Nāṭifī in his Ajnās holds it is accepted and the claim is dismissed. Finally, the passage distinguishes between 'claim of absolute ownership' (daʿwā al-mulk al-muṭlaq) and 'claim of an act' (daʿwā al-fiʿl). In the latter, such as claiming usurpation (ghaṣb) with the active verb, the defendant's defense of deposit does not dismiss the claim. But if the passive verb is used (e.g., 'it was usurped from me'), Muhammad ibn al-Ḥasan al-Shaybānī considered it analogous to active, while Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf applied istiḥsān, treating it as a claim of an act because using the active verb spreads immorality. The correct view is that even with passive, the claim is not dismissed. The author concludes by noting that the possessor's claim of purchase from an absent person gives the plaintiff a right to rescind (faskh) any lease of the property, as it is an excuse for rescission.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-ghā'ib): An absent third party who is not present in court and not a party to the lawsuit.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person who physically possesses the disputed item; the defendant in a claim of ownership.
دعوى الملك المطلق (da‘wā al-mulk al-muṭlaq): A claim of absolute ownership without specifying a cause (e.g., purchase, inheritance).
دعوى الفعل (da‘wā al-fi‘l): A claim that the defendant committed an act against the plaintiff, such as usurpation (ghaṣb) or theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A plaintiff proves that he owns a house. The defendant (possessor) proves that he bought it from an absent third party. What is the ruling?
A) The defendant's evidence dismisses the plaintiff's claim; the house stays with the defendant.
B) The plaintiff's evidence prevails, and the judge rules for the plaintiff; the defendant's defense does not dismiss the lawsuit.
C) The judge dismisses both claims and waits for the absent party to appear.
D) The defendant must return the house to the plaintiff, but the plaintiff must compensate the defendant for his loss.

Key Takeaway
A plaintiff's proven ownership claim prevails over a defendant's defense of purchase from an absent party; the lawsuit is not dismissed.

Plaintiff's Recovery When Defendant Claims from Absent Third Party
Ruling on plaintiff's claim when defendant claims from absent party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a defendant claims possession from an absent third party via i'arah, ijara, rahn, or purchase, and explain the conditions for the plaintiff's recovery of the property.

Core Idea
Plaintiff's ownership overrides defendant's derivative possession, but defendant's right to rent or mortgage may require protection.

When a plaintiff proves ownership of a property, but the defendant claims he obtained it from an absent third party through a non-ownership transfer (loan, lease, mortgage, or unpaid purchase), the Hanafi school prioritizes the plaintiff's ownership. However, the defendant's rights must be safeguarded if rent was uncollected or if the property is mortgaged. This lesson dissects the ruling and its conditions from the text of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
The plaintiff proves ownership of a house. The defendant (person in possession) claims that the house came to him from an absent third party via i'arah, ijara, rahn, or purchase without payment of price.
2
State the general ruling
In all four scenarios, judgment is given to the plaintiff. The house is recovered by the plaintiff.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each contract
For i'arah: non-binding contract, plaintiff can revoke. For ijara: it is an excuse for cancellation because plaintiff wants to remove from his ownership. For purchase: plaintiff has right to recover for unpaid price. For rahn: treated as absolute ownership claim.
4
Identify conditions for recovery
If defendant had leased the property and not received rent, the judge takes a surety from the plaintiff for the defendant's person until the term ends. If rent was received or the claim is for mortgage, the property is not given to plaintiff but placed with a trustworthy person.
5
Apply the general principle
Claiming from the absent third party is like claiming absolute ownership; the defendant's possession does not defeat the plaintiff's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن عدم النقض فأدى البائع الدين وفك الرهن قبل قبضه ثم البيع، وإن كان المدعي برهن الدار له أعارها أو أجرها أو رهنها من الغائب أو اشتراها الغائب منه ولم ينقد الثمن قبل أن يشتريها منه ذو اليد يقضى بها للمدعي في الوجوه كلها. أما في الإعارة فلعدم اللزوم، وأما في الإجارة فلأنه عذر في الفسخ لأنه يريد إزالتها عن ملكه وأما في الشراء فلأن له حق الاسترداد لاستيفاء الثمن، فإن دفع الحاكم الدار إلى المدعي فإن كان أجرها ولم يقبض الأجرة أخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفس إلى انقضاء المدة، وإن كان قبض الأجرة أو كان ادعى رهناً لا يدفع إلى المدعي ويضعها على يد عدل ا هـ . وبه علم أن دعوى الرهن أو الإجارة أو الإعارة من الغائب كدعوى الملك المطلق على ذي اليد.
That the non-refutation, so the seller paid the debt and released the mortgage before taking possession then the sale, and if the plaintiff proved the house, that he loaned it or leased it or mortgaged it from the absent one, or the absent one bought it from him and did not pay the price before the person in possession bought it from him, judgment is given to the plaintiff in all these cases. As for loan: because it is non-binding. As for lease: because it is an excuse for cancellation, as he wants to remove it from his ownership. As for purchase: because he has the right of recovery for the price. Then if the judge gives the house to the plaintiff, if he had leased it and not received the rent, a surety is taken from him for the person until the end of the term. If he had received the rent, or if he claimed a mortgage, it is not given to the plaintiff but placed with a trustworthy person. And by this it is known that claiming mortgage, lease, or loan from the absent one is like claiming absolute ownership against the person in possession.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on a specific scenario in claims (da'wa). The scenario: a plaintiff proves ownership of a house, but the defendant (person in possession) claims that the house came to him through a third party (gha'ib) by way of loan (i'arah), lease (ijara), mortgage (rahn), or purchase (shira') without payment of price. The ruling: In all these cases, the judge gives the house to the plaintiff. The reasoning: For loan, it is non-binding; for lease, the defendant's claim of purchase from the gha'ib gives the plaintiff an excuse to cancel the lease because the defendant wants to remove the house from the plaintiff's ownership; for purchase, the plaintiff has the right to recover the house for the price. However, if the defendant had leased the house and not yet received rent, the judge takes a surety from the plaintiff for the person of the defendant until the lease term ends. If the defendant had already received rent, or if the claim is about mortgage (rahn), the house is not given to the plaintiff but placed with a trustworthy person (on trust). The author then generalizes: claiming mortgage, lease, or loan from the absent one is treated like claiming absolute ownership against the person in possession. This means the defendant's possession does not defeat the plaintiff's ownership; the plaintiff can recover.

Key Terms
إعارة (i'arah): Loan of property for use without compensation, non-binding per Hanafi fiqh.
إجارة (ijara): Lease, a binding contract but with grounds for termination if the lessor wants to sell the property.
رهن (rahn): Mortgage; the property is pledged as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the Hanafi ruling when a plaintiff proves ownership of a house and the defendant claims he leased it from an absent third party and has not received rent?
A) The judge gives the house to the plaintiff with no conditions.
B) The judge gives the house to the plaintiff but takes a surety from him for the defendant's person until the lease ends.
C) The judge leaves the house with the defendant until the lease ends.
D) The judge places the house with a trustworthy person until the absent party appears.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a plaintiff's proven ownership overrides a defendant's claim of possession from an absent third party via loan, lease, or mortgage, with protective conditions for the defendant's rights to rent or security.

Defense of Sale to a Third Party and Distinction in Act vs. Ownership Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale defense and distinction in act/ownership claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ikhtilaf on accepting a defendant's claim that the plaintiff sold the property to an absent third party, and distinguish between claims of a specific act and claims of absolute ownership, identifying the mufta bihi for each.

Core Idea
Whether a defendant's claim of prior sale repels a plaintiff's claim is disputed; the later view rejects it. Claims of act and ownership differ in how possession-based defenses operate.

This lesson examines two intertwined issues in Hanafi litigation: the effectiveness of a defendant's claim that the plaintiff sold the disputed property to a third party, and the critical distinction between a claim of a specific act (like theft or usurpation) and a claim of absolute ownership. Understanding these distinctions is essential for analysing procedural outcomes in property disputes. The passage also covers the passive/active voice nuance in theft and ghasb claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, the defendant claims the plaintiff sold the property to an absent third party. Second, the plaintiff may claim a specific act (theft/ghasb) or absolute ownership. Recognize which scenario is being discussed.
2
State the ikhtilaf on sale defense
According to 'Isharat al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat, the defense is not accepted. According to an-Natafi, it is accepted. Determine the practical consequence: if accepted, plaintiff's claim is dismissed; if not, case proceeds.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Distinguish act claim from ownership claim
In a claim of act, the defendant is contestant due to the act; possession-based defenses (e.g., deposit from a third party) do not repel. In a claim of absolute ownership, possession makes the defendant a contestant, and a proven deposit defense can repel.
Key: act claim = personal liability; ownership claim = possession-based.
4
Analyze passive/active voice nuance
For theft (sariqa), if the plaintiff uses passive voice (e.g., 'was stolen from me'), it is still an act claim by istihsan. For ghasb, passive voice is treated like active voice by analogy. Understand the difference and the ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
Mentioning the doer in theft spreads obscenity, so istihsan applies.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
For the sale defense, the later relied-upon view is that it is not accepted, following the majority and the mutun's absolute use of 'purchase'. For act vs. ownership, the rules are settled: act claim defenses are harder to prove; ownership claims allow possession-based defenses.
The author leans toward the view of the group of scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بدعوى الشراء من الغائب من غير أن يدعي أن المدعي باعها من الغائب، فلو ادعى ذو اليد أن المدعي باع العين من الغائب ففيه اختلاف قال في القنية : ادعى عليه عبداً وأثبته بالبينة فأقام المدعى عليه البينة أنك بعته من فلان الغائب فعلى ما عليه إشارات الجامع والزيادات لا تقبل وذكر الناطفي في أجناسه أنها تقبل وتندفع الدعوى، ثم إذا قبلت فإن لم يدع تلقي الملك من المشتري فأولى أن تقبل إذا ادعاه ا.هـ. وفيها قبله: ادعى عليه داراً أنها ملكه وأثبته بالبينة ثم أقام المدعي عليه بينة أن المدعي باعها من زوجته وباعتها هي مني تسمع وإذا لم تندفع في المسألة الأولى وأقام الخارج البينة فقضى له ثم جاء المقر له الغائب بدعوى الفعل عليه إلا بيده بخلاف دعوى الملك المطلق لأنه خصم فيه باعتبار يده حتى لا تصح دعواه على غير ذي اليد وتصح دعوى الفعل. وقد بنى فعل الغصب للفاعل وفعل السرقة للمفعول فخرج ما إذا بني الأول للمفعول وذكر الغصب والسرقة تمثيل والمراد دعوى فعل عليه، فلو قال المدعي أودعتك إياه أو اشتريته منك ذو اليد كما ذكرنا على وجه لا يفيد ملك الرقبة له لا تندفع؛ كذا في البزازية. ولو قال المدعي ملكي وفي يده بغير حق أحكامه فصار كالعدم فكان كدعوى ملك مطلق حتى لو لم يشهدوا على قبضه لم تندفع ولم يذكر في الخلاصة نقد الثمن وإنما ذكر الشراء مع القبض وفي البزازية بعد ما ذكر القبض كدعوى ملك مطلق قال : وجماعة من مشايخنا قالوا: لا تندفع أيضاً لأن دعوى مع الشراء بقي معتبراً ولهذا لا يحكم القاضي بالزوائد المنفصلة ولا يكون للباعة أن يرجع بعضهم على بعض ولو كان كدعوى الملك المطلق لكان الأمر بخلافه ا هـ . والظاهر ما عليه هؤلاء لإطلاق المتون الشراء. وأفاد المؤلف رحمه الله بما ذكره من دعوى الفعل ودفعها أن المدعى عليه بعد دعوى الفعل عليه لا يقدر على التحويل إلى غيره فلو دفع بأنه لابنه الصغير بعد دعوى الغصب عليه لم تندفع كونه أحق بإمساكها، ولو صدقه ذو اليد في شرائه منه لا يأمره القاضي بالتسليم لا يكون قضاء على الغائب بإقراره وهي عجيبة . قيد بتلقي اليد من الغائب للاحتراز عما إذا قال ذو اليد أودعينه وكيل فلان ذلك لم تندفع إلا ببينة لأنه لم يثبت تلقي اليد ممن إليه حتى قوله : (وهي عجيبة أقول : تقدمت المسألة متناً في آخر كتاب الوكالة قبل باب عزل الوكيل ووجهها أنه إقرار على الغير وهو رب الوديعة فلا يسلمها إلى مدعي الوكالة بالقبض أو الشراء بخلاف ما لو كان مديون الغائب وادعى عليه شخص الوكالة بالقبض وصدقه فإنه يدفع إليه لأن الديون
And restricted to claiming purchase from the absent one without him claiming that the plaintiff sold it to the absent one. So if the possessor claims that the plaintiff sold the object to the absent one, there is difference. Al-Qunyah said: He claimed a slave against him and proved it by evidence, then the defendant brought evidence that you sold him to so-and-so who is absent. According to the indications of al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat, it is not accepted. An-Natafi in his Ajnas mentioned that it is accepted and the claim is repelled. Then if accepted, and he does not claim receipt of ownership from the buyer, then it is more appropriate that it be accepted if he claims it. And earlier in it: He claimed a house that it was his property and proved it by evidence, then the defendant brought evidence that the plaintiff sold it to his wife and she sold it to me; it is heard. And if it is not repelled in the first issue, and the outsider brought evidence and judgment was given for him, then the acknowledged one (the absent person) came and proved, his evidence is accepted because the absent one is not a party to the judgment; judgment was only against the possessor. That is mentioned by the commentator. The summary of the second: the plaintiff claimed an act against the possessor, so he defends by deposit from the absent one and proves; then it is not repelled because he became a contestant only by the claim of an act against him, unlike the claim of absolute ownership because he is a contestant by virtue of his possession, so his claim is not valid against someone else, but the claim of an act is valid. And the passive voice of usurpation (ghasb) and the passive voice of theft (sariqa) were used, so the case where the first is in passive voice is excluded, such as saying 'was usurped from me' as in al-Bazzaziyya. The restriction in theft to passive voice is to know the ruling when he uses the active voice, which is agreed upon; in the passive voice there is difference. Muhammad said it is like the active voice of usurpation, which is analogy. We (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) used istihsan and made it a claim of an act against him because mentioning the doer spreads obscenity, unlike usurpation. If he claims it by a masdar, the commentators did not mention it. And in al-Bazzaziyya: He claimed that it is his property and in his hand as usurpation, and the possessor proved deposit; it is said to be repelled because there is no claim of an act against him; the correct view is that it is not repelled. By 'burhan' he means establishing evidence, so confession is excluded. That is why in al-Bazzaziyya, quoting al-Dhakhira: The plaintiff said 'he stole from me' and the possessor said 'so-and-so deposited it with me' and proved it; it is not repelled. And if the plaintiff said 'I bought it from so-and-so' and the possessor said 'so-and-so deposited it with me', the case is dropped without proof. And he became a contestant for the claim of an act against him. If he proves the plaintiff's confession of deposit from the absent one, it is repelled; if not, it is not repelled by establishing evidence of deposit because the plaintiff's confession establishes that his hand is not a hand of contestation. Usurpation and theft are examples; the intent is claim of an act against him. So if the plaintiff says 'I deposited it with you' or 'I bought it from you', as we mentioned in a manner that does not give him ownership of the corpus, it is not repelled; such is in al-Bazzaziyya. If the plaintiff says 'it is my property and in his hand without right' that is not a claim of usurpation, so it is repelled if he proves deposit in the mentioned manner; also in al-Bazzaziyya. And the restriction to 'claim of an act against the possessor' is to avoid claims against others: if the possessor defends with one of the mentioned things and proves, it is repelled like a claim of absolute ownership, as in al-Bazzaziyya. The commentator mentioned: If he claimed that he bought it from the possessor, took possession, and paid the price, and the possessor brought evidence that so-and-so deposited it with him, the case is dropped. But if he claimed an act against the possessor, because the plaintiff's contract has fulfilled its rulings, it becomes like non-existent, so it is like a claim of absolute ownership. Even if they did not testify to his possession, it is not repelled. Al-Khulasa did not mention payment of price; it only mentioned purchase with possession. In al-Bazzaziyya after mentioning possession, he said it is like a claim of absolute ownership. A group of our scholars said it is not repelled either, because the claim with purchase remains valid; that is why the judge does not rule for separate accretions, and the sellers cannot refer to each other. If it were like a claim of absolute ownership, it would be otherwise. The apparent view is that of these scholars because the texts (mutun) use 'purchase' absolutely. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) by mentioning the claim of an act and its defense indicates that the defendant after a claim of an act against him cannot transfer it to someone else. So if he defends that it is for his young son after a claim of usurpation against him, it is not repelled as in al-Bazzaziyya, or if he defends that it is his father's property deposited with him, as in al-Khaniyya. He said: (And if the plaintiff said 'I bought it from so-and-so' and the possessor said 'so-and-so deposited it with me', the case is dropped.) That is without proof. Its summary: the plaintiff claimed ownership through the absent one, so the possessor replied that his hand is from the absent one. They agree that the origin of ownership is for the absent one, so its arrival to the hand of the possessor is from him, so his hand is not a hand of contestation unless the plaintiff brings evidence that so-and-so authorized him to take possession because he proves by his evidence that he is more entitled to hold it. Even if the possessor affirms his purchase from him, the judge does not order delivery; it is not judgment against the absent one by his confession, and it is odd ('ajiba). The restriction to receiving the hand from the absent one is to avoid the case where the possessor says 'so-and-so's agent deposited it with me'; that is not repelled without evidence because he did not prove receipt of the hand from whom he claims. Until he said: (It is odd) I say: The case preceded in the text in the last chapters of Wakalah before the chapter on removal of the agent. Its aspect: it is a confession concerning another, the owner of the deposit, so he should not hand it over to the claimant of agency for receipt or purchase, unlike if the absent one was indebted and a person claimed agency for receipt and he affirmed, then he would hand over to him because debts...
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion from the Hanafi school on the defense of the defendant in a claim (da'wa). It covers several sub-issues: 1) When the plaintiff proves ownership and the defendant claims that the plaintiff sold the property to a third party (gha'ib). There is a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) among Hanafi scholars: according to the 'Isharat al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat, such a defense is not accepted; according to an-Natafi in his Ajnas, it is accepted and the plaintiff's claim is repelled. 2) The distinction between a claim of an act (like theft or usurpation) against the defendant and a claim of absolute ownership. If the plaintiff claims a specific act committed by the defendant, then the defendant's defense that he received the property from a third party (deposit) does not repel the claim, because the plaintiff is a contestant by virtue of the act. In contrast, if the plaintiff claims absolute ownership, the defendant's possession makes him a contestant, and the defense of deposit can repel the claim if proven. 3) The use of passive vs. active voice in claims: if the plaintiff says 'this was usurped from me' (passive), it is a claim of an act against the defendant? There is ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Imam Muhammad says it is like active voice (usurpation), which is analogy; the two use istihsan and consider it a claim of an act because mentioning the doer in theft spreads obscenity. 4) Specific cases: if the plaintiff says 'I deposited it with you' or 'I bought it from you', the defense of deposit from a third party does not repel because the plaintiff's own statement shows a relationship with the defendant. But if the plaintiff says 'it is my property and in his hand without right', it is not a claim of usurpation, so a deposit defense can repel. 5) A notable case: if the plaintiff claims purchase from a specific person (not the defendant), and the defendant claims deposit from that same person, the case is dropped without need for proof because both acknowledge the property belongs to that third party. The author notes this is 'odd' ('ajiba) because it is a confession concerning the absent third party. He relates that this case was mentioned earlier in the chapter on agency (wakalah). The underlying reason: the defendant's confession of deposit is a confession about the owner's right, so the judge cannot order delivery to the plaintiff based on the defendant's confession alone.

Key Terms
دعوى فعل (da'wa fi'l): A claim that the defendant committed a specific act against the plaintiff, such as theft or usurpation.
دعوى ملك مطلق (da'wa mulk mutlaq): A claim of absolute ownership of property without alleging a specific wrongful act by the defendant.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation, unlawful taking of property without violence.
سرقة (sariqa): Theft, taking property secretly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi school, if a plaintiff claims ownership of property and the defendant proves that the plaintiff sold it to a third party, what is the ruling?
A) The claim is repelled.
B) The claim is not repelled.
C) It is repelled only if the third party confirms the sale.
D) The judge must summon the third party.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a defendant's claim of prior sale to a third party does not repel a plaintiff's ownership claim, and claims of act and ownership have different rules for possession-based defenses.
