Two External Claimants: Division by Evidence
Ruling with proof for two external claimants with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two external claimants both present evidence of ownership, and explain the proof from the hadith and the istihsan rationale.

Core Idea
Two equal external claimants both with evidence: property is divided half each, not left to lottery.

When two people who do not possess a property both bring valid evidence claiming ownership, the Hanafi school does not resort to lottery or prefer one witness over the other. Instead, the property is divided equally. This ruling, grounded in a Prophetic precedent and supported by istihsan (juridical preference), ensures that both testimonies are honoured as much as possible. The passage from Al-Hidayah explains the rule, its evidence, and its application to related cases like waqf and specific causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Two external claimants (non-possessors) both present evidence on property held by a third party. The ruling: the property is divided equally between them (half each).
External claimant (kharj) means the claimant does not physically possess the property.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Tamim bin Tarafa: two men disputed over a she-camel, each produced evidence, and the Prophet (ﷺ) judged it to be between them half each. This is a Sunnah ahad (single-chain narration).
The hadith is reported in Sunan al-Tirmidhi and other collections.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action of physically dividing the she-camel indicates that the ruling is equal division. The imperative implication (amr) is understood from his practice. The Hanafi usul principle al-amr yufidu al-wujub applies here to show that acting on both evidences is obligatory when possible.
The property must be divisible; if not, it would be sold and proceeds divided.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text mentions that the lottery (qur'a) was used initially but was abrogated. Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may still allow lottery or other methods when evidence is equal. The Hanafi position relies on istihsan over strict analogy (qiyas).
Istihsan permits departing from qiyas because the hadith provides a stronger reason.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The established (mufta bihi) position is division. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah presents this as the relied-upon view. The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah (the 6 core books).
Abrogation of the lottery hadith is a key factor in preferring division.

كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
باب دعوى الرجلين
برهنا على ما في يد واحد آخر قضى لهما لحديث تميم بن طرفة أن رجلين اختصما إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في ناقة أقام كل واحد منهما البينة فقضى بها بينهما نصفين. وحديث القرعة كان في الابتداء ثم نسخ، ولأن المطلق للشهادة في حق كل واحد منهما يحتمل الوجوه بأن يعتمد أحدهما الملك والآخر اليد فصحت الشهادتان فيجب العمل بهما ما أمكن وقد أمكن بالتنصيف إذ المحل يقبله، وإنما ينصف لاستوائهما في سبب الاستحقاق والضمير في قوله «برهنا» عائد على الرجلين أي الخارجين بقرينة على ما في يد أخر، والمعنى على ملك ما في يد الآخر ملكاً فالكلام في دعوى الخارجين الملك المطلق فخرج ما إذا ادعيا بسبب معين أو مقيد بتاريخ هذا القبيل ما في منية المفتي: أقاما بينة على عبد في يد رجل أحدهما بغصب وسيأتي. ومن والآخر بوديعة فهو بينهما ا هـ . وأطلقهما فشمل ما إذا ادعيا الوقف في يد ثالث فيقضى بالعقار نصفين لكل وقف النصف وهو من قبيل دعوى الملك المطلق باعتبار ملك الواقف ولهذا قال في القنية : دار في يد رجل أقام رجل عليه بينة أنها وقفت عليه وأقام تيم المسجد بينة أنها وقف المسجد، فإن أرخا فهي للسابق منهما، وإن لم يؤرخا فهي بينهما نصفان ا هـ . ذو اليد الملك فيها أو الوقف على جهة أخرى. والحاصل أن أن ذلك ولا فرق في بین يدعي فدفعه دعوى الوقف من قبيل دعوى الملك المطلق ولهذا لو ادعى وقفية ما في يد آخر وبرهن
ذو اليد بأنه مودع فلان ونحوه وبرهن فإنها تندفع خصومة المدعي كما في الإسعاف، فدعوى الوقف داخل في المسألة المخمسة، وكما يقسم الدار بين الواقفين كذلك لو برهن كل على أن الواقف جعل له الغلة ولا مرجح فإنها تكون بينهما نصفين لما في الاسعاف من باب إقرار الصحيح بأرض في يده أنها وقف لو شهد اثنان على إقرار رجل بأن أرضه وقف على زيد ونسله وشهد آخران على إقراره بأنها وقف على عمرو ونسله تكون وقفاً على الأسبق وقتاً إن علم، وإن لم يعلم أو ذكروا وقتاً واحداً تكون الغلة الفريقين إنصافاً، مات من ولد زيد فنصيبه لمن بقي منهم وكذلك حكم أولاد عمرو، وإذا انقرض أحد الفريقين رجعت إلى الفريق الباقي لزوال المزاحم وقيد بالبرهان منهما إذ لو برهن أحدهما فقط فإنه يقضى له اهـ.

ومن
قوله: (ولآخر بوديعة فهو بينهما) أي لأن المودع بالجحود يصير غاصباً ثم إن ما ذكره عن المنية
Chapter of the two men's claim. [The two men] presented evidence on what is in the hand of another person, then judgment was given for both of them. Due to the hadith of Tamim bin Tarafa that two men disputed before the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, concerning a she-camel; each one of them produced evidence, so he judged it to be between them half each. And the hadith of the lottery was at the beginning then abrogated. And because the absolute [term] for testimony in respect of each one of them admits of possibilities: that one relied on ownership and the other on possession, so both testimonies are valid, and it is obligatory to act upon both as much as possible, and it is possible by half-splitting since the property accepts it. It is only split because they are equal in the cause of entitlement. The pronoun in his statement 'they presented evidence' refers to the two men, i.e., the external claimants, by context, concerning what is in the hand of another. The meaning is: concerning the ownership of what is in the hand of another as ownership. So the discussion is about the external claimants' claim of absolute ownership, thus excluded is when they claim by a specific cause or restricted by a date. Of this type is what is in Munyat al-Mufti: Two men presented evidence over a slave in the hand of a man, one claiming by usurpation and the other by deposit; then it is between them. End. And he [the author] left them unrestricted, so it includes when they claim a waqf in the hand of a third person; then judgment is given for the property half each, for each waqf half. This is of the type of absolute ownership claim, considering the ownership of the waqif. For this, he said in al-Qunyah: A house in the hand of a man; a man presented evidence that it is a waqf upon him, and the mosque committee presented evidence that it is a waqf of the mosque; if they dated, it is for the earlier of them; if they did not date, it is between them half each. End. The possessor [claims] ownership or the waqf for another purpose. The result is that there is no difference between the claim of waqf being of the type of absolute ownership claim, and for this, if one claims waqfiyyah of what is in the hand of another and presents evidence, while the possessor [claims] that it is a deposit of so-and-so and presents evidence, then the claimant's suit is repelled, as in al-Is'af. So the claim of waqf is included in the five-fold issue. And just as the house is divided between the two waqifs, likewise if each presents evidence that the waqif assigned the produce to him and there is no preponderance, then it is between them half each, because in al-Is'af from the chapter of a healthy person's acknowledgment of land in his hand that it is a waqf: if two witnesses testify to a man's acknowledgment that his land is a waqf for Zayd and his descendants, and two others testify to his acknowledgment that it is a waqf for 'Amr and his descendants, then it is a waqf for the earlier in time if known; if not known or they stated one time, then the produce is between the two groups equally. When a child of Zayd dies, his share goes to those remaining of them; likewise the ruling for 'Amr's children. When one group becomes extinct, it reverts to the remaining group due to the removal of the competitor. It is restricted to both presenting evidence; if only one presents evidence, then judgment is given for him. End. Between the chapter of the two men's claim, and from his statement: (and the other by deposit, it is between them), meaning because the depositor by denial becomes a usurper, then what he mentioned from al-Munya...
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of the claim of two men. The author (al-Marghinani) states the rule: if two external claimants both bring evidence (bayyinah) on property in the hand of a third person, the judge rules that the property is divided equally between them. The proof is the hadith of Tamim bin Tarafa, in which two men disputed over a she-camel before the Prophet ﷺ, each produced evidence, and the Prophet ﷺ divided it between them. The author notes that the lottery (qur'a) was initially used but later abrogated. The rational reasoning (istihsan) is that each claimant's testimony is valid—one may have ownership, the other possession—so both must be honored, and half-splitting is possible since the property is divisible. The ruling applies only to claims of absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq), not specific causes like usurpation or deposit with a date. However, if both claim specific causes, such as one claiming usurpation and the other deposit, the property is also divided. The same applies to waqf claims: if two claim waqf on the same property without priority, it is divided. The passage further discusses the case of waqf on a specific person versus a general waqf, and details the distribution of produce when multiple beneficiaries claim. The key point is that the external claimant is one who does not hold the property; the possessor (dhu al-yad) is the one in physical control. The ruling encourages acting upon both evidences as much as possible.

Key Terms
الخارج (al-kharj): The external claimant who does not possess the property in dispute.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The possessor who has physical control over the property.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females in property cases.
الإستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference that allows departing from a strict analogy for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The property is given to the one with the stronger evidence.
B) The property is divided equally between them.
C) The judge uses lottery to determine the owner.
D) The property remains with the possessor.

Key Takeaway
When two external claimants provide equal evidence, the Hanafi school divides the property equally, relying on the Sunnah and istihsan.

The Exhaustive Classification of Claims: A Hanafi Methodological Refinement
Presents and critiques a comprehensive classification of scenarios in claims between two men

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the original classification of claim scenarios and apply the commentator's method to produce a more exhaustive enumeration, demonstrating the Hanafi commitment to comprehensive legal classification.

Core Idea
Exhaustive classification (taqsim) is essential in fiqh to ensure no legal scenario is overlooked; a valid classification must account for all relevant variables.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a classification from al-'Umadiyyah regarding claims between two men when both present evidence. He finds it incomplete and proposes a more detailed taxonomy based on the nature of the claim (ownership, cause), the evidence scenario, and possession. This critical refinement illustrates the Hanafi method of meticulous analysis to cover all possible cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original classification
List the three variables used by al-'Umadiyyah: type of claim (absolute ownership, inheritance, purchase), location of possession (third party, both, or one claimant), and dating of evidence (no date, same date, one earlier, one only). Calculate the total scenarios: 3 × 3 × 4 = 36.
2
Identify the commentator's objection
The commentator states the classification is 'not exhaustive'. It misses distinctions like whether the cause of ownership is uniform, repeatable, or stronger. These gaps could lead to unaddressed legal scenarios.
3
Map the commentator's new classification
The commentator proposes nine categories of claim based on: absolute ownership vs. ownership by cause, whether the cause is repeatable or not, whether the causes are equal or different in strength, and whether the claimants claim the same or different types. Then combine with four evidence scenarios (both prove, one proves, none proves without preponderance, none proves with preponderance), yielding 9 × 4 = 32 scenarios. Possession location adds another variable (the text says 'four aspects' but likely three: third hand, both hands, one hand—though the total seems inconsistent).
Al-Ramli advises reflecting on this classification to see its merits.
4
Evaluate the completeness
Compare the two classifications. The new one accounts for cause-related variables, making it more exhaustive. The original only considered claim type at a superficial level. The Hanafi method demands such thoroughness to avoid gaps in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه قال في العمادية : اعلم أن الرجلين إذا ادعيا عيناً وبرهنا فلا يخلو إما أن يدعيا ملكاً مطلقاً أو إرثاً أو شراء، وكل قسم على ثلاثة إما أن يكون المدعي في يد ثالث أو في يدهما أو في يد أحدهما، وكل وجه على أربعة أقسام لأنه إما أن لا يؤرخا أو أرخا تاريخاً واحداً أو أرخا وتاريخ أحدهما أسبق أو أرخ أحدهما لا الآخر وجملة ذلك ستة وثلاثون أقول : إن هذا التقسيم ليس بحاصر والصواب أن يقال : إذا ادعيا عيناً فإما أن يدعيا ملكاً مطلقاً أو ملكاً بسبب متحد قابل للتكرار أو غير قابل له، أو مختلف أحدهما أقوى من الآخر، أو مستويان من واحد أو من متعدد، أو يدعي أحدهما الملك المطلق والآخر الملك بسبب، أو أحدهما ما يتكرر والآخر ما لا يتكرر فهي تسعة. وكل منهما إما أن يبرهن أو يبرهن أحدهما فقط، أو لا برهان لواحد منهما ولا مرجح أو لأحدهما مرجح فهي أربعة صارت اثنين وثلاثين. وكل منها إما أن يكون المدعي في يد ثالث أو في يدهما أو في يد أحدهما فهي أربعة فصلاً ا هـ. سيذكره المصنف في هذا الباب قوله : (ثم بعده إذا أقام صاحب اليد البينة أنه ملكه لا يقبل) انظر ما كتبناه عند قوله وقضى له إن نكل مرة . قوله : (أقول أن هذا التقسيم ليس بحاصر والصواب أن يقال الخ) قال الرملي: تأمل في هذا التقسيم يظهر لك ما فيه .
Then know that he said in al-'Umadiyyah: Know that when two men claim a specific item and present evidence, it is not free from either they claim absolute ownership, or inheritance, or purchase. And each division has three: either the claimant is in the hand of a third, or in their hands, or in the hand of one of them. And each aspect has four divisions because either they do not date, or they date with one date, or they date and the date of one is earlier, or one dates and the other does not. The total of that is thirty-six. I say: This classification is not exhaustive. The correct is to say: When they claim a specific item, then either they claim absolute ownership, or ownership by a uniform cause that is repeatable or not repeatable, or different, one stronger than the other, or equal from one or from multiple, or one claims absolute ownership and the other claims ownership by cause, or one claims what is repeatable and the other what is not. So these are nine. And each of them either they both present evidence, or only one presents evidence, or neither presents evidence and there is no preponderance, or one has a preponderance. So these are four, making thirty-two. And each of them either the claimant is in the hand of a third, or in their hands, or in the hand of one of them. So these are four aspects. End. He [the author] will mention it in this chapter, his statement: (Then afterwards, if the possessor presents evidence that it is his ownership, it is not accepted.) Look at what we wrote at his statement: 'and judgment is given for him if he refuses once.' His statement: (I say: This classification is not exhaustive, and the correct is to say...) al-Ramli said: Reflect on this classification, and what is in it will appear to you.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a classification from al-'Umadiyyah regarding the various scenarios in a claim between two men when both present evidence. The original author (al-'Umadiyyah) divided cases based on the type of claim (absolute ownership, inheritance, purchase), the location of possession (third party, both, or one of them), and whether the evidence is dated. This yields 36 scenarios. The commentator (Ibn al-Humam or the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) objects that this classification is not exhaustive. He proposes a more detailed classification based on whether the claim is absolute or by a cause, whether the cause is repeatable, whether the causes are equal or different, etc., totaling nine categories. Combined with whether both or only one present evidence, and with or without preponderance (four), that yields 32 scenarios. Multiplying by three possession locations gives 96, but he only says 'four aspects'—perhaps he means the same three locations but that's four? He mentions 'four aspects' possibly meaning four: third hand, both hands, one hand, and the case where the claimant is also the possessor? The text is not fully clear. Al-Ramli advises reflecting on this classification to see its merits. The point is that the commentator aims to provide a more systematic and complete enumeration of possible cases in this chapter. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of detailed legal classification (taqsim) to ensure no scenario is overlooked.

Key Terms
التقسيم (al-taqsim): The classification of legal scenarios based on different variables (type of claim, evidence, possession).
المرجح (al-murajjih): A factor that makes one party's evidence preponderate over the other's, such as an earlier date or a stronger cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's correction, how many basic categories of claims do two men have when claiming a specific item, before considering evidence and possession?
A) Three: absolute ownership, inheritance, purchase
B) Nine: based on whether the claim is absolute or by cause, repeatable or not, etc.
C) Six: based on possession location
D) Three: based on evidence

Key Takeaway
A comprehensive legal classification must consider all possible variables of claim, cause, evidence, and possession to avoid gaps in jurisprudence.

Two Outsiders Proving Purchase: Option to Take Half
Ruling when two outsiders each prove purchase from the current possessor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two outsiders each prove purchase from a possessor and explain the option each has to take half at half the price.

Core Idea
Equal proofs of purchase split the item; each outsider may take half at half the price or leave it.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in property disputes: two outsiders each provide credible evidence that they bought the same item from its current possessor. Hanafi fiqh treats their proofs equally and gives each a right to choose. Understanding this ruling helps you grasp how the law balances conflicting claims without forcing one party to lose unfairly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the three roles: the current possessor (dhu al-yad) and two outsiders (kharijan) who each claim to have purchased the item from the possessor.
The outsiders are not the possessor themselves; they are third-party claimants.
2
Recognize the evidence strength
Both outsiders present equally strong proof (bayyina) that they bought the item. Because neither proof outweighs the other, the judge treats them as equal.
This is a case of bayyinatan mutakifatan (conflicting equal proofs).
3
Understand the judge's initial ruling
The judge splits the item between the two outsiders in halves. This is due to equality of the cause (sabab) of their claims.
The possessor is set aside because his claim is defeated by both outsiders' proofs.
4
Apply the option
Each outsider has the right (khiyar) to either accept his half by paying half the price (badal) he originally paid, or to abandon his claim entirely.
If he wishes to take the half, he must compensate with half the price.
5
Compare with the fuduli analogy
The author compares this to two unauthorized agents (fuduliyyan) each selling the same item, after the owner permits both sales. In both cases, the buyer gets a choice because part of the bargain has changed.
The analogy reinforces that the option arises from the conflict of sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعلى الشراء منه لكل نصفه ببدله إن شاء) أي لو برهن الخارجان على الشراء من ذي اليد خير كل منهما إن شاء أخذ النصف بنصف الثمن، وإن شاء ترك لأن القاضي يقضي به بينهما نصفين لاستوائهما في السبب فصار كفضوليين باع كل منهما من رجل وأجاز المالك البيعين فإن كلاً منهما يخير لأنه تغير عليه شطر عقده فلعل رغبته في تملك الكل . أشار المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى إلى أن الخارجين لو برهن كل منهما على ذي اليد أنه أودعه الذي في يده فإنه يقضي به بينهما نصفين ثم إذا أقام أحدهما البينة على صاحبه أنه عبده لم تسمع، ولو أقام أحدهما البينة على دعواه ولم يقم الآخر وأقام شاهداً واحداً أو شاهدين لم يزكيا فقضى بالعبد لصاحب البينة ثم أقام الآخر بينة عادلة على أنه عبده أودعه
His saying: (And regarding purchase from him [the possessor], each gets half at its price if he wishes) – i.e., if the two outsiders prove purchase from the possessor, each is given a choice: if he wishes, he takes half at half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Because the judge adjudges the item between them in halves due to equality in the cause (sabab). So it becomes like two unauthorized agents (fuduliyyan) each sold to a man and the owner permitted both sales – then each is given a choice because part of his contract has changed, and perhaps his desire is to own the whole. The author indicated that if the two outsiders each prove that the possessor deposited the item with him (each claims it is his deposit), then the judge adjudges it between them in halves. Then if one of them establishes evidence against the other that the slave is his, it is not heard. If one of them establishes evidence for his claim and the other does not, but brings one witness or two who are not verified, and the judge adjudges the slave to the one with evidence, then the other brings just evidence that it is his slave deposited (with the possessor) – [the ruling is].
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario in claims (da'wa) where two outsiders each present evidence that they purchased the same item (e.g., a slave) from the current possessor (dhu al-yad). Since both proofs are equally strong, the judge splits the item between them equally, and each has the option to accept half at half the price they paid. If they do not wish, they can abandon. The reasoning is analogous to two unauthorized agents (fuduliyyan) independently selling the same item: upon the owner's permission, both sales are effective, and the buyer has a choice. The author also contrasts this with a case where both claim the possessor deposited the item with him: then it is split equally too, but if later one proves ownership against the other, that evidence is not heard because the judgment has already been passed. Another nuance: if one outsider brings complete evidence and the other brings one witness or unverified testimony, the judge rules for the first, but if the latter later brings just evidence, the prior judgment stands? The text seems incomplete but indicates the later evidence is considered? Actually, the passage ends abruptly; it likely says that the later evidence is not heard because the first evidence was decisive. The commentator's point is that the ruling is final once based on complete evidence.

Key Terms
خارجان (kharijan): Two persons who each claim to have purchased the item from the current possessor, and are not the possessor themselves.
ذي اليد (dhu al-yad): The current possessor of the disputed item, who is presumed to own it unless evidence suggests otherwise.
بدل (badal): The price or substitute paid for the half share; here it is half of the original purchase price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two outsiders each prove purchase from the possessor, what is the option given to each?
A) They must accept half of the item.
B) They may take half at half the price they paid.
C) They may demand a full refund from the possessor.
D) They may sue the possessor for fraud.

Key Takeaway
When two outsiders equally prove purchase from the possessor, each has the option to take half at half the price, reflecting the Hanafi principle that equal proofs lead to equal shares with a right of choice.

The Ruling When Two Claimants Prove Purchase from Different Sellers: Date Priority vs. Equality
Commentary on rulings for two claimants each proving purchase in the chapter of claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two outsiders each prove purchase from different sellers, identify the disagreement between the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah and Imam Muḥammad, and apply the preferred position to a concrete scenario.

Core Idea
The earlier purchase date gives priority only under Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah; Imam Muḥammad splits equally because each claim traces to separate sellers.

This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq examines a classic procedural dilemma: two outsiders each present evidence that they purchased the same property from two different sellers. When one has an earlier purchase date, should that decide the case? The Hanafi school contains a nuanced internal disagreement, with the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah (favored by Abu Ḥanīfah) prioritizing the earlier date, while Imam Muḥammad disregards dates and divides equally. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the school weighs explicit evidence against inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that we have two outsiders (not the possessor) each claiming purchase from different sellers, with one having an earlier documented date.
The sellers are distinct; the property is in the hand of a third party.
2
State the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position
According to the apparent narration (attributed to Abu Ḥanīfah), the earlier date gives priority—that claimant takes the entire property.
This is the muftā bihī (operative) position according to the author.
3
State Imam Muḥammad’s position
Imam Muḥammad holds that both claimants are equal; the property is split equally regardless of dates.
His reasoning: each proves ownership for his own seller, so it resembles two separate claims without date.
4
Examine the proofs
Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah: the earlier date establishes ownership at a time when no one else disputes it—explicit evidence outweighs inference. Imam Muḥammad: without dating the sellers’ ownership, the purchase dates are irrelevant to the current dispute.
The author sides with Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, noting it is the stronger proof.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, a judge following the muftā bihī ruling awards the property to the earlier-dated claimant. If both have no dates or equal dates, they share equally.
Possession (qabḍ) can also affect priority, but date is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين الذي في يده أو لم يذكروا ذلك فإنه يقضى به للثاني على المقضى له وتمامه في خزانة الأكمل. ويستفاد منه أحكام مسألة الكتاب فيما إذا أقام أحدهما بينة على الشراء وقضى له ثم أقام الآخر فإنه يقضى له على المقضى له بخلاف ما إذا برهنا وقضى بالتنصيف فبرهن أحدهما لم تسمع. وقيد بكون كل منهما مدعياً للشراء فقط للاحتراز عما إذا ادعى أحدهما شراء وعتيقاً والآخر شراء فقط فإن مدعي العتق أولى فإن العتق بمنزلة القبض؛ كذا في خزانة الأكمل. وقيد بقوله «منه لأنهما لو ادعيا الشراء من غير ذي اليد فسيأتي وقوله «ببدله» أي بنصف الثمن الذي عينه فإن ادعى أحدهما أنه اشتراه بمائة والآخر بمائتين أخذ الأول نصفه بخمسين والآخر نصفه بمائة . ولم يذكر المؤلف رحمه الله أن الثمن منقود أولاً لأنه لا فرق لكن إن برهن كل منهما على الشراء والنقد استرد نصف ما دفعه كما في خزانة الأكمل. وظاهر إطلاقه أنه لا اعتبار بتصديق ذي اليد أحدهما. وفي العمادية وإقرار صاحب اليد لأحدهما يعتبر لأنه شهادة على قوله. وفي فوائد جدي شيخ الإسلام برهان الدين : إذا شهد البائع بالملك لمشتريه والعين في يد غيره بأن قال هذه العين ملكه لأني بعته منه أو قال كان ملكاً لي وبعته منه فإن كان المدعي في دعواه ادعى الشراء منه لا تقبل لأنه شهادة على قول نفسه ا هـ . وأفاد بإشارة كلامه مسألة التنازع في الميراث فلو ادعى كل من خارجين الميراث عن أبيه لا : هو الحق فلما شهدا قال للقاضي سل عنهما فإنهما شهدا علي بباطل وما كنت أظنهما يشهدان لم يلزمه وسأل عنهما لأنه إقرار معلق بالحظر فلا يصح قوله : ( وقيد بدعوى الشراء من واحد الخ) قال في نور العين قاضيخان خارجان ادعيا شراء من اثنين يقضي بينهما نصفين وإن أرخا وأحدهما أسبق فهو أحق في ظاهر الرواية، وعند محمد لا يعتبر التاريخ يعني يقضي بينهما، وإن أرخ أحدهما فقط يقضي بينهما نصفين وفاقاً فلو لأحدهما يد فالخارج أولى؛ خلاصة إلا إذا سبق تاريخ ذي اليد؛ هداية برهن خارجان على شراء شيء من اثنين وأرخا فهما سواء لأنهما يثبتان الملك لبائعهما فيصير كأنهما حضر أو ادعيا ثم يخير كل منهما كما مر يعني في مسألة دعوى الخارجين شراء من ذي اليد (كفا): لو برهنا على شراء من اثنين وتاريخ أحدهما أسبق اختلف روايات الكتب فما في الهداية يشير إلى أنه لا عبرة لسبق التاريخ بل يقضى بينهما. وفي (بس) ما يدل صريحاً أن الأسبق أولى. يقول ما الحقير : ويؤيده مر عن قاضيخان أنه ظاهر الرواية، فما في الهداية اختيار قول محمد اهـ. ثم نقل بعده عن صاحب جامع الفصولين ترجيح ما في الهداية ورده بأن دليل ما في المبسوط وقاضيخان أن الأسبق تاريخاً يضيف الملك إلى نفسه في زمان لا ينازعه فيه غيره أقوى من دليل من ذهب إلا أنه لا يعتبر سبق التاريخ وهو قولهم لأنهما يثبتان الملك لبائعهما فكأنهما حضرا وادعيا الملك بلا تاريخ. ووجه قوة الأول غير خاف على من تأمل. ويرجحه أنه ظاهر الرواية ثم قال : ولم أر ما لو ادعى ذوا يدين شراء من اثنين في الكتب صريحاً غير أن صاحب الوجيز قال بعد ذكر مسائل دعوى القضاء لم يأخذ الآخر كله وإن أرخا فللسابق وإلا فلذي القبض والشراء أحق من الهبة وبرهن قضى بها بينهما ولذا قال في خزانة الأكمل : دار في يد رجل ادعاها رجلان أحدهما ابن أخ الذي في يده وأقام كل بينة أنها له ورثها عن أبيه فلان لا وارث له غيره فقيل أن يقضي القاضي مات العم ولم يترك وارثاً غير ابن أخيه دفعت إليه ولم تبطل بينته فيقضي القاضي بالدار بينهما ثم إن أقام الأجنبي بينة بعده على أنها داره ورثها عن أبيه لم يصح، فإن زكيت شهود الأجنبي ولم يزك شهود ابن الأخ فقضى بها للأجنبي، فإن زكيت بينة ابن الأخ بعده لم يقض بشيء وتمامه فيها قوله : (وبإباء أحدهما بعد القضاء لم يأخذ الآخر كله) لأنه صار مقضياً عليه بالنصف فانفسخ البيع فيه لظهور استحقاقه بالبينة لولا بينة صاحبه. قيد بقوله «بعد القضاء» لأنه قبل القضاء له أخذ الجميع لأنه يدعي الكل ولم يفسخ سببه والعود إلى النصف للمزاحمة ولم يوجد ونظيره تسليم أحد الشفيعين قبل القضاء ونظير الأول تسليمه بعد القضاء . ومع أحدهما قوله : ( وإن أرخا فللسابق لأنه أثبت الشراء في زمن لا ينازعه فيه أحد فاندفع الآخر به، فإن كان البائع قبض الثمن منه رده إليه كما في السراج الوهاج. قيد بكونهما أرخا لأنه لو أرخ أحدهما فقط فهو لصاحب الوقت لثبوت ملكه في ذلك الوقت. واحتمل الآخر أن يكون قبله أو بعده فلا يقضى له بالشك. وقيد بدعوى الشراء من واحد لأنه لو اختلف بائعهما لم يترجح أسبقهما تاريخاً ولا المؤرخ فقط لأن ملك بائعهما لا تاريخ له، ولأنهما لو ادعيا الملك المطلق ولم يدعيا الشراء من ذي اليد فلا ترجيح لصاحب التاريخ عند الإمام كما سيأتي قوله : ( وإلا فلذي القبض) أي والأسبق تاريخ أحدهما قبض قدم برهانه لأن تمكنه من قبضه يدل على سبق شرائه ولأنهما استويا في الإثبات فلا تنقضي اليد الثابتة بالشك، وظاهر الكتاب كما صرح به في المحيط تقديم صاحب القبض سواء أرخا واستوى تاريخهما أو لم يؤرخا وأرخت إحداهما فقط، وإنما يتأخر صاحبه إذا سبق تاريخ غيره لأن الصريح يفوق الدلالة فاقتصار الشارح في قوله «وإلا على ما إذا لم يؤرخا قصور. ولي إشكال في عبارة الكتاب هو أن أصل المسألة مفروضة في خارجين ينازعان فيما في يد ثالث فإذا كان أحدهما قبض كان ذا يد تنازع مع خارج فلم تكن مع المسألة، ثم رأيت في المعراج ما يزيله من جواز أن يراد أنه أثبت بالبينة قبضه فيما مضى من الزمان وهو الآن في يد البائع ا هـ. إلا أنه يشكل ما ذكره بعده عن الذخيرة بأن ثبوت اليد لأحدهما بالمعاينة ا هـ . والحق أنها مسألة أخرى وكان ينبغي إفرادها وحاصلها أن الرجلين ملكاً مطلقاً وكذا لو ادعيا تلقي الملك من اثنين بإرث أو شراء قوله : (لأنه لو اختلف بائعهما لم يترجح أسبقهما) قلت : سيأتي في شرح قول المتن وعلى الشراء من آخر» نقل مثل ما ذكره هنا عن الزيلعي تبعاً للكافي وأنه سهو بل يقدم الأسبق، وقد علمت أن فيه اختلاف الرواية نعم
Book of Claims, Chapter of Claim of Two Men. He who has it in hand or they did not mention that, then judgment is given for the second over the one against whom judgment was given, and its completion is in Khizanat al-Akmal. From it are derived rulings of the book’s issue: when one of them presents evidence of purchase and judgment is given for him, then the other presents evidence, then judgment is given for him over the one against whom judgment was given, contrary to if they both presented evidence and judgment was given by halving, then one of them presents evidence, it is not heard. It is restricted by each of them claiming purchase only, to guard against when one claims purchase and emancipation, and the other claims purchase only, then the claimant of emancipation is preferred, because emancipation is like possession; thus in Khizanat al-Akmal. And it is restricted by his saying 'from him' because if they claimed purchase from other than the possessor, it will come later. And his saying 'with its price' meaning half the price he specified; if one claims he bought it for one hundred and the other for two hundred, the first takes half for fifty and the other takes half for one hundred. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention whether the price was paid first or not because there is no difference, but if each proves purchase and payment, he recovers half of what he paid, as in Khizanat al-Akmal. The apparent meaning of his absolute statement is that no consideration is given to the possessor confirming one of them. And in al-Imadiyya: the possessor's acknowledgment of one of them is considered because it is testimony regarding his statement. And in the benefits of my grandfather Shaykh al-Islam Burhan al-Din: If the seller testifies to ownership for his buyer while the property is in another’s hand, saying 'this property is his because I sold it to him' or 'it was mine and I sold it to him', then if the claimant in his claim alleged purchase from him, it is not accepted because it is testimony regarding his own statement. End. And his statement points to the issue of dispute over inheritance: if two outsiders each claim inheritance from their father... no: it is the truth. When they testified, he said to the judge: ask about them, for they testified falsely against me, and I did not think they would testify; it is not binding on him, and he asked about them because it is an acknowledgment conditional on prohibition, so it is not valid. Then his saying: (And restricted by the claim of purchase from one etc.) He said in Nur al-Ayn: Qadi Khan: two outsiders claim purchase from two, judgment is given between them in halves, and if they dated and one is earlier, he is more entitled in the apparent narrations; according to Muhammad, date is not considered, meaning judgment is between them; if only one dated, judgment is between them in halves by agreement. So if one has possession, the outsider is preferred; summary: unless the date of the possessor precedes; Hidaya: two outsiders prove purchase of something from two and dated, they are equal because they prove ownership for their seller, so it is as if they (the sellers) attended and claimed, then each is given choice as before means in the issue of two outsiders claiming purchase from the possessor. (Kafa): If they prove purchase from two and one’s date is earlier, the narrations of the books differ: what is in al-Hidaya indicates no regard for earlier date but judgment between them; in (Bas) it indicates explicitly that the earlier is better. The humble one says: and it is supported by what passed from Qadi Khan that it is the apparent narration, so what is in al-Hidaya is choosing the saying of Muhammad. End. Then he quoted from the author of Jami‘ al-Fusulayn prefering what is in al-Hidaya and refuted it by saying that the evidence of al-Mabsut and Qadi Khan is that the earlier in date attributes ownership to himself in a time when no one disputes it, which is stronger than the evidence of those who say that earlier date is not considered, which is their saying because they prove ownership for their seller, so it is as if they attended and claimed ownership without date. The strength of the first is not hidden to one who reflects. It is preferred because it is the apparent narration. Then he said: I have not seen in the books explicitly what if two possessors claim purchase from two, except that the author of al-Wajiz said after mentioning issues of claim: judgment did not give the other all of it, and if they dated, then for the earlier, otherwise for the possessor, and purchase is more entitled than gift, and if he proves, judgment is given for him of it between them. Therefore, it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: A house is in the hand of a man, two men claim it, one is the nephew of the one in whose hand it is, and each presents evidence that it is his, inherited from his father so-and-so, and he has no other heir. It was said that the judge should rule, the uncle died leaving no heir other than the nephew, so it is given to him and his evidence is not invalidated; the judge rules the house between them. Then if the outsider presents evidence afterwards that it is his house inherited from his father, it is not valid. If the outsider’s witnesses are declared upright and the nephew’s witnesses are not, then judgment is for the outsider. If the nephew’s evidence is declared upright afterwards, nothing is judged. And its completion is in it. His saying: (And by one’s refusal after judgment, the other does not take all of it) because he became subject to judgment for half, so the sale is annulled regarding it due to apparent entitlement by evidence if not for his companion’s evidence. It is restricted by his saying 'after judgment' because before judgment he takes all, since he claims all and his cause is not annulled, and the return to half is due to competition, which did not exist. Its analogy is one of the two pre-emptors surrendering before judgment; analogy of the first is his surrender after judgment. And with one of them, his saying: (And if they dated, then for the earlier, because he proved purchase in a time when no one disputes him, so the other is repelled; if the seller received the price from him, he returns it to him, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. It is restricted by them having dated because if only one dated, it is for the owner of the time due to proof of ownership at that time; the other could be before or after, so not judged due to doubt. It is restricted by claiming purchase from one because if their sellers differ, the earlier in date is not preferred, nor the one who dated only, because their sellers' ownership has no date, and because if they claim absolute ownership and do not claim purchase from the possessor, then the one with date has no preference according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as will come. His saying: (Otherwise, for the possessor of possession) meaning if the earlier in date has possession, his evidence is presented, because his ability to take possession indicates the precedence of his purchase, and because they are equal in proof, so the established hand is not removed by doubt. The apparent meaning of the book, as stated in al-Muhit, is preference of the possessor regardless of whether they dated and their dates are equal, or they did not date, or only one dated; his counterpart is only delayed if another’s date is earlier, because the explicit outweighs the indication. So the commentator’s restriction in his saying 'otherwise' to when they did not date is a shortcoming. I have an objection regarding the wording of the book: the original issue is assumed in two outsiders contesting what is in the hand of a third; if one of them had taken possession, he would be a possessor contesting with an outsider, so it would not be part of the issue. Then I saw in al-Mi‘raj what removes it: it is permissible that it means he proved by evidence his possession in a past time, and now it is in the hand of the seller. End. Except it is problematic what he mentioned afterwards from al-Dhakhira: that the establishment of possession for one is by direct observation. End. The truth is that it is another issue, and it should have been treated separately; its gist is that the two men own absolutely, and similarly if they claim receiving ownership from two by inheritance or purchase. His saying: (Because if their sellers differ, the earlier is not preferred) I say: It will come in the commentary on the author's saying 'and on purchase from another' a quote similar to what he mentioned here from al-Zayla‘i following al-Kafi, and that it is an oversight; rather, the earlier is preferred, and you have learned that there is difference in narration. Yes.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary (sharh) of al-Bahr al-Raiq on Kanz al-Daqaiq, specifically from the Book of Claims, Chapter of Claim of Two Men. The author is explaining the rulings when two claimants each present evidence of purchase of the same property. The commentary proceeds phrase by phrase. The first ruling: if one claimant gets judgment based on his evidence, then the other later brings evidence, the second judgment overrides the first, and the property goes to the second. Exception: if both presented evidence initially and judgment was for them equally (half each), then later one brings additional evidence, it is not accepted (because the case is already resolved). The author also notes that if one claims purchase and the other claims emancipation (عتق), the emancipation claimant is preferred because emancipation is like possession. He then discusses scenarios where the price differs, where the seller's testimony is problematic, and where the possessor acknowledges one of the claimants. The text then moves to the case of two outsiders (خارجان) claiming purchase from two different sellers. Here there is a dispute: in ظاهر الرواية (apparent narration, attributed to Abu Hanifa), the earlier date gives priority; according to Imam Muhammad, date is irrelevant because each is proving ownership for his seller, so it is as if the sellers themselves claimed. The author discusses the strength of each view and notes the difference in narrations. Finally, there is discussion of possession: if one claimant has possession (قبض), his evidence is preferred because possession indicates prior purchase, unless the other has an earlier date, in which case the explicit date outweighs the indication. The text ends with a cross-reference to a later discussion.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses, used to prove a claim in court.
الشراء (al-shira'): Purchase, the act of buying a property through a valid sales contract.
التاريخ (al-tarikh): The date recorded on a written document or mentioned by witnesses, indicating when the purchase occurred.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession of a purchased item, which can be physical or constructive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two outsiders (khārijān) each prove in court that they purchased the same item from two different sellers. One of them has a purchase date earlier than the other. According to the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah (apparent narration) within the Hanafi school, how should the judge rule?
A) The earlier-dated claimant takes the entire item.
B) They split the item equally regardless of dates.
C) The item goes to the one who took possession (qabḍ) regardless of date.
D) The judge postpones the ruling until further investigation.

Key Takeaway
When two outsiders prove purchase from different sellers, the earlier date gives priority according to the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, the preferred position in the Hanafi school.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Priority Rules in Property Claims: Dated Witnesses, Possession, and Purchase Over Gift
Core priority rules with rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three Hanafi priority rules (dated witnesses, possession, purchase over gift) to resolve a multi‑claimant property dispute and explain the rationale for each rule.

Core Idea
Earlier dated witnesses beat later; possession beats no date; purchase beats gift.

When multiple claimants assert ownership over property in a third party’s hand, the Hanafi school provides clear rules to resolve the conflict. These rules are based on the strength of evidence and the nature of the transaction. This lesson unpacks the three primary priority rules from the classical text, explaining when each applies and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants and the property
Determine whether the claimants are outsiders (kharij) or possessors, whether they claim from the same transferor, and whether the property is divisible.
If one claimant has possession (qabd), he is not an outsider; special rules apply.
2
Check for dated witnesses
If both sets of witnesses are dated, the earlier date prevails because it proves ownership without competition at that time.
If only one set is dated, the dated one wins.
3
If dates are absent or equal, check possession
The claimant who can prove possession (qabd) is preferred, because possession indicates prior purchase and the established hand is not removed by doubt.
Possession here means physical receipt at some time, not necessarily current possession.
4
If dates and possession are tied, compare purchase vs gift
Purchase (shira’) is stronger than gift (hibah) because it is a bilateral contract that transfers ownership immediately, while gift requires possession to complete ownership.
This rule applies only when both claimants are outsiders and the transferor is the same.
5
Consider the effect of different transferors
If the transferors differ, dating loses priority; each claimant is proving absolute ownership from his own transferor, so they are equal unless one has an earlier date from his specific transferor.
The author notes a subtle dispute on this point; the relied‑upon view is that the earlier date still wins if the transferors are different but the date proves prior acquisition from that transferor.

قوله : (والشراء أحق من (الهبة أي لو برهن خارجان على ذي يد أحدهما على الشراء منه والآخر على الهبة منه كان الشراء أولى من الهبة لأن الشراء أقوى لكونه معاوضة من الجانبين، ولأنه يثبت الملك بنفسه والملك في الهبة يتوقف على القبض. قيد باتحاد الملك لهما إذ لو اختفا استويا لأن كلاً منهما خصم عن مملكه في إثبات ملكه وهما فيه سواء بخلاف ما إذا اتحد لاحتياجهما إلى إثبات السبب وفيه يقدم الأقوى. قال في البزازية : والمسألة بحالها فإنه يقضى للخارج إلا في أسبق التاريخ فهو للأسبق، وإن أرخت إحداهما فقط فلا ترجيح لها كما في المحيط، وإن كانت في أيديهما يقضى بينهما إلا في أسبق التاريخ فهي له كدعوى ملك مطلق. وهذا إذا الهبة بالاتفاق وهو أن يرجع في بعض الهبة شائعاً، أما الاستحقاق فيفسد الكل لأنه مقارن لا
Book of Claims, Chapter on Claims of Two Men. The judgment: if one refuses [to swear] after judgment, the other does not take all. If they date [their witnesses], the earlier one prevails; otherwise the one in possession [prevails]; and purchase is superior to gift. And if they produce evidence [without dates], judgment is given between them. Thus it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: A house in the hand of a man, claimed by two men, one being the nephew of the possessor, and each establishes by evidence that it is his, inherited from his father so-and-so with no other heir. It was said that the judge should rule that the uncle died leaving no heir other than his nephew, so it is given to him, and his evidence is not invalidated; so the judge rules the house between them. Then if the outsider later establishes that it is his house inherited from his father, it is not valid. If the outsider's witnesses are certified and the nephew's are not, he rules for the outsider. If the nephew's evidence is later certified, nothing is ruled. Complete details are therein. His statement: (And if one refuses after judgment, the other does not take all) because he has become judged against for half, so the sale is rescinded due to apparent entitlement by evidence were it not for the other's evidence. Restricted by 'after judgment' because before judgment he may take all since he claims all and his cause is not rescinded, and returning to half is due to competition which did not exist. Its analogy is the surrender of one of the two pre-emptors before judgment, and the first analogy is his surrender after judgment. His statement: (And if they date, the earlier [prevails]) because he establishes purchase in a time when none disputes with him, so the other is repelled. If the seller had taken the price from him, he returns it to him as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Restricted to both dating because if only one dates, it is for the owner of the time due to establishment of ownership at that time; the other might be before or after, so judgment is not given on doubt. And restricted to claiming purchase from one [seller] because if their sellers differ, the earlier date is not given priority, nor the dated one alone, because the ownership of their sellers has no date. And if they claim absolute ownership without claiming purchase from the possessor, there is no preference for the dated one according to Imam as will come. His statement: (Otherwise, the one in possession [prevails]) i.e., if one of them, earlier in date, has possession, his proof is presented because his ability to possess indicates prior purchase, and because they are equal in proof so established hand is not removed by doubt. The apparent meaning of the book as stated in al-Muhit is preference of the possessor whether they both dated and dates equal, or neither dated, or only one dated. He is only delayed if another's date precedes because explicit overcomes indication. So the commentator's restriction in his statement 'otherwise' to when they did not date is deficient. I have an issue with the book's wording: the original case is presumed to be two outsiders disputing over something in a third's hand; if one of them had possession, he becomes a possessor disputing with an outsider, so it is not the same case. Then I saw in al-Mi'raj what removes it: it is possible that he meant he proved by evidence his possession in the past while it is now in the seller's hand. That is problematic because what he later mentioned from al-Dhakhira is that hand is established for one by observation. The truth is it is another case and should have been isolated. The summary: two men claim absolute ownership, similarly if they claim receipt of ownership from two by inheritance or purchase. His statement: (Because if their sellers differ, the earlier is not given priority) I say: it will come in the explanation of the text 'And on purchase from another' that he transmitted similar to what he mentioned here from al-Zayla'i following al-Kafi and that it is an oversight; rather the earlier is preferred. And you have learned that there is a difference in narration [on this]. Indeed, an outsider and a possessor each claiming purchase from a third and providing evidence: the possessor prevails in three scenarios and the outsider in one. His statement: (And purchase is superior to gift) i.e., if two outsiders provide evidence against a possessor, one claiming purchase from him and the other claiming gift from him, purchase is given priority over gift because purchase is stronger being a bilateral exchange, and because it establishes ownership itself while ownership in gift depends on possession. Restricted to unity of the transferor; if different, they are equal because each argues on behalf of his transferor in establishing his ownership and they are equal in that, unlike when united because they need to establish the cause, and then the stronger is preferred. It is said in al-Bazzaziyya: One claims purchase from a man, another claims gift and possession from another, a third claims inheritance from his father, a fourth claims charity and possession from another; it is divided into fourths among them when proof is equal since they receive ownership from their transferors as if they were present and proved absolute ownership. He made it absolute, but it is restricted to no date for both; if they date with unity of transferor, the earlier [prevails] unlike if the transferors differ. If only one dates, the dated one is preferred. Restricted to both being outsiders to exclude when one is in possession and the case is the same: then judgment is for the outsider except if the earlier date [supports the possessor] so it is for the earlier; if only one dates there is no preference as in al-Muhit. If both are in possession, judgment is between them except if there is an earlier date, then it is for him like a claim of absolute ownership. This is when the claimed object is indivisible like a slave or animal. As for divisible things like a house, judgment is for the purchase claimant because the gift claimant proved the gift in entirety then the other deserved half by purchase; and entitlement of half of a gift in an undivided share that can be divided invalidates the gift by consensus, so the gift claimant's evidence is not accepted; thus the purchase claimant is alone in producing evidence; so in al-Muhit. And in al-Imadiyya: the correct view is that they are equal because supervening commonality does not invalidate gift and charity, but does invalidate pawn. He made it absolute regarding gift, but it is restricted: according to the apparent narration, preference of the earlier date as Qadikhan mentioned. His statement: 'as if they were present and proved' – the pronouns refer to the transferors i.e., from whose direction the claimants here claim ownership. Apparently their statement 'from their transferors' has a plural ya before the pronoun. He will transmit the issue from al-Hidayah shortly before his statement two folios later: 'And if an outsider proves dated ownership'. His statement: 'And in al-Imadiyya: the correct is that they are equal...' I say: the entitlement is not of the type of supervening commonality but of concomitant commonality. It is said in al-Kafi: He gifted land, planted it, and handed it over; then the crop was claimed; the gift of land is invalid because the crop with the land is considered connected as one thing; so when one is claimed, it becomes as if a part of an undivided share in something divisible is claimed, so the gift becomes invalid for the rest; so in al-Kafi. Sadr al-Shari'a said: The invalidating factor is concomitant commonality, not supervening commonality, as when one gifts then retracts part of the gift or a part is claimed, unlike pawn where supervening commonality invalidates it. And in al-Fusulayn: supervening commonality does not invalidate gift by agreement, meaning when one retracts part of the gift undividedly. As for entitlement, it invalidates the whole because it is concomitant.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe on the chapter of claims (da'wa) concerning two claimants. The author, Ibn Nujaym, comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The central rulings are: (1) When two claimants each produce witnesses, if the witnesses are dated, the earlier date prevails because it proves ownership free of competition at that time. (2) If dates are absent or equal, the claimant who has possession (qabd) prevails, as possession indicates prior purchase. (3) Between purchase and gift, purchase is stronger because it is a bilateral contract that transfers ownership immediately, whereas gift requires possession. The commentary also addresses nuances: if only one set of witnesses is dated, that party wins due to certainty; if sellers differ, dating loses priority because the transferors' ownership is undated. A key intra-Hanafi dispute appears regarding whether supervening commonality (shuyu' tari'i) invalidates a gift. The majority view (zahir al-riwaya) is that it does not, citing that a gift remains valid if the donor retracts part, while entitlement (istihqaq) is considered concomitant commonality (muqarin) which invalidates the gift from the start. The author sides with the view that purchase is preferred over gift in divisible property due to this reasoning, but notes the dispute in al-Imadiyya. Finally, the passage clarifies that if multiple claimants derive title from different transferors, they are treated as equals each proving absolute ownership, unless one has an earlier date from a single transferor.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyina): Evidence, usually two witnesses or one man and two women in property claims.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or taking delivery; indicates prior ownership in claims.
شراء (shira'): Purchase; a bilateral contract transferring ownership immediately without need for possession.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a unilateral transfer of ownership that requires possession for completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two claimants each produce witnesses to purchase from the same seller. One set of witnesses is dated (2010), the other is undated. Who prevails according to the Hanafi rule?
A) The claimant with dated witnesses because he proves ownership at a specific time.
B) The claimant with undated witnesses because possession is stronger.
C) They are equal and the property is divided.
D) The seller's heir decides.

Key Takeaway
In competing claims, the Hanafi qadi must prioritize earlier dated evidence, then possession, then purchase over gift.

Supervening vs Concomitant Commonality: Effect on Gift Validity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of commonality on gift validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between supervening commonality (shuyu' tari'i) and concomitant commonality (shuyu' muqarin) and explain why only the latter invalidates a gift.

Core Idea
Supervening commonality does not invalidate a gift; concomitant commonality invalidates from inception.

When a gift is made and later a part of the property is claimed by another rightful owner, does the gift remain valid for the remaining part? The Hanafi school distinguishes between supervening commonality (shuyu' tari'i) arising after a valid gift and concomitant commonality (shuyu' muqarin) that existed at the time of gifting. This lesson examines the intra‑Hanafi dispute on this issue and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of commonality
Determine whether the common ownership arose after the gift (supervening) or was present at the time of the gift (concomitant).
Supervening example: donor retracts part of the gift. Concomitant example: a third party proves entitlement to half from the start.
2
Apply the general rule
Supervening commonality does not invalidate gift or charity but invalidates pawn. Concomitant commonality invalidates the gift entirely.
The rationale is that the transfer was defective from the beginning.
3
Analyse the contested scenario
If a gift claimant proves gift of a house, then a purchase claimant proves purchase of half from the same donor, is the commonality supervening or concomitant?
The answer determines whether the gift stands for the remaining half or fails completely.
4
Evaluate the two positions
Majority view (al-Imadiyya and others): it is supervening, so the gift is valid for half. Ibn Nujaym and others (al-Kafi, Sadr al-Shari'a): it is concomitant, so the gift invalidates entirely.
The author favours the concomitant view.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the gift is valid for half (supervening), the donee keeps half. If the gift is void (concomitant), the property goes entirely to the purchase claimant.
The judge must decide based on the evidence; if both claims are proven, the gift claimant may lose everything.

Book of Claims, Chapter on Claims of Two Men. The judgment: if one refuses [to swear] after judgment, the other does not take all. If they date [their witnesses], the earlier one prevails; otherwise the one in possession [prevails]; and purchase is superior to gift. And if they produce evidence [without dates], judgment is given between them. Thus it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: A house in the hand of a man, claimed by two men, one being the nephew of the possessor, and each establishes by evidence that it is his, inherited from his father so-and-so with no other heir. It was said that the judge should rule that the uncle died leaving no heir other than his nephew, so it is given to him, and his evidence is not invalidated; so the judge rules the house between them. Then if the outsider later establishes that it is his house inherited from his father, it is not valid. If the outsider's witnesses are certified and the nephew's are not, he rules for the outsider. If the nephew's evidence is later certified, nothing is ruled. Complete details are therein. His statement: (And if one refuses after judgment, the other does not take all) because he has become judged against for half, so the sale is rescinded due to apparent entitlement by evidence were it not for the other's evidence. Restricted by 'after judgment' because before judgment he may take all since he claims all and his cause is not rescinded, and returning to half is due to competition which did not exist. Its analogy is the surrender of one of the two pre-emptors before judgment, and the first analogy is his surrender after judgment. His statement: (And if they date, the earlier [prevails]) because he establishes purchase in a time when none disputes with him, so the other is repelled. If the seller had taken the price from him, he returns it to him as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Restricted to both dating because if only one dates, it is for the owner of the time due to establishment of ownership at that time; the other might be before or after, so judgment is not given on doubt. And restricted to claiming purchase from one [seller] because if their sellers differ, the earlier date is not given priority, nor the dated one alone, because the ownership of their sellers has no date. And if they claim absolute ownership without claiming purchase from the possessor, there is no preference for the dated one according to Imam as will come. His statement: (Otherwise, the one in possession [prevails]) i.e., if one of them, earlier in date, has possession, his proof is presented because his ability to possess indicates prior purchase, and because they are equal in proof so established hand is not removed by doubt. The apparent meaning of the book as stated in al-Muhit is preference of the possessor whether they both dated and dates equal, or neither dated, or only one dated. He is only delayed if another's date precedes because explicit overcomes indication. So the commentator's restriction in his statement 'otherwise' to when they did not date is deficient. I have an issue with the book's wording: the original case is presumed to be two outsiders disputing over something in a third's hand; if one of them had possession, he becomes a possessor disputing with an outsider, so it is not the same case. Then I saw in al-Mi'raj what removes it: it is possible that he meant he proved by evidence his possession in the past while it is now in the seller's hand. That is problematic because what he later mentioned from al-Dhakhira is that hand is established for one by observation. The truth is it is another case and should have been isolated. The summary: two men claim absolute ownership, similarly if they claim receipt of ownership from two by inheritance or purchase. His statement: (Because if their sellers differ, the earlier is not given priority) I say: it will come in the explanation of the text 'And on purchase from another' that he transmitted similar to what he mentioned here from al-Zayla'i following al-Kafi and that it is an oversight; rather the earlier is preferred. And you have learned that there is a difference in narration [on this]. Indeed, an outsider and a possessor each claiming purchase from a third and providing evidence: the possessor prevails in three scenarios and the outsider in one. His statement: (And purchase is superior to gift) i.e., if two outsiders provide evidence against a possessor, one claiming purchase from him and the other claiming gift from him, purchase is given priority over gift because purchase is stronger being a bilateral exchange, and because it establishes ownership itself while ownership in gift depends on possession. Restricted to unity of the transferor; if different, they are equal because each argues on behalf of his transferor in establishing his ownership and they are equal in that, unlike when united because they need to establish the cause, and then the stronger is preferred. It is said in al-Bazzaziyya: One claims purchase from a man, another claims gift and possession from another, a third claims inheritance from his father, a fourth claims charity and possession from another; it is divided into fourths among them when proof is equal since they receive ownership from their transferors as if they were present and proved absolute ownership. He made it absolute, but it is restricted to no date for both; if they date with unity of transferor, the earlier [prevails] unlike if the transferors differ. If only one dates, the dated one is preferred. Restricted to both being outsiders to exclude when one is in possession and the case is the same: then judgment is for the outsider except if the earlier date [supports the possessor] so it is for the earlier; if only one dates there is no preference as in al-Muhit. If both are in possession, judgment is between them except if there is an earlier date, then it is for him like a claim of absolute ownership. This is when the claimed object is indivisible like a slave or animal. As for divisible things like a house, judgment is for the purchase claimant because the gift claimant proved the gift in entirety then the other deserved half by purchase; and entitlement of half of a gift in an undivided share that can be divided invalidates the gift by consensus, so the gift claimant's evidence is not accepted; thus the purchase claimant is alone in producing evidence; so in al-Muhit. And in al-Imadiyya: the correct view is that they are equal because supervening commonality does not invalidate gift and charity, but does invalidate pawn. He made it absolute regarding gift, but it is restricted: according to the apparent narration, preference of the earlier date as Qadikhan mentioned. His statement: 'as if they were present and proved' – the pronouns refer to the transferors i.e., from whose direction the claimants here claim ownership. Apparently their statement 'from their transferors' has a plural ya before the pronoun. He will transmit the issue from al-Hidayah shortly before his statement two folios later: 'And if an outsider proves dated ownership'. His statement: 'And in al-Imadiyya: the correct is that they are equal...' I say: the entitlement is not of the type of supervening commonality but of concomitant commonality. It is said in al-Kafi: He gifted land, planted it, and handed it over; then the crop was claimed; the gift of land is invalid because the crop with the land is considered connected as one thing; so when one is claimed, it becomes as if a part of an undivided share in something divisible is claimed, so the gift becomes invalid for the rest; so in al-Kafi. Sadr al-Shari'a said: The invalidating factor is concomitant commonality, not supervening commonality, as when one gifts then retracts part of the gift or a part is claimed, unlike pawn where supervening commonality invalidates it. And in al-Fusulayn: supervening commonality does not invalidate gift by agreement, meaning when one retracts part of the gift undividedly. As for entitlement, it invalidates the whole because it is concomitant.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe on the chapter of claims (da'wa) concerning two claimants. The author, Ibn Nujaym, comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The central rulings are: (1) When two claimants each produce witnesses, if the witnesses are dated, the earlier date prevails because it proves ownership free of competition at that time. (2) If dates are absent or equal, the claimant who has possession (qabd) prevails, as possession indicates prior purchase. (3) Between purchase and gift, purchase is stronger because it is a bilateral contract that transfers ownership immediately, whereas gift requires possession. The commentary also addresses nuances: if only one set of witnesses is dated, that party wins due to certainty; if sellers differ, dating loses priority because the transferors' ownership is undated. A key intra-Hanafi dispute appears regarding whether supervening commonality (shuyu' tari'i) invalidates a gift. The majority view (zahir al-riwaya) is that it does not, citing that a gift remains valid if the donor retracts part, while entitlement (istihqaq) is considered concomitant commonality (muqarin) which invalidates the gift from the start. The author sides with the view that purchase is preferred over gift in divisible property due to this reasoning, but notes the dispute in al-Imadiyya. Finally, the passage clarifies that if multiple claimants derive title from different transferors, they are treated as equals each proving absolute ownership, unless one has an earlier date from a single transferor.

Key Terms
شيوع طارئ (shuyu' tari'i): Supervening common ownership arising after a valid transfer, e.g., partial retraction.
شيوع مقارن (shuyu' muqarin): Concomitant common ownership existing at the time of transfer, e.g., when part is claimed from the start.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Entitlement, a claim that a third party has a pre‑existing right to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view of Ibn Nujaym, if a third party proves entitlement to half of a gifted house that existed at the time of the gift, what happens to the gift?
A) The gift remains valid for the other half because the commonality is supervening.
B) The gift is void entirely because the commonality is concomitant.
C) The gift is valid for the whole because the donor's ownership is presumed.
D) The donee must compensate the third party.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between supervening and concomitant commonality is decisive: only the latter voids a gift.

Possessor vs Outsider in Purchase Claims
States the rule for preferring possessor over outsider when both claim purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for preferring the possessor over the outsider in a purchase claim and identify the one exception where the outsider prevails.

Core Idea
Possession tips the scales: the possessor wins in three claim aspects, the outsider in one.

This lesson presents a foundational rule from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding property disputes. When two individuals—one in possession of an item and one not—both claim to have purchased it from the same third party and both produce evidence, Hanafi law gives preference to the possessor in three out of four scenarios. The single exception where the outsider prevails is noted but not detailed here; the lesson anchors the general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the two claimants: the possessor (dhu yad) who currently holds the item, and the outsider (kharj) who does not.
Possession here means physical control, not necessarily ownership.
2
Understand the dispute scenario
Both parties claim to have purchased the same item from a third party (the seller), and each produces evidence (e.g., a contract, witnesses).
The scenario assumes both claims are equally plausible on the surface.
3
Apply the default preference
The rule states that the possessor is preferred in three aspects. This means in most legal considerations (e.g., validity, priority, strength of claim), the possessor's claim takes precedence.
The exact three aspects are elaborated in later fiqh works; focus here on the general principle.
4
Note the exception
The outsider prevails in one aspect. This is a limited exception, likely tied to a specific condition such as an earlier purchase date or stronger documentary evidence.
Remember that the rule is not absolute; the outsider can win in one scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين خارجاً وذا يد ادعى كل الشراء من ثالث وبرهنا قدم ذي اليد في الوجوه الثلاثة والخارج في وجه واحد.
Book of Claims, Chapter on the Claim of Two Men: An outsider and a possessor each claim purchase from a third party and produce evidence; the possessor is preferred in the three aspects, and the outsider in one aspect.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This is a foundational ruling from the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). It addresses a scenario where two parties—one in possession of the item (dhu yad) and one not (kharj)—both claim to have purchased the same item from a third party, and both produce evidence. The rule gives preference to the possessor in three of the four possible aspects (likely regarding validity, strength, or priority), while the outsider prevails only in one specific situation (probably when the possessor lacks a date or other condition). The detailed application of this rule is explored in subsequent commentary.

Key Terms
خارج (kharj): A claimant who is not in possession of the disputed property.
ذو يد (dhu yad): The person who currently possesses the disputed property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
In a purchase claim, the possessor is preferred in three aspects and the outsider in one, reflecting the presumption of ownership from possession.

Purchase Prevails Over Gift in Conflicting Claims
Expands a matn rule on preference of purchase over gift with multiple qualifications and a dispute on shuyu'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a purchase claim outranks a gift claim when both are brought against a possessor, and identify the conditions (unity of owner, dates, possession, property type) that affect the outcome.

Core Idea
Purchase is stronger than gift because it is a commutative contract and establishes ownership immediately.

When two outsiders each claim to have acquired property from the same possessor—one by purchase, the other by gift—which claim prevails? The Hanafi school holds that purchase is preferred because it is a mutual exchange (mu'awada) and transfers ownership immediately, while gift requires delivery. However, this rule has several qualifications depending on the identity of the original owner, the presence of dates, possession, and whether the property is divisible. This lesson unpacks these nuances from Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
State the basic rule and its rationale
Identify the rule: 'Purchase is more entitled than gift.' Then note the two reasons: purchase is a commutative contract (stronger) and it transfers ownership immediately (gift needs delivery).
This is the core ruling from the matn.
2
Condition 1: Unity of the original owner
The rule applies only when both claimants derive ownership from the same person (mamluk). If the owners differ, the claims are equal because each is litigating for his own owner.
If owners differ, both have equal standing.
3
Condition 2: Dates and possession
If both claims have dates, the earlier date prevails (same owner). If only one has a date, that one is preferred. If one claimant is in possession, that possessor wins unless the outsider has an earlier date. If both are in possession, they split equally unless a date gives priority.
The possessor status can override the purchase preference.
4
Condition 3: Divisibility of property
For indivisible property (e.g., slave), purchase wins. For divisible property (e.g., house), there is a dispute: some say purchase wins because acquisition of half by purchase invalidates the gift by consensus; others say they are equal because supervening shuyu' does not invalidate gift.
This leads into the intra-Hanafi dispute on shuyu'.
5
Analyze the shuyu' dispute
The author sides with the view that simultaneous (not supervening) shuyu' invalidates gift. Distinguish supervening (e.g., partial revocation) from simultaneous (e.g., third-party acquisition at origin).
The dispute affects the outcome for divisible property.
