His statement: 'And purchase is more entitled than gift' means: If two outsiders produce evidence against a possessor—one proving purchase from him and the other proving a gift from him—then purchase is preferred over gift because purchase is stronger, being a mutual exchange (mu'awada) from both sides, and it establishes ownership by itself, whereas ownership in gift depends on possession (qabd). This is conditioned on the unity of the owner (mamluk) for both; if the owners differ, they are equal because each is a litigant on behalf of his owner in establishing his ownership, and they are equal in that. But if the owner is the same, they need to prove the cause, and the stronger one is preferred. It is stated in al-Bazzaziyya: If one claims purchase from a man, another claims gift and possession from someone else, a third claims inheritance from his father, and a fourth claims charity and possession from yet another, it is divided among them in quarters when evidence is equal, since they derive ownership from their respective owners, as if they were present and proved absolute ownership. End quote. This is unconditional but is restricted to cases without dates; if they have dates and the owner is the same, the earlier one prevails, unlike when the owners differ. If only one has a date, the dated one is preferred. It is conditioned on both being outsiders to exclude the case where one is in possession; in that case, judgment is for the possessor except when the earlier date is for the outsider—then it is for the earlier. If only one has a date, there is no preference, as in al-Muhit. If both are in possession, judgment is between them except for the earlier date, then it is for that one, like a claim of absolute ownership. This applies if the claimed property is indivisible, like a slave or an animal. As for divisible property like a house, judgment is for the purchase claimant because the gift claimant proves the gift in the whole, then the other acquires half by purchase. The acquisition of half in a share subject to division invalidates the gift by consensus, so the evidence of the gift claimant is not accepted; thus the purchase claimant is alone in producing evidence. So in al-Muhit. In al-Imadiyya: The correct view is that they are equal because supervening shuyu' (co-ownership) does not invalidate a gift or charity but invalidates a mortgage. End quote. He spoke generally about gift, but it is conditioned: according to zahir al-riwaya, the earlier is preferred, as mentioned by Qadi Khan. His statement: 'as if they were present and proved'—the pronouns refer to the owners (mamlikin), meaning those from whom the claimants here claim ownership. It appears that their phrase 'from their owners' (min mamlikihim) has a plural ya' before the pronoun. He will later transmit the issue from al-Hidayah shortly before his statement two pages later: 'If the outsider proves a dated ownership'... His statement: 'And in al-Imadiyya, the correct view is that they are equal'—I say: The acquisition (istihqaq) is not of the type of supervening shuyu' but rather simultaneous (muqarin). It is stated in al-Kafi: If one gives a land as a gift, plants it, and delivers it, then the crop is acquired (by a third party), the gift of the land becomes void because the crop and land are connected as one thing; if one is acquired, it is as if a part of the indivisible property is acquired, so the gift becomes void for the remainder. So in al-Kafi. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: The invalidator is simultaneous shuyu', not supervening shuyu', as when one gives a gift and then takes back a part of it as a share, or a part is acquired as a share. Unlike a mortgage, supervening shuyu' invalidates it. In al-Fusulayn: Supervening shuyu' does not invalidate a gift by consensus...
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the rule that a purchase claim outranks a gift claim when both outsiders bring evidence against a possessor. The commentary opens by stating the rule: purchase is more entitled (ahaqq) than gift. The reason is twofold: purchase is a commutative contract (mu'awada) from both sides, making it inherently stronger, and it transfers ownership immediately upon contract, whereas gift requires delivery (qabd) for ownership to be established. The author then qualifies the rule: it applies only when both claimants derive ownership from the same original owner (mamluk). If the owners differ, the two claims are equal because each litigates on behalf of his own owner. When the owner is the same, the stronger claim (purchase) prevails. The commentary further discusses the effect of dates (tarikh): if both have dates and the owner is the same, the earlier is preferred; if only one has a date, that one is preferred. If the property is in the possession of one of the claimants, the possessor wins unless the outsider has an earlier date. When both are in possession, judgment is between them equally unless one has an earlier date. Special rules apply for divisible vs. indivisible property. For indivisible property (e.g., a slave), the purchase claimant wins; for divisible property (e.g., a house), there is a dispute: some say purchase wins because acquisition of half by purchase invalidates the gift by consensus, while others (in al-Imadiyya) say they are equal because supervening shuyu' does not invalidate a gift. The author sides with the latter, citing al-Kafi and Sadr al-Shari'ah that the istihqaq (acquisition) is simultaneous (muqarin) to the gift, not supervening, so it invalidates the gift. The passage ends by distinguishing between supervening and simultaneous shuyu' in various transactions.

Key Terms
معاوضة (mu'awada): A commutative contract involving exchange of countervalues, like sale.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession/delivery that is necessary for ownership in gift.
شيوع (shuyu'): Shared ownership in an undivided part of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is purchase preferred over gift when both claims are made against a possessor?
A) Because purchase requires a witness
B) Because purchase is a commutative contract and transfers ownership immediately
C) Because gift is always invalid
D) Because the possessor prefers purchase

Key Takeaway
Purchase outranks gift in conflicting evidence because it is a commutative contract and immediate ownership, but this preference is subject to conditions of owner unity, dates, possession, and property divisibility, with a key intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of shuyu'.

Equality of Purchase and Dower – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on relative strength of purchase and dower in claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether purchase and dower are equal in strength when both claim from a possessor, and state the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf says purchase and dower are equal; Muhammad says purchase prevails – the difference lies in how to satisfy both claims.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator discusses the strength of purchase and dower as commutative contracts when two claimants produce evidence against a single possessor. The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani reveals subtle differences in how they balance conflicting evidence and the nature of ownership transfer.

Guided Path
1
State the core claim
Both purchase and dower are commutative contracts. What is the default ruling regarding their strength?
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf says they are equal; Muhammad says purchase is more entitled.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's dalil
Each contract establishes ownership immediately by itself, so they are equal in strength.
4
Examine Muhammad's dalil
Preferring purchase allows both claims to be satisfied: the purchaser gets the item, the wife gets the value from the husband, because marriage on a chattel owned by another is valid and its value is due.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If equal, the item is divided; if purchase prevails, the wife gets the value from the husband.

Source Text

من الرهن لأن الشراء يفيد الملك بعوض للحال والرهن لا يفيد الملك للحال فكان الشراء أقوى ا هـ . ومقتضاه استواء الشراء والهبة بعوض، ولم أر حكم الشراء الفاسد مع القبض والهبة مع القبض فإن الملك في كل منهما عند عدم التاريخ القبض كما سنبينه وأما إذا أرخا قدم الأسبق، وإن لم أحدهما يؤرخا ومع قبض كان أولى، وكذا إذا أرخ أحدهما فقط كما قدمناه في الشراء من ذي اليد. وفي الخلاصة : ولو كان كلاهما هبة أو صدقة أو أحدهما هبة والآخر صدقة فما لم لم يذكر الشهود القبض لا يصح، وإن ذكروا القبض ولم يؤرخوا أو أرخوا تاريخاً واحداً فهو بينهما إذا كان لا يحتمل القسمة كالعبد ،ونحوه، وإن كان يحتمل القسمة كالدار ونحوها فلا يقضى لهما بشيء عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يقضى بينهما نصفين ولو كان في يد أحدهما يقضى له بالإجماع ا هـ . قوله : (والشراء والمهر (سواء يعني لو ادعى أحدهما الشراء من ذي اليد وامرأة أنه تزوّجها عليه فهما سواء لاستوائهما في القوة فإن كل واحد منهما معاوضة يثبت الملك بنفسه، وهذا عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد الشراء أولى ولهما على الزوج القيمة لأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بتقديم الشراء إذ التزويج على عين مملوك للغير صحيح فيجب قيمته عند تعذر طارئ؛ كذا ذكر شيخ الإسلام أبو بكر في هبة المحيط، هكذا قرره منلا خسرو في شرحه ثم قال أقول : عدة صورة الاستحقاق من أ أمثلة الشيوع الطارئ غير صحيح، والصحيح ما في الكافي والفصولين فإن الاستحقاق إذا ظهر بالبينة كان مستنداً إلى ما قبل الهبة فيكون مقارناً لها لا طارئاً عليها اهـ. كذا في منح الغفار . قوله : ( وينبغي تقديم الشراء للمعاوضة قال بعض الفضلاء : رده المقدسي بأن الأولى تقديم الهبة لكونها مشروعة قوله : (فهو بينهما إذا كان لا يحتمل القسمة إلى آخر كلامه تأمله مع قوله «الصدقة أولى من الهبة .
And purchase and dower are equal, and mortgage is more entitled than gift. Even if the two outsiders produce evidence of ownership and date, or of delivery as in al-Muhit, and it is conditioned that it is without compensation, because if it is with compensation it is a sale as in al-Muhit, for he said: Gift with compensation is more entitled than mortgage because purchase gives ownership with compensation immediately, and mortgage does not give ownership immediately, so purchase is stronger. End quote. Its implication is the equality of purchase and gift with compensation. I have not seen the ruling of a void purchase (fāsid) with possession and a gift with possession, for ownership in each depends on possession, and purchase should be preferred due to its commutative nature. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that purchase is more entitled than charity and that a charity that has been received is equal to a gift that has been received due to equality in gratuitousness; there is no preference for charity due to irrevocability (luzūm) because the effect of irrevocability appears in the second state (i.e., inability to revoke in the future), and preference is based on a quality existing in the present. A gift may be irrevocable if given to a mahram, and charity may not be irrevocable if given to a rich person. This applies to indivisible property by agreement, and to divisible property according to some because the shuyū' is supervening; according to others it is not valid because it would be executing the gift in a share, making it like establishing two evidences on a mortgage, and this is more correct. Thus in al-Hidāyah. The gist is that charity is more entitled than gift regarding divisible property, and this is when there is no date of possession as we will explain; if they have dates, the earlier is preferred, and if one of them does not have a date and the other has possession, it is more entitled; similarly if only one has a date, as we have presented regarding purchase from a possessor. In al-Khulāṣah: If both are gift or charity, or one is gift and the other charity, and the witnesses do not mention possession, it is not valid; if they mention possession and do not date it, or they have the same date, it is between them if it is indivisible like a slave and the like; if it is divisible like a house and the like, nothing is judged for both according to Abu Hanifah, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is judged between them in halves. If it is in the hand of one, it is judged for him by consensus. End quote. His statement: 'And purchase and dower are equal' means that if one claims purchase from the possessor and a woman claims that she married him (the possessor) for that dower, they are equal because they are equal in strength; each is a commutative contract that establishes ownership by itself. This is according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Purchase is more entitled, and they (the purchaser and the wife) have the value from the husband because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, since marriage on a chattel owned by another is valid, so its value is obligatory when inability arises. Thus mentioned Shaykh al-Islam Abu Bakr in Hibat al-Muhit, and Mulla Khusraw confirmed it in his commentary. Then he said: I say that several cases of acquisition (istihqaq) are examples of supervening shuyū' but this is not correct; the correct view is what is in al-Kafi and al-Fusulayn, for acquisition when proven by evidence is based on something before the gift, so it is simultaneous (muqarin) with it, not supervening. End quote. Thus in Manh al-Ghaffar. His statement: 'And it is recommended to prefer purchase due to commutativity'—some scholars said: al-Maqdisi refuted it saying that the better is to prefer the gift because it is legislated. His statement: 'So it is between them if it is not subject to division...' to the end of his words, reflect on it along with his statement 'charity is more entitled than gift.'
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage addresses multiple rules in the chapter of claims. First, the matn states that purchase and dower (mahr) are equal (sawa') in strength when both claims are made against a possessor. The commentary explains: if a man claims purchase from the possessor, and a woman claims that she married the possessor (and the dower is that specific item), both are commutative contracts that transfer ownership immediately, so they are equal. This is the view of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, saying purchase is more entitled because it is a more common transaction, and the purchaser and the wife jointly have a claim for the value against the husband. The author then discusses the rule that mortgage (rahn) is more entitled than gift (hiba). However, a gift with compensation (hiba bi-iwad) is actually a sale, so it outranks mortgage. Purchase and such gift-with-compensation are equal. The author notes he hasn't seen a ruling on void purchase (fāsid) vs. gift with possession, but suggests purchase should be preferred due to its commutative nature. Next, the author explains the author's (of Kanz) reference to charity (sadaqa): purchase is more entitled than charity, but a charity that has been delivered is equal to a gift that has been delivered. Charity is not given preference due to irrevocability (luzum) because that is a future effect; the present strength of the contract matters. Charity may not be irrevocable if given to a rich person, and gift may be irrevocable if given to a mahram. For indivisible property, they are equal by agreement; for divisible property, there is a disagreement. The correct view (from al-Hidayah) is that charity is more entitled than gift for divisible property, because supervening shuyu' does not invalidate charity as it does gift. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Khulasah: if both are gift or charity or one each, and witnesses mention possession but no date or same date, then if the property is indivisible (e.g., slave), it is between them; if divisible (e.g., house), according to Abu Hanifah, nothing is given to either, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say split half and half. If one is in possession, that one wins by consensus. Finally, there is a brief comment on preferring purchase due to commutativity, with some scholars preferring gift because it is more legislated. The last statement is a reference to a matn phrase that is left for reflection.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): Dower; the property given by the husband to the wife in marriage.
معاوضة (mu'awada): Commutative contract; a transaction where each party gives something in exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, why are purchase and dower considered equal in strength?
A) Because both are gratuitous transfers
B) Because both are commutative contracts that establish ownership immediately
C) Because dower is always superior
D) Because purchase requires delivery

Key Takeaway
The purchase-dower dispute shows that even within the Hanafi school, the relative strength of commutative contracts can be contested based on how evidence and transaction validity are balanced.

Mortgage vs Gift and Charity vs Gift in Claims
Multifaceted rules on priority of claims: mortgage vs gift, charity vs gift, and effect of dates and possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that mortgage is less entitled than a gift with compensation, and explain the conditions under which charity is preferred over gift when two claimants produce evidence without dates.

Core Idea
Mortgage is weaker than purchase but stronger than pure gift; charity and gift are equal in strength unless the property is divisible, where charity may be preferred.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion of conflicting claims. It covers the relative strength of mortgage, gift, charity, and purchase, and how possession, date, and divisibility affect the outcome. The rulings are based on the nature of each contract as commutative or gratuitous.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hierarchy
Purchase and gift-with-compensation are strongest; mortgage is weaker; pure gift and charity are weaker still but equal among themselves.
2
Analyze the mortgage vs gift rule
Mortgage is more entitled than pure gift; but gift with compensation (i.e., sale) outranks mortgage.
3
Examine the charity vs gift dispute
The author indicates that charity and gift are equal in strength, but for divisible property, some say charity is more entitled because shuyu' (shared ownership) is supervening and does not prevent charity as it does gift.
4
Apply the Khulasah rule
If two claimants both have possession and witnesses mention possession but no date or same date, then for indivisible property they share; for divisible property, Abu Hanifa says neither gets anything, while the two say split half and half.
5
Note the impact of date and possession
The earlier date prevails; if one has possession without date, that one is preferred.

عند عدم التاريخ القبض كما سنبينه وأما إذا أرخا قدم الأسبق، وإن لم أحدهما يؤرخا ومع قبض كان أولى، وكذا إذا أرخ أحدهما فقط كما قدمناه في الشراء من ذي اليد. وفي الخلاصة : ولو كان كلاهما هبة أو صدقة أو أحدهما هبة والآخر صدقة فما لم لم يذكر الشهود القبض لا يصح، وإن ذكروا القبض ولم يؤرخوا أو أرخوا تاريخاً واحداً فهو بينهما إذا كان لا يحتمل القسمة كالعبد ،ونحوه، وإن كان يحتمل القسمة كالدار ونحوها فلا يقضى لهما بشيء عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يقضى بينهما نصفين ولو كان في يد أحدهما يقضى له بالإجماع ا هـ . قوله : (والشراء والمهر (سواء يعني لو ادعى أحدهما الشراء من ذي اليد وامرأة أنه تزوّجها عليه فهما سواء لاستوائهما في القوة فإن كل واحد منهما معاوضة يثبت الملك بنفسه، وهذا عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد الشراء أولى ولهما على الزوج القيمة لأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بتقديم الشراء إذ التزويج على عين مملوك للغير صحيح فيجب قيمته عند تعذر طارئ؛ كذا ذكر شيخ الإسلام أبو بكر في هبة المحيط، هكذا قرره منلا خسرو في شرحه ثم قال أقول : عدة صورة الاستحقاق من أ أمثلة الشيوع الطارئ غير صحيح، والصحيح ما في الكافي والفصولين فإن الاستحقاق إذا ظهر بالبينة كان مستنداً إلى ما قبل الهبة فيكون مقارناً لها لا طارئاً عليها اهـ. كذا في منح الغفار . قوله : ( وينبغي تقديم الشراء للمعاوضة قال بعض الفضلاء : رده المقدسي بأن الأولى تقديم الهبة لكونها مشروعة قوله : (فهو بينهما إذا كان لا يحتمل القسمة إلى آخر كلامه تأمله مع قوله «الصدقة أولى من الهبة .
And purchase and dower are equal, and mortgage is more entitled than gift. Even if the two outsiders produce evidence of ownership and date, or of delivery as in al-Muhit, and it is conditioned that it is without compensation, because if it is with compensation it is a sale as in al-Muhit, for he said: Gift with compensation is more entitled than mortgage because purchase gives ownership with compensation immediately, and mortgage does not give ownership immediately, so purchase is stronger. End quote. Its implication is the equality of purchase and gift with compensation. I have not seen the ruling of a void purchase (fāsid) with possession and a gift with possession, for ownership in each depends on possession, and purchase should be preferred due to its commutative nature. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that purchase is more entitled than charity and that a charity that has been received is equal to a gift that has been received due to equality in gratuitousness; there is no preference for charity due to irrevocability (luzūm) because the effect of irrevocability appears in the second state (i.e., inability to revoke in the future), and preference is based on a quality existing in the present. A gift may be irrevocable if given to a mahram, and charity may not be irrevocable if given to a rich person. This applies to indivisible property by agreement, and to divisible property according to some because the shuyū' is supervening; according to others it is not valid because it would be executing the gift in a share, making it like establishing two evidences on a mortgage, and this is more correct. Thus in al-Hidāyah. The gist is that charity is more entitled than gift regarding divisible property, and this is when there is no date of possession as we will explain; if they have dates, the earlier is preferred, and if one of them does not have a date and the other has possession, it is more entitled; similarly if only one has a date, as we have presented regarding purchase from a possessor. In al-Khulāṣah: If both are gift or charity, or one is gift and the other charity, and the witnesses do not mention possession, it is not valid; if they mention possession and do not date it, or they have the same date, it is between them if it is indivisible like a slave and the like; if it is divisible like a house and the like, nothing is judged for both according to Abu Hanifah, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is judged between them in halves. If it is in the hand of one, it is judged for him by consensus. End quote. His statement: 'And purchase and dower are equal' means that if one claims purchase from the possessor and a woman claims that she married him (the possessor) for that dower, they are equal because they are equal in strength; each is a commutative contract that establishes ownership by itself. This is according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Purchase is more entitled, and they (the purchaser and the wife) have the value from the husband because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, since marriage on a chattel owned by another is valid, so its value is obligatory when inability arises. Thus mentioned Shaykh al-Islam Abu Bakr in Hibat al-Muhit, and Mulla Khusraw confirmed it in his commentary. Then he said: I say that several cases of acquisition (istihqaq) are examples of supervening shuyū' but this is not correct; the correct view is what is in al-Kafi and al-Fusulayn, for acquisition when proven by evidence is based on something before the gift, so it is simultaneous (muqarin) with it, not supervening. End quote. Thus in Manh al-Ghaffar. His statement: 'And it is recommended to prefer purchase due to commutativity'—some scholars said: al-Maqdisi refuted it saying that the better is to prefer the gift because it is legislated. His statement: 'So it is between them if it is not subject to division...' to the end of his words, reflect on it along with his statement 'charity is more entitled than gift.'
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage addresses multiple rules in the chapter of claims. First, the matn states that purchase and dower (mahr) are equal (sawa') in strength when both claims are made against a possessor. The commentary explains: if a man claims purchase from the possessor, and a woman claims that she married the possessor (and the dower is that specific item), both are commutative contracts that transfer ownership immediately, so they are equal. This is the view of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, saying purchase is more entitled because it is a more common transaction, and the purchaser and the wife jointly have a claim for the value against the husband. The author then discusses the rule that mortgage (rahn) is more entitled than gift (hiba). However, a gift with compensation (hiba bi-iwad) is actually a sale, so it outranks mortgage. Purchase and such gift-with-compensation are equal. The author notes he hasn't seen a ruling on void purchase (fāsid) vs. gift with possession, but suggests purchase should be preferred due to its commutative nature. Next, the author explains the author's (of Kanz) reference to charity (sadaqa): purchase is more entitled than charity, but a charity that has been delivered is equal to a gift that has been delivered. Charity is not given preference due to irrevocability (luzum) because that is a future effect; the present strength of the contract matters. Charity may not be irrevocable if given to a rich person, and gift may be irrevocable if given to a mahram. For indivisible property, they are equal by agreement; for divisible property, there is a disagreement. The correct view (from al-Hidayah) is that charity is more entitled than gift for divisible property, because supervening shuyu' does not invalidate charity as it does gift. The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Khulasah: if both are gift or charity or one each, and witnesses mention possession but no date or same date, then if the property is indivisible (e.g., slave), it is between them; if divisible (e.g., house), according to Abu Hanifah, nothing is given to either, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say split half and half. If one is in possession, that one wins by consensus. Finally, there is a brief comment on preferring purchase due to commutativity, with some scholars preferring gift because it is more legislated. The last statement is a reference to a matn phrase that is left for reflection.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge; a security interest in property for a debt.
صدقة (sadaqa): Charity; a gratuitous transfer for religious merit.
هبة بعوض (hiba bi-iwad): Gift with compensation; legally a sale.
شيوع (shuyu'): Common ownership or shared ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when both claimants are gift and charity, and the property is indivisible and no date, what is the ruling?
A) Nothing is given to either
B) It is between them (shared)
C) The charity claimant gets it
D) The gift claimant gets it

Key Takeaway
The relative strength of claims depends on the nature of the transaction: commutative contracts outrank gratuitous ones, and within gratuitous, possession, date, and property divisibility determine the outcome.

Priority Between Purchase and Dowry Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on priority of transactional claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on priority between purchase and dowry claims, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) to a dispute scenario.

Core Idea
Purchase and dowry are both commutative contracts, but Muhammad gives purchase priority due to immediate exchange.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how to resolve a conflict when a purchaser and a dowry claimant each present evidence against the same property. The base text claims equality, but the commentator reveals a split between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ear for ikhtilaf within the school and its practical impact on property allocation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
The scenario: one person claims purchase of a property from its current possessor; a woman claims the property was assigned as her dowry. Both present evidence (bayyinah). The base text says they are equal.
Equal in strength because both are commutative contracts (mu'awadah).
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds both claims are equal; the property is split equally between them if no date determines priority.
His reasoning: both contracts establish ownership immediately upon conclusion.
3
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani gives priority to the purchaser; the purchaser takes the property, and the woman claims its value from the husband.
His reasoning: purchase involves immediate exchange of price and property, while dowry requires valuation.
4
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf, property split equally; under Muhammad, purchaser gets property, woman gets value from husband.
This affects how a judge would rule in a real case.
5
Note the later commentator's correction
Mulla Khusraw (and the commentator) corrects a previous view that some cases of accidental common ownership (shuyu' tari') apply here; he clarifies that when evidence shows the right existed before the gift, it is concurrent, not accidental.
This refines the application of the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشراء والمهر سواء . قوله : (والشراء والمهر (سواء يعني لو ادعى أحدهما الشراء من ذي اليد وامرأة أنه تزوّجها عليه فهما سواء لاستوائهما في القوة فإن كل واحد منهما معاوضة يثبت الملك بنفسه، وهذا عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد الشراء أولى ولهما على الزوج القيمة لأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بتقديم الشراء إذ التزويج على عين مملوك للغير صحيح فيجب قيمته عند تعذر طارئ ؛ كذا ذكر شيخ الإسلام أبو بكر في هبة المحيط، هكذا قرره منلا خسرو في شرحه ثم قال أقول : عدة صورة الاستحقاق من أمثلة الشيوع الطارئ غير صحيح، والصحيح ما في الكافي والفصولين فإن الاستحقاق إذا ظهر بالبينة كان مستنداً إلى ما قبل الهبة فيكون مقارناً لها لا طارئاً عليها اهـ. كذا في منح الغفار .
And purchase and dowry are equal. His saying: (And purchase and dowry are equal) means if one claimant claims purchase from the possessor and a woman claims that he married her on it, then they are equal due to their equal strength, for each is a commutative contract that establishes ownership by itself, and this is according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said purchase is better, and both have the value against the husband because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving priority to purchase, since marriage on property owned by another is valid, so its value is due when delivery is impossible; as stated by Shaykh al-Islam Abu Bakr in Hibat al-Muhit, thus confirmed by Mulla Khusraw in his commentary, then he said: I say: Several examples of accidental common ownership are incorrect; the correct is what is in al-Kafi and al-Fusulayn: for when the right appears by evidence, it is based on before the gift, so it is concurrent with it, not accidental. End quote from Manh al-Ghaffar.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the priority between a purchase claim and a dowry claim over the same property. The base text states they are equal. The commentator explains that according to Abu Yusuf, both are commutative contracts (معاوضة) that establish ownership immediately, hence equal. Muhammad argues that purchase is preferred because it involves an immediate exchange of price and delivery of the property, while dowry is compensation for marriage that may require valuation, so priority is given to purchase. The practical consequence: under Muhammad, the purchaser takes the property, and the woman gets its value from the husband. The commentator then quotes Mulla Khusraw correcting a previous view about examples of accidental common ownership (شیوع طارئ), affirming that when the right is proven by evidence, it is concurrent with the gift, not accidental.

Key Terms
شراء (shiraa): Purchase; a contract of sale establishing ownership immediately upon offer and acceptance.
مهر (mahr): Dowry; the property given by husband to wife in marriage, claimed here as a debt against the husband's property.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence; proof presented in court, typically two witnesses.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): Commutative contract; a contract where each party gives and receives something of value, e.g., sale, marriage.
شيوع طارئ (shuyu' tari'): Accidental common ownership; when a property becomes shared due to an external event, not by the owner's choice.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Entitlement; a right established by evidence, here referring to the right of the original owner appearing after a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, which claim is preferred when a purchaser and a dowry claimant both present evidence on the same property?
A) The purchaser's claim
B) The dowry claimant's claim
C) Both are equal
D) Neither is valid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when purchase and dowry claims conflict, Abu Yusuf treats them equally, while Muhammad gives purchase priority due to immediate exchange.

Ruling on Indivisible Property and the Meaning of Nikah in Claims
Definition and application of rulings for claims over indivisible property, clarifying that 'nikah' means 'mahr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on property that cannot be divided when two claimants present evidence, and explain why 'nikah' in this context means 'mahr'.

Core Idea
In a claim over indivisible property, equal evidence means equal shares; and 'nikah' here means mahr, not marriage.

When two parties each present evidence over a single indivisible item like a slave, how does a Hanafi judge decide? And when one party claims through 'nikah', what does that term really mean? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies these tricky procedural points, distinguishing between the marriage contract and the dowry obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute scenario
Recognize that the property in question (e.g., a slave) cannot be physically divided without harm. This triggers special rules.
Indivisibility is key; if divisible, the rules differ.
2
State the ruling on evidence when dates are equal
If both claimants present evidence with equal dates (or no dates), the property is split equally between them.
If one date precedes, that claimant is preferred.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'nikah'
In this claim context, 'nikah' refers to the mahr (dowry) claim of the wife, not the marriage itself. This is a crucial definition to avoid confusion.
Evidence: the commentator cites al-Muhit and al-Imadiyya to support this.
4
Apply to the specific case: mahr vs purchase
When one claimant is a woman seeking mahr and the other is a purchaser, with equal evidence, the woman gets half the property and half its value from the husband; the buyer gets half and may rescind the sale.
Recovery of price and option to rescind are specific remedies.
5
Note the equality of ghasb and wadi'ah
If two claimants present evidence over a slave, one claiming usurpation (ghasb) and the other deposit (wadi'ah), they are treated equally; the slave is split between them.
This is based on al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وينبغي تقديم الشراء للمعاوضة قال بعض الفضلاء : رده المقدسي بأن الأولى تقديم الهبة لكونها مشروعة قوله : (فهو بينهما إذا كان لا يحتمل القسمة إلى آخر كلامه تأمله مع قوله «الصدقة أولى من الهبة . تسليمه. وأفاد باستوائهما أنها بينهما فيكون للمرأة نصفها ونصف قيمتها على الزوج لاستحقاق نصف المسمى وللمشتري نصفها ويرجع بنصف الثمن إن كان أداه وله فسخ البيع لتفرق الصفقة عليه وفي البناية : هذا إذا لم يؤرخا أو أرخا واستوى تاريخهما فإن سبق تاريخ أحدهما كان أولى اهـ. وفي العمادية : ولو اجتمع نكاح وهبة أو رهن أو صدقة فالنكاح أولى ا هـ. وفي جامع الفصولين أقول : لو اجتمع نكاح وهبة يمكن أن يعمل بالبينتين لو استويا بأن تكون منكوحة لذا وهبة للآخر بأن يهب أمته المنكوحة فينبغي أن لا تبطل بينة الهبة حذراً عن تكذيب المؤمن وحملاً على الصلاح، وكذ الصدقة مع النكاح، وكذا الرهن مع النكاح هـ. وقد كتبت في حاشيته أنه وهم لأنه فهم أن المراد لو تنازعا في أمة أحدهما ادعى أنها ملكه بالهبة وآخر أنه تزوجها وليس مرادهم، وإنما المراد من النكاح المهر كما عبر به في الكتاب ولذا قال في المحيط والشراء أولى من النكاح عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف هما سواء، لمحمد أن المهر صلة من وجه إلى آخره فقد أطلق النكاح وأراد المهر، ومما يدل على ما ذكرناه أن العمادي بعد ما ذكر أن النكاح أولى قال : ثم إن كانت العين في يد أحدهما فهو أولى إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ الخارج أسبق فحينئذ يقضى للخارج، ولو كانت في أيديهما يقضى بها بينهما نصفين إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ أحدهما أسبق فيقضى له ا.هـ. فكيف يتوهم عاقل أن الكلام في المنكوحة بعد قوله تكون بينهما نصفين فيما إذا كانت في أيديهما فآخر الكلام أزال اللبس وأوضح كل تخمين وحدس وحكم بغلط الجامع عفا الله عنه. وينبغي أنهما لو تنازعا في الأمة ادعى أحدهما ملكه والآخر أنها منكوحته وهما من رجل واحد وبرهنا ولا مرجح يثبتا لعدم المنافاة فيكون ملكاً لمدعي الملك هبة أو شراء منكوحة للآخر كما بحثه الجامع ولم أره صريحاً. والغصب والإيداع سواء لما في الخلاصة : عبد في يد رجل أقام رجل البينة أنه عبده غصبه الذي في يديه وأقام آخر البينة أنه عبده أودعه الذي في يديه يقضى به بينهما اهـ.
His saying: (And it is appropriate to give priority to purchase because of compensation) Some virtuous people said: al-Maqdisi rejected it, saying that giving priority to gift is better because it is legislated. His saying: (So it is between them if it does not admit division) to the end of his speech, reflect on it with his saying: 'Charity is better than gift'. Taslimuhu. And it benefits from their equality that it is between them, so the woman takes half of it and half of its value from the husband for the entitlement of half of the named dowry, and the purchaser takes half of it and recovers half of the price if he paid it, and he has the option to rescind the sale due to division of the subject matter. In al-Binaya: This is if they did not date or dated and their dates are equal; if one's date precedes, it is better. End quote. In al-Imadiyya: If marriage (nikah) and gift or pledge or charity coexist, then marriage is better. End quote. In Jami' al-Fusulayn I say: If marriage and gift coexist, it is possible to act on both evidences if they are equal, such as if she is married to one and gifted to another by gifting his married female slave; then it is better not to nullify the evidence of gift out of caution against disbelieving the believer and to interpret it as righteousness. Similarly charity with marriage, and likewise pledge with marriage. End quote. And it was written in its margin that it is an error because he understood that the meaning is if they dispute over a female slave, one claiming ownership by gift and another that he married her, but that is not their intended meaning; rather the intended meaning of marriage (nikah) here is the dowry (mahr) as expressed in the book. Therefore al-Muhit said: Purchase is better than marriage according to Muhammad, and according to Abu Yusuf they are equal. For Muhammad, the mahr is a gift in one respect, etc. So he used 'marriage' and intended 'mahr'. Evidence of what we said is that al-Imadi after mentioning that marriage is better said: Then if the property is in the hand of one of them, he is better unless they date and the outsider's date is earlier, then judgment is for the outsider. If it is in both hands, judgment is between them in halves unless they date and one's date is earlier, then judgment is for him. End quote. So how can a sensible person imagine that the speech is about the married woman after he says 'they are between them in halves' when it is in both hands; the latter part of the speech removes confusion and clarifies all conjecture. He ruled the collector mistaken, may Allah forgive him. And it is appropriate that if they dispute over a female slave, one claiming ownership and the other that she is his wife, both being from one man, and they present evidence with no preference, they are both established because there is no contradiction, so she is owned by the one claiming ownership (as gift or purchase) and is a wife for the other, as the collector discussed, but I have not seen it explicitly. And usurpation and deposit are equal. In al-Khulasa: A slave in the hand of a man; one man presents evidence that it is his slave usurped by the possessor, and another presents evidence that it is his slave deposited with the possessor; judgment is between them. End quote.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings. First, it comments on a prior statement: some scholars argue that a gift should be preferred over purchase because it is legislated, but the author leans toward purchase due to compensation. Then it discusses the case when two claimants present evidence over property that does not admit division (e.g., a slave). The base text says 'it is between them' meaning if the property is indivisible and both have evidence with equal dates, they split equally. The commentary explains: if one is a woman claiming mahr and the other a purchaser, she gets half the property and half its value from the husband, the purchaser gets half and can rescind the sale due to partial ownership. If one date precedes, that claimant is preferred. Then it clarifies that in this context 'nikah' (marriage) actually means 'mahr' (dowry). This is a crucial definition: earlier texts like al-Imadiyya said 'marriage is better' but intended 'mahr'. The commentator refutes a misunderstanding by some that the dispute was over a married woman; rather it is about a claim of mahr against the property. The passage ends with a separate ruling: claims of usurpation (ghasb) and deposit (wadi'ah) are equal; if both present evidence, the property is split equally.

Key Terms
نكاح (nikah): In this passage, 'nikah' is used to mean 'mahr' (dowry), i.e., the woman's claim for her marriage gift against the property of her husband.
قسمة (qismah): Division; whether a property can be physically divided among co-owners without harm.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful possession of another's property without force.
إيداع (ida'ah): Deposit; entrusting property to another for safekeeping without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The wife's claim for maintenance
C) The dowry (mahr) claim
D) The husband's claim for obedience

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi claim law, 'nikah' in this context means mahr (dowry), not the marriage itself; and indivisible property is split equally when evidence is equal, with compensation adjustments.

Pledge vs Gift: Priority Based on Istihsan
Contracts of pledge vs gift: priority based on guarantee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the priority between a pledge (rahn) and a gift (hibah) when both are claimed with possession, and explain the istihsan basis involving guarantee (daman).

Core Idea
A pledge is preferred over a gift because the pledgee guarantees the property, making the contract stronger.

When two parties claim possession of the same property—one as a pledge, the other as a gift—the Hanafi school gives priority to the pledge. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than qiyas (analogy), which would favor the gift. The key distinction lies in the legal nature of the two contracts: a pledge is a guarantor contract (daman), while a gift without compensation is not. Let us examine the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Two claimants: one claims a pledge in possession, the other a gift in possession. The ruling is that the pledge is more deserving (أحق). This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling applies when both present evidence and the gift is without compensation.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas (analogy) would prioritize the gift because it transfers ownership immediately, while a pledge does not. Ownership is generally stronger than mere possession.
This is the argument of someone who follows qiyas without istihsan.
3
Grasp the istihsan departure
Hanafis depart from qiyas using istihsan: the pledged property is guaranteed (مضمون) – the pledgee is liable for its loss. The gift property is not guaranteed. A guarantor contract is stronger and more deserving of protection.
The illa (effective cause) is the element of daman (guarantee).
4
Recognize the qualifications
If the gift is with compensation (iwad), it becomes a sale and takes priority over the pledge because it transfers ownership both in form and meaning. Also, the rule depends on the location of the property: if in a third party's hand, the rule applies; if in one claimant's hand, that claimant is preferred unless the other has an earlier date; if in both hands, they split equally.
Al-Imadi notes that this rule works for sale, gift, and charity, but for pledge it is problematic due to the possibility of common ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرهن أحق من الهبة . قوله (والرهن أحق من الهبة يعني لو ادعى أحدهما رهناً مقبوضاً والآخر هبة وقبضاً وبرهنا فالرهن أولى وهذا استحسان والقياس أن الهبة أولى لأنها تثبت الملك والرهن لا يثبته. ووجه الاستحسان أن المقبوض بحكم الرهن مضمون وبحكم الهبة غير مضمون وعقد الضمان أقوى. أطلق الهبة وهي مقيدة بأن لا عوض فيها فإن كانت بشرط العوض فهي أولى من الرهن لأنها انتهاء، والبيع أولى من الرهن لأنه ضمان يثبت الملك صورة ومعنى والرهن لا يثبته إلا عند الهلاك لا صورة فكذا الهبة بشرط العوض قيد بكون العين في يد ثالث إذ لو كانت في يد أحدهما فإنه أولى إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ الخارج أسبق فهو أولى، ولو كانت في أيديهما يقضى بها بينهما نصفين إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ أحدهما أسبق فيقضى له قال العمادي : هذا في الشراء والهبة والصدقة مستقيم لأن الشيوع الطارئ لا يفسد الهبة والصدقة على ما عليه الفتوى أما في الرهن لا يستقيم لأن معنى
And the pledge is more deserving than the gift. His saying: (The pledge is more deserving than the gift) meaning if one claims a pledge in possession and the other a gift in possession and they present evidence, the pledge is better, and this is istihsan; the qiyas is that the gift is better because it establishes ownership while the pledge does not. The basis of istihsan is that a thing possessed by way of pledge is guaranteed, while by way of gift it is not guaranteed, and a guarantor contract is stronger. The gift was mentioned absolutely, but it is restricted to one without compensation; if it is with compensation, then it is better than the pledge because it is a sale. Sale is better than the pledge because it is a guarantee establishing ownership in form and meaning, while the pledge establishes it only at destruction, not in form; similarly the gift with compensation. It is conditioned that the property is in the hand of a third party; if it is in the hand of one of them, then he is better unless they date and the outsider's date is earlier, then he is better; if it is in both hands, judgment is between them in halves unless they date and one's date is earlier, then judgment is for him. al-Imadi said: This is correct for purchase, gift, and charity because accidental common ownership does not invalidate gift and charity according to the fatwa; but for pledge it is not correct because the meaning ...
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a pledge (رهن) is more deserving than a gift (هبة) when both claimants present evidence of possession. The author first states the ruling, then explains it via istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) would suggest the gift is better because it transfers ownership immediately, while a pledge only provides security. However, istihsan favors the pledge because something held as a pledge is guaranteed (مضمون) — the pledgee is liable for it if damaged — whereas a gift is not guaranteed (the donee is not liable). A contract with guarantee (daman) is stronger. The gift is then qualified: if the gift is with compensation (i.e., a sale), then it is better than the pledge because it is a sale that transfers ownership both in form and meaning. A pledge only transfers ownership in case of loss. The ruling is also conditioned on the property being in the hands of a third party; if in the hand of one claimant, that claimant is preferred except if the other has an earlier date. If both have possession, they split equally. The passage ends with a quote from al-Imadi noting that this rule works for purchase, gift, and charity because accidental common ownership does not invalidate them, but for pledge it is problematic; the text is cut off here.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Pledge; a contract where property is held as security for a debt; the pledgee has possessory right, not ownership.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a gratuitous transfer of ownership without compensation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason (here, guarantee).
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; liability for property; a pledge is a guarantor contract because the pledgee is liable for loss.
عوض (iwad): Compensation; consideration given in exchange, turning a gift into a sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a pledge is preferred over a gratuitous gift because the pledgee's liability (daman) makes the contract stronger, even though the gift transfers ownership.

Priority of Pledge over Gift in Conflicting Claims
Explaining the ruling that pledge takes precedence over gift with istihsan reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pledge is preferred over a gift without compensation, explain the istihsan basis, and identify the exceptions for gifts with compensation and the effect of subsequent co-ownership.

Core Idea
A pledge is stronger than a gift because it involves guarantee, but a gift with compensation is stronger than a pledge.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two parties present conflicting claims over the same property — one as pledgee and one as donee — the pledgee’s claim takes precedence. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than strict analogy (qiyas). The key reason is that a pledge creates a contract of guarantee (daman), whereas a gift without compensation does not. However, this priority is reversed if the gift is given with compensation, making it akin to a sale. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the nuanced differences that arise when the property is divisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
State the matn's ruling: the pledge is more entitled than the gift when both claim possession. Note that this ruling is based on istihsan, not qiyas.
2
Contrast with the Qiyas
Understand the formal analogy (qiyas) would suggest the gift should be stronger because it establishes ownership (tamlik), while a pledge does not. Recognize why the Hanafi school departs from this analogy.
Qiyas would give priority to the gift, but istihsan overrides.
3
Understand the Istihsan Reasoning
The property in the pledgee's hands is guaranteed (mazmun) — the pledgee is liable for loss. A gift, being gratuitous, does not impose such liability. The contract of guarantee is considered stronger.
Damān makes the pledge contract more binding.
4
Note the Exceptions
If the gift is with compensation (e.g., a sale), it becomes a final transfer (intahā) and takes priority over the pledge. Sale itself also takes priority over pledge because it establishes ownership in both form and meaning.
Gift with compensation is like a sale.
5
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Divisibility
When pledge and sale conflict, and the property is divisible, Al-Umadi and the correct view say that subsequent co-ownership does not invalidate a gift/charity, so both share equally. Khaharzadeh held that divisibility invalidates the gift, favoring the sale claimant. The text adopts the correct view that maintains the gift.
Subsequent co-ownership invalidates pledge but not gift/charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (والرهن أحق من الهبة يعني لو ادعى أحدهما رهناً مقبوضاً والآخر هبة وقبضاً وبرهنا فالرهن أولى وهذا استحسان والقياس أن الهبة أولى لأنها تثبت الملك والرهن لا يثبته. ووجه الاستحسان أن المقبوض بحكم الرهن مضمون وبحكم الهبة غير مضمون وعقد الضمان أقوى. أطلق الهبة وهي مقيدة بأن لا عوض فيها فإن كانت بشرط العوض فهي أولى من الرهن لأنها انتهاء، والبيع أولى من الرهن لأنه ضمان يثبت الملك صورة ومعنى والرهن لا يثبته إلا عند الهلاك لا صورة فكذا الهبة بشرط العوض قيد بكون العين في يد ثالث إذ لو كانت في يد أحدهما فإنه أولى إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ الخارج أسبق فهو أولى، ولو كانت في أيديهما يقضى بها بينهما نصفين إلا أن يؤرخا وتاريخ أحدهما أسبق فيقضى له قال العمادي : هذا في الشراء والهبة والصدقة مستقيم لأن الشيوع الطارئ لا يفسد الهبة والصدقة على ما عليه الفتوى أما في الرهن لا يستقيم لأن الشيوع الطارئ يفسد الرهن فينبغي أن يقضى بالكل لمدعي الشراء إذا اجتمع الرهن والشراء لأن الرهن أثبت رهناً فاسداً فلا تقبل بينته فصار كأن مدعي الشراء انفرد بإقامة البينة ولهذا قال شيخ الإسلام خواهر زاده إنه إنما يقضى به بينهما فيما إذا اجتمع الشراء والهبة إذا كان المدعي مما لا يحتمل القسمة كالعبد والدابة، أما إذا كانت شيئاً يحتملها يقضى بالكل لمدعي الشراء قال: لأن الشراء قد استحق النصف على مدعي الهبة واستحقاق نصف الهبة في مشاع يحتمل القسمة يوجب فساد الهبة فلا تقبل بينة مدعي الهبة غير أن الصحيح ما أعلمتك أن الشيوع الطارئ لا يفسد الهبة والصدقة ويفسد الرهن والله أعلم ا هـ .
His saying (The pledge is more entitled than the gift) means if one claimed a pledge in possession and the other claimed a gift in possession and they provided evidence, then the pledge is preferred. This is istihsan, and the analogy is that the gift is preferred because it establishes ownership and the pledge does not. The basis of istihsan is that what is held as a pledge is guaranteed (mal mazmun) while a gift is not guaranteed, and the contract of guarantee is stronger. He mentioned gift absolutely, but it is restricted to a gift without compensation. If it is a gift with compensation (i.e., sale-like), then it is preferred over the pledge because it is a final transfer. And sale is preferred over the pledge because it is a guarantee (daman) that establishes ownership in form and meaning, while the pledge does not establish ownership except in case of destruction, not in form. Similarly, the gift with compensation. This is conditioned on the property being in the hand of a third party. If it is in the hand of one of them, then he is preferred unless they date their evidence and the date of the outsider is earlier, then the outsider is preferred. If it is in both their hands, it is decided between them equally unless they date and one's date is earlier, then it is decided for him. Al-Umadi said: This is sound in sale, gift, and charity because subsequent co-ownership does not invalidate the gift and charity according to the fatwa. As for pledge, it is not sound because subsequent co-ownership invalidates the pledge. Therefore, it should be decided wholly for the claimant of sale when pledge and sale come together, because the pledge established a defective pledge so his evidence is not accepted, so it is as if the claimant of sale alone provided evidence. For this reason, Shaykh al-Islam Khaharzadeh said that it is only decided between them in the case of sale and gift if the claimed item is indivisible like a slave or animal. But if it is divisible, then it is decided wholly for the claimant of sale. He said: Because the sale has proved entitlement to half against the claimant of the gift, and proving entitlement to half of the gift in a divisible common share necessitates the invalidity of the gift, so the evidence of the claimant of the gift is not accepted. However, the correct view is what I informed you: that subsequent co-ownership does not invalidate gift and charity but invalidates pledge. And Allah knows best. End.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'The pledge is more entitled than the gift'. The commentator explains the ruling: if two people both claim the same property, one as a pledgee and the other as a donee, the pledgee's claim prevails by istihsan. The qiyas (formal analogy) would suggest the gift is stronger because it establishes ownership, while a pledge only secures a debt. However, istihsan (juristic preference) gives priority to the pledge because the property in the hands of a pledgee is 'damān' (guaranteed), meaning the pledgee is liable for loss, making the contract stronger. The gift, being gratuitous, is non-guaranteed. The commentator then nuances: this applies only to a gift without compensation. If the gift is with compensation (e.g., a sale), it is stronger than a pledge because it is a final transfer of ownership, and sale is even stronger than pledge. The rules of preference also depend on possession: if the property is in a third party's hands and the claimants have evidence, the pledge prevails. If possession is with one claimant, that person is preferred unless the other has earlier evidence. If both possess, it is divided equally unless one's evidence is dated earlier. The commentator then notes an issue: in the case of pledge vs sale, Al-Umadi argues that subsequent co-ownership invalidates a pledge but not a gift, so if the property is indivisible, the judge should award it entirely to the claimant of sale, as the pledge's evidence is futile. Khaharzadeh agrees for divisible property but says for indivisible it is divided equally. The correct view, however, is that subsequent co-ownership does not invalidate a gift or charity, but does invalidate a pledge.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge, a contract where property is handed over as security for a debt
هبة (hibah): Gift, a gratuitous transfer of ownership
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, departing from qiyas for a stronger reason

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because a pledge contract is guaranteed (damān) while a gift is not
C) Because a gift requires possession
D) Because the pledgee is always the first claimant

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes a pledge over a gift without compensation due to the guarantee element, but this is reversed when the gift involves compensation, and subsequent co-ownership does not invalidate a valid gift.

Precedence by Earlier Date When Two Outsiders Claim from Same Seller
Ruling on earlier date priority with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the earlier date determines priority when two outsiders claim ownership from the same seller, and apply the ruling to scenarios with and without dates.

Core Idea
Earlier purchase date establishes prior ownership, giving priority over a later claimant from the same seller.

When two outsiders both claim ownership from the same transferor, how do we decide who is entitled? The key is the date of the transaction: the earlier date proves prior ownership, so the later claimant could not have received valid title from the same seller. However, Hanafi scholars differed on cases where only one party provides a date. This lesson unpacks the main rule and its intra-madhhab nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claim types
The passage distinguishes two scenarios: (1) both claim ownership with a date ('ala al-mulk wa al-tarikh), and (2) both claim purchase from the same seller ('ala al-shira' min wahid). The ruling may differ between them when only one party dates.
In the first, the evidence is for absolute ownership with a date; in the second, it is specifically a purchase contract.
2
State the general rule
If both outsiders provide dated evidence, the earlier date prevails. The proof (dalil) is rational: the earlier purchaser proves he was the first owner, so the later purchaser could not have received ownership from the seller, who had already transferred it.
This is based on qiyas (analogy) from the principle that ownership cannot be transferred twice from the same source.
3
Examine the case with only one date
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes: in the first claim type (ownership with date), if only one party dates, they are treated as equal (no preference). In the second claim type (purchase), the dated party is preferred. This is the mufta bihi position.
Abu Yusuf holds that the dated party is always preferred; Muhammad holds the opposite in the first type. The author's summary reconciles the views.
4
Apply the ruling to branches
The passage mentions branches: e.g., one claims purchase, the other claims gift and possession. Also, if the claimed item is an animal and its age matches one date, that claimant is preferred due to external corroboration.
These branches show the same principle of priority through evidence strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو برهن الخارجان على الملك والتاريخ أو على الشراء من واحد فالأسبق أحق) لأنه أثبت أنه أول المالكين فلا يتلقى الملك إلا من جهته ولم يتلق الآخر منه . وأطلق الواحد فشمل ذا اليد، وقيده في الهداية بغير ذي اليد وتعقبه الشارحون بأنه لا فائدة فيه فإنه الحكم لا يتفاوت أن يكون دعواهما الشراء من صاحب اليد أو غيره بعد أن يكون البائع واحداً ولا يعلم فيه خلاف .اهـ. ويتأتى التفريع فيها كالتي قبلها من أن أحدهما إذا ادعى شراء والآخر هبة وقبضاً إلى آخره وحاصل المسألتين أن الخارجين ادعيا تلقي الملك من واحد سواء كان ذلك الواحد ذا يد أو غيره قلت إنما قيده به لأنهما لو ادعيا الشراء من ذي اليد فقد تقدمت فلا فائدة في التعميم مع تقدم تلك المسألة. وقيد بالبرهان على التاريخ منهما في الأولى لأنه لو أرخت إحداهما دون الأخرى فهما سواء كما لو لم يؤرخا عنده. وقال أبو يوسف : المؤرخ أولى. وقال محمد : المبهم أولى بخلاف ما إذا أرخت إحداهما فقط في الثانية فإن المؤرخ أولى . والحاصل أنهما إذا لم يؤرخا أو أرخا واستويا فهي بينهما في المسألتين، وإن أرخا وسبق إحداهما فالسابق أولى فيهما، وإن أرخت إحداهما فقط فهي الأحق في الثانية لا في الأولى، وقدمنا أن دعوى الوقف كدعوى الملك المطلق فيقدم الخارج والأسبق تاريخاً. وفي السراج الوهاج : فإن كان المدعي دابة أو أمة فوافق سنها أحد التارخين كان أولى لأن سن الدابة مكذب لأحد البينتين فكان من صدقه أولى
His saying: (If the two outsiders provide evidence of ownership and date, or of purchase from one [seller], then the earlier one is more entitled) because he proves that he is the first owner, so ownership can only be traced from his side and the other did not receive from him. He said 'from one' absolutely, including the possessor, but al-Hidayah restricted it to non-possessor. The commentators criticized this restriction, saying there is no benefit in it because the ruling does not differ whether they claim purchase from the possessor or another as long as the seller is one, and there is no known dispute. End. The branches apply here like the previous one: if one claims purchase and the other claims gift and possession, etc. The summary of the two issues is that the two outsiders claim receipt of ownership from one person, whether that person is the possessor or not. I say: He only restricted it because if they claim purchase from the possessor, that [issue] has already preceded, so there is no benefit in generalization with the precedence of that issue. He restricted with evidence on date from both in the first [issue] because if only one dates, they are equal as if they did not date in his view. Abu Yusuf said: The dated one is preferred. Muhammad said: The ambiguous one is preferred. Contrast to when only one dates in the second [issue], then the dated one is preferred. The summary: if they do not date or date equally, it is between them in both issues. If they date and one is earlier, the earlier is preferred in both. If only one dates, that one is entitled in the second [issue] but not in the first. We have already stated that the claim of endowment is like the claim of absolute ownership, so the outsider with earlier date is preferred. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if the claimed item is an animal or slave-girl, and its age matches one of the dates, that one is preferred because the age of the animal belies one of the two evidences, so the one whose evidence is truthful is preferred.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn line about two outsiders each claiming purchase from the same seller with dates. The ruling: the one with the earlier date is more entitled because he proves he was the first owner, and the later purchaser cannot derive ownership from the seller since the seller had already transferred ownership. The commentator notes that al-Hidayah restricted the seller to being a non-possessor, but other scholars say there is no difference. The important points: (1) If neither dates or both date equally, the property is divided between them. (2) If one dates earlier, he wins. (3) If only one provides a date, then according to the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, in the first issue (claim of ownership with date) they are equal, but in the second issue (claim of purchase) the dated one is preferred. There is a difference: Abu Yusuf says the dated one is always preferred, Muhammad says the ambiguous (undated) one is preferred in the first issue? The text is a bit confusing. The summary is given. Additionally, a note from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if the claimed property is an animal and its age matches one of the dates, that claimant is preferred because the age provides external corroboration.

Key Terms
خارج (kharaj): A person who is not in possession (outsider) as opposed to the current possessor
تاريخ (tarikh): Date of the transaction recorded in evidence

Equal Claims from Different Sellers with Dates
Ruling with critical refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two outsiders claim purchase from different sellers with dates and explain the critical refinement about the nature of co-ownership.

Core Idea
Two outsiders claiming purchase from different sellers with dates are equal—each proves his seller's ownership.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key rule in property disputes: if two outsiders both claim to have bought the same property from different sellers, and both present evidence with dates, they are deemed equal. The reasoning is that each sale is valid against its own seller, so the sellers become co-owners. The commentary then corrects a previous view about the type of co-ownership, which affects contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two outsiders each claim to have purchased the same property from different sellers, and both provide evidence with dates.
The sellers are different and neither is the current possessor.
2
State the ruling
The claims are considered equal. Neither takes priority over the other.
The equality is based on the strength of evidence from both sides.
3
Explain the reason
Each buyer proves his seller's ownership at the time of sale, so both sellers are deemed co-owners. The conflict is between two valid ownership claims.
Effectively, it is as if both sellers are present and have equal ownership.
4
Examine the critical refinement
Al-Umadi described this as 'subsequent co-ownership' (shuyuʿ ṭāriʾ). The correct view is 'concomitant co-ownership' (shuyuʿ muqārin) which invalidates the contract because the co-ownership existed at the time of sale.
This distinction matters for the validity of the sale: concomitant co-ownership makes the sale of the entire property void, whereas subsequent co-ownership does not.
5
Apply the refinement to the ruling
Since the co-ownership is concomitant, the sale from each seller of the whole property is invalid because each seller did not own the entire property at the time of sale. However, the claims are still treated as equal in terms of evidence.
The practical effect is that the buyers do not acquire full ownership; instead, they have a claim to share the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعلى الشراء من آخر وذكرا تاريخاً استويا) أي برهن كل واحد منهما على الشراء من آخر وذكر تاريخاً فهما سواء لأنهما يثبتان الملك لبائعهما فيصير كأنهما حضرا. أطلق في قوله وذكرا تاريخاً» فشمل ما إذا استوى قوله : (غير أن الصحيح ما أعلمتك قال الرملي قال الغزي هذا الكلام من العمادي يشير إلى أن الاستحقاق من قبيل الشيوع الطارئ وليس كذلك، بل هو من الشيوع المقارن المفسد كما صرح به في جامع الفصولين وصححه في شرح الدرر والغرر وقد نقله المصنف في كتابه هذا من كتاب الهبة وأقره اهـ . قلت : وقدمنا عبارة الغزي في كتابه المنح قبل ورقة قوله : قلت إنما قيد به الخ) يمكن أن يقال إنه كان الأولى حينئذ حذف تلك المسألة والاستغناء عنها بهذه روماً للاختصار بتعميم ليشمل ذا اليد وغيره ولذا قال في العناية : قوله من واحد أي من غير ذي اليد ليس فيه زيادة فائدة
His saying: (And if the purchase is from another, and they mentioned a date, they are equal) i.e., each of them provides evidence of purchase from another and mentions a date, so they are equal because they prove ownership for their respective sellers, so it becomes as if both sellers are present. He said 'and they mentioned a date' absolutely, including when they are equal. His saying: (However, the correct view is what I informed you: al-Ramli said, al-Ghazzi said: This statement of al-Umadi indicates that the entitlement is of the type of subsequent co-ownership, but it is not so; rather it is of the type of concomitant co-ownership that is invalidating, as clarified in Jami' al-Fusulayn and corrected in Sharh al-Durar wa al-Ghurar. The author (of al-Bahr) has transmitted it in this book from the Book of Gift and approved it. End. I say: We have already transmitted the statement of al-Ghazzi in his book al-Minah before a leaf. His saying: I say, he only restricted it with that, etc.) It can be said that it would have been better then to delete that issue and dispense with it for the sake of brevity by generalizing to include the possessor and others, and therefore al-Inayah said: 'His saying: from one, i.e., from non-possessor, there is no added benefit.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains a matn ruling: if two outsiders both claim to have purchased the same property from different sellers, and both provide evidence with dates, they are considered equal. The reason is that each proves that his seller owned the property at the time of sale, so effectively both sellers appear to be owners. The judge then faces a conflict of equal proof. The commentary then includes a critical refinement: al-Umadi's earlier statement about entitlement being 'subsequent co-ownership' is incorrect; it is actually 'concomitant co-ownership' which invalidates certain contracts. This refinement is from al-Ramli and al-Ghazzi, and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq approves it. The author then comments that it would have been better to generalize the earlier ruling to include the possessor, as al-Hidayah's restriction to non-possessor adds no benefit, as al-Inayah noted.

Key Terms
شُيُوع طارئ (shuyuʿ ṭāriʾ): Subsequent co-ownership: arises after the sale, meaning the sellers were full owners at sale time.
شُيُوع مُقارِن (shuyuʿ muqārin): Concomitant co-ownership: existed at the time of sale, so each seller owned only a share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two outsiders each claim purchase from different sellers and both provide dates, what is the ruling?
A) The earlier date wins
B) They are equal
C) The later date wins
D) The one with better evidence wins

Key Takeaway
When two outsiders claim purchase from different sellers with dates, their claims are equal; the co-ownership is concomitant, not subsequent, which invalidates the sales but preserves equality of claims.

Priority of Dated Proofs When Claimants Derive from Different Transferors
Intra-Hanafi dispute and correction on dated proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on priority of dated proofs when claimants derive from different transferors, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and explain the reasoning behind the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Even when sellers differ, the earlier date on a proof gives priority because it indicates earlier acquisition.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author corrects a previous commentator's claim that when transferors are multiple, the earlier date does not give priority. He affirms that the correct Hanafi position, supported by al-Hidayah, is that the earlier date prevails regardless of whether the sellers are the same or different. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi disagreement and the reasoning behind the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed rule
Two claimants each produce a dated proof of purchase, but from different sellers. The key question: does the earlier date determine priority, or is the property divided equally?
Note the distinction from the case where the seller is the same.
2
State the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa, the later opinion of Abu Yusuf, and one narration of Muhammad hold that the earlier date gives priority. The first opinion of Abu Yusuf holds for equal division. This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The mufta bihi is the earlier-date priority as per later authorities.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each side
The first Abu Yusuf view analogizes to two sellers claiming absolute ownership without dates, which results in equal division. The majority view argues that the date is attached to the claimant's proof of acquisition, not the seller's claim. Earlier date implies earlier valid acquisition, and later possession must derive from that earlier owner unless proven otherwise.
The majority view treats the date as a decisive quality of the proof, not mere context.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical consequence: under the preferred view, the earlier-dated claimant takes the entire property; under the first opinion, they split half and half. The mufta bihi is the earlier-date priority, as affirmed by al-Hidayah and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
This ruling applies even when the holder is not the same as the earlier-dated claimant.
