The Nature of Compromise in Debt: Ibra' vs Mu'awada
Defines sulh in debt and provides examples distinguishing ibra' from mu'awada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any compromise in debt as ibra' or mu'awada and determine its validity based on the Hanafi principle.

Core Idea
Compromise in debt is default ibra' (release); only when the settlement exceeds the original right does it become mu'awada requiring sale conditions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the foundational classification of compromise in debt (sulh fi al-dayn). The author distinguishes between two types: ibra' (release) where the creditor takes less than his due and drops the rest, and mu'awada (exchange) where the settlement includes something beyond the original claim, such as earlier payment or different type. Understanding this classification is crucial because ibra' is valid without the strict conditions of sale, while mu'awada must meet all requirements of a valid exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Compromise in debt is a valid transaction. The Hanafi school holds it to be sahih (valid).
2
Locate the defining principle
The text states: 'أخذ لبعض حقه وإسقاط الباقي لا معاوضة' – it is taking some of the right and dropping the rest, not an exchange.
3
Distinguish ibra' from mu'awada
Apply the principle: if the compromise is for less than the original right (in amount, quality, or type), it is ibra'. If it includes an excess (like acceleration of payment or change of genus), it is mu'awada.
4
Work through the examples
The text provides many examples of valid and invalid compromises. Practice classifying each according to the principle.
5
Note the cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools classify most compromises as sales; the Hanafi position is based on the nature of debt sulh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح باب الصلح في الدين لم يضمنه بل يلزم الموكل وإن صالح عنه بلا أمر صح إن ضمن المال أو أضاف إلى ماله أو قال على ألف وسلم وإلا توقف فإن أجازه المدعى عليه جاز وإلا بطل.
باب الصلح في الدين
الصلح عما استحق بعقد المداينة أخذ لبعض حقه وإسقاط الباقي لا معاوضة فلو مقراً للشريك بالطريق الأولى. وإن كان المغصوب مكيلاً أو موزوناً فصالحه أحدهما على غير جنسه وهو غائب شاركه الآخر كالمستهلك، وإن كان حاضراً مقراً به لا أو منكراً لا . ولو ادعيا أن هذه الدار ميراث أبيهما فصالح رب الدار أحدهما لم يشاركه الآخر سواء كان المصالح منكراً أو مقراً ومن وكل رجلاً بالصلح عنه فصالح لم يلزم الوكيل ما صلح عليه) والمال لازم للموكل إذا كان عن دم عمد أو على بعض ما يدعيه من الدين ولو بعد الإقرار إلا أن يضيفه الوكيل، أو كان عن مال بمال على إقرار وعلى إنكار لا يلزمه مطلقاً. والأمر بالصلح أمر بالضمان فله الرجوع عليه إن أدي بغير أمره كالخلع بخلاف الأمر بالنكاح لصحتهما من الأجنبي بلا أمر بخلافه وهو على أوجه إن صالح بمال وضمنه ثم وهو متبرع لا شيء له من المصالح عنه بل هو للذي في يده مقراً كان أو منكراً إلا إذا كان عن عين والمدعى عليه مقر فهي للمصالح، وكذا إن صالحه على مال نفسه كألفي هذا وعبدي صح ولزمه التسليم، وكذا لو قال صالح فلاناً على ألف وسلمها وإن لم يسلمها فهو موقوف إن أجازه المدعى عليه جاز ولزمه الألف وإلا بطل إلا إذا قال صالحني ففرق بينه وبين صالح فلاناً. والخامس يقول صالح فلاناً على هذه الألف أو على هذا العبد من غير نسبة له فهو كالإضافة إلى نفسه وفي صالحتك على «ألف اختلف المشايخ منهم من جعله موقوفاً، ومنهم من جعله نافذاً، والأول أولى. ولو استحق العوض في الوجوه كلها أو وجده زيوفاً أو ستوقة لم يرجع على المصالح ويرجع بالدعوى إلا إذا ضمن المصالح ا م
والله أعلم.
باب الصلح في الدين
وكل شيء وقع عليه (الصلح) وهو من جنس ما يستحقه المدعي على المدعى عليه (بعقد المداينة) أي البيع بالدين لم يحمل على المعاوضة وإنما هو (أخذ لبعض حقه وإسقاط للباقي فلو صالح عن ألف على نصفه أو على ألف مؤجل أو خمسمائة مؤجلة أو جياد على خمسمائة زيوف حالة أو مؤجلة أو عن ألف مؤجلة على ألف حالة مقبوضة أو عن ألف درهم ومائة دينار على مائة حالة أو ألف مؤجلة بدل الكتابة على خمسمائة حالة (جاز) وعن ألف مؤجلة على مؤجلة أو عن ألف سود على ألف بيض مقبوضة أو عن ألف خمسمائة حالة في غير المكاتب أو عن ألف درهم على دنانير مؤجلة أو خمسمائة بيض أو عن ألف على طعام موصوف في الذمة مؤجل أو غير مؤجل غير مقبوض لم يجز. والأصل أنه متى كان الذي وقع عليه الصلح أدون من حقه قدراً أو وصفاً أو في أنه إحداهما فهو إسقاط للبعض واستيفاء للباقي، وإن كان أزيد منه بمعنى دخل فيه ما لا يستحق من وصف أو ما هو بمعنى الوصف كتعجيل المؤجل وعن اختلاف الجنس فهو معاوضة. ويجوز الصلح بدراهم عن دراهم مجهولة في الذمة ومن له على آخر ألف فقال أد غداً نصفه على أنك بريء من الفضل ففعل برئ وإلا لا وكذا لو قال وأنت بريء من الزيادة على أنك إن لم تدفعها إلي غداً فلا تبرأ عن الباقي، ولو قال أبرأتك كذا على أن عن تعطيني كذا فإنه يبرأ وإن لم يؤد غداً، وكذا لو قال أد إلي كذا على أنك بريء من باقيه ولم يوقت، ولو قال إن أديت إلي خمسمائة أو إذا أديت أو متى أديت فأنت بريء من الباقي لم مطلقاً صحة تعليق البراءة بصريح الشرط بخلاف ما إذا كان بمعناه (ومن قال لعدم لآخر لا أقر لك بمالك حتى تؤخره عني أو تحط بعضه ففعل صح) إن قال ذلك سراً وإن قال علانية يؤخذ به ولو ادعى ألفاً فجحده فقال اقرر لي بها على أن أحط منها مائة أو على أن حططت منها مائة فأقر جاز بخلاف قوله على أن أعطيك مائة لأن الإقرار لا يستحق به البدل، ولو قال إن أقررت لي حططت منها مائة فأقر صح الإقرار لا الحط؛ كذا في المجتبى والله أعلم.
Book of Compromise, Chapter of Compromise in Debt. He (the agent) does not guarantee it, but rather it binds the principal. If he compromises without permission, it is valid if he guarantees the money, or adds it to his own wealth, or says 'for one thousand and delivers', otherwise it is suspended. If the defendant accepts, it is valid; otherwise void. Chapter of Compromise in Debt: Compromise regarding what is due by a debt contract is taking some of his right and dropping the rest, not an exchange. So if... (continues with examples). And the principle is that when the compromise is for less than his right in quantity, quality, or type, it is dropping of some and collection of the rest. If it is more in the sense of including something not owed, like acceleration of a deferred debt or change of type, it is an exchange. And compromise with dirhams for unknown dirhams in the debt is permissible. And if someone has a thousand owed to him and says 'Pay half tomorrow on condition that you are free from the excess', and he does, he becomes free; otherwise not. And similarly if he says 'I release you from such-and-such on condition that you give me such-and-such', he is released even if he does not pay tomorrow. And if he says 'Pay me such-and-such on condition that you are free from the rest' without specifying a time, it is valid. And if he says 'If you pay me five hundred...' or 'When you pay...' or 'Whenever you pay... then you are free from the rest', it is not valid absolutely due to the conditional release being suspended by an explicit condition, unlike when it is implied. And if someone says to another 'I will not acknowledge your right until you defer it from me or reduce some of it', and he does, it is valid if said secretly; if said publicly, it is acted upon. And if someone claims a thousand and the other denies, then says 'Acknowledge for me that I reduce a hundred' or 'that I have reduced a hundred', and he acknowledges, it is valid, unlike saying 'that I give you a hundred' because acknowledgement does not entitle a substitute. And if he says 'If you acknowledge for me, I will reduce a hundred', and he acknowledges, the acknowledgement is valid but not the reduction. Thus in al-Mujtaba, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the chapter of compromise in debt (sulh fi al-dayn). The author first restates the matn: if an agent makes a compromise without the principal's permission, it is binding only if the agent guarantees the payment or designates it from his own wealth; otherwise it is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance. Then he defines the nature of such compromise: it is not a sale but a taking of some of one's right and dropping the rest. This classification is crucial because it determines the rules of validity. The commentator then provides numerous examples to illustrate when a compromise is considered an ibra' (release) and when it becomes a mu'awada (exchange). The key principle is: if the settlement amount is less than the original debt in any way (amount, quality, or type), it is ibra' and valid; if it includes something extra not owed (like earlier payment of a deferred debt), it is mu'awada and must follow exchange rules. The passage also covers special cases: shared debt, agency, conditional releases, and acknowledgements with reductions. The commentary is technical and expects the reader to know the basic fiqh of sulh. It repeatedly distinguishes between valid and invalid forms, emphasizing that the default in debt compromise is ibra' unless there is an element of excess or change of genus.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract of compromise to end a dispute, here specifically in debt: taking a portion of one's right and dropping the rest.
الإسقاط (al-isqat): Dropping or releasing a right, which is the nature of sulh when the settlement amount is less than the original claim.
المعاوضة (al-mu'awada): An exchange of property for property, which applies to sulh when the settlement includes something not originally owed (e.g., earlier payment or different type).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default nature of compromise in debt (sulh fi al-dayn)?
A) It is an exchange (mu'awada) like a sale.
B) It is a release (ibra') of part of the debt in exchange for immediate payment of the remainder.
C) It is a gift (hiba) requiring acceptance by the debtor.
D) It is a loan (qard) that must be repaid later.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compromise in debt is default ibra'; only when the settlement includes an excess or different genus does it become mu'awada requiring sale conditions.

Agency and Conditional Releases in Sulh
Explains rulings on agency in sulh and conditional releases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on agency in compromise and distinguish valid from invalid conditional releases.

Core Idea
An agent's compromise without guarantee is suspended; conditional releases are invalid if phrased as explicit conditions but valid if implied as part of an ibra'.

This passage addresses two practical scenarios: (1) when an agent makes a compromise without the principal's permission, and (2) various phrasings of conditional release. The author explains that an agent's act binds the principal only if the agent guarantees the payment or designates his own wealth; otherwise it is suspended pending the defendant's acceptance. For conditional releases, the key is whether the release is truly conditional or merely a statement of ibra' with a motivating condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the agency rule
An agent's compromise without permission is valid only if he guarantees the payment; otherwise it is suspended.
If the agent says 'I compromise and I guarantee it', it binds the principal.
2
Apply to scenarios
If the agent says 'I compromise without guarantee', the compromise is suspended; it becomes valid only if the defendant accepts.
3
Identify the conditional release forms
The text examines phrasings like 'Pay half tomorrow on condition that you are free from the excess', 'I release you on condition that you give me something', etc.
4
Determine validity of conditional releases
Releases with explicit conditions (if/then) are invalid because they suspend the release. Statements that imply a motive (e.g., 'Pay me this and you are free') are valid.
5
Apply to acknowledgements
E.g., 'Acknowledge that I reduce part of the debt' is valid, but 'Acknowledge that I give you something' is not because acknowledgement does not entitle a substitute.

Source Text

Which of the following conditional release statements is INVALID according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) 'Pay me 500 now and you are free from the rest.'

Compromise on Shared Debt: Rights of the Non-Compromising Partner
Ruling on shared debt compromise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one partner compromises his share of a joint debt for a specific item, and identify the rights of the other partner.

Core Idea
When one partner compromises his share for an item, the other partner may claim half of that item from the debtor.

This lesson examines a scenario where two creditors are owed a debt jointly. If one creditor accepts a compromise (sulh) from the debtor in the form of a specific item, what are the consequences for the other creditor? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the non-compromising partner retains the right to pursue the debtor for his share of that item, rather than the compromising partner. This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal principle and its applications, including that the compromising partner has no option to rescind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Scenario
Two people are jointly owed a debt. One of them compromises his share (e.g., half the debt) for a specific item (e.g., a garment) from the debtor.
The compromise is a sulh transaction.
2
State the Ruling for the Other Partner
The other partner (sharik) has the right to follow the debtor for half of that item. He does not need to get the item from the compromising partner.
The text says: 'لشريكه أن يتبع المديون بنصفه'.
3
Explain the Principle Behind the Ruling
The compromise is essentially a partial remission (ibra) and collection of the remainder, not a sale. Therefore, the compromising partner has no khiyar (option) because he has simply received part of his debt.
The text says: 'ولا خيار للمصالح لأنه كقبض بعض الدين'.
4
Understand Conditions for Validity
The compromise must be on a known item (ma'lum). If the compromise involves a change in description or timing (e.g., black dirhams for white, or accelerating payment), it becomes a mu'awada (exchange) and requires additional conditions. The text lists examples of invalid compromises.
The principle: if the compromise is less in amount or description, it is ibra; if it includes extra benefits, it is exchange.

فصل في الدين المشترك دين بينهما صالح أحدهما عن نصيبه على ثوب لشريكه أن يتبع المديون بنصفه أو عن درهم سود على ألف بيض مقبوضة أو ألف درهم ومائة دينار على مائة حالة أو ألف مؤجلة بدل الكتابة على خمسمائة حالة (جاز) وعن ألف مؤجلة على مؤجلة أو عن ألف سود على ألف بيض مقبوضة أو عن ألف خمسمائة حالة في غير المكاتب أو عن ألف درهم على دنانير مؤجلة أو خمسمائة بيض أو عن ألف على طعام موصوف في الذمة مؤجل أو غير مؤجل غير مقبوض لم يجز. والأصل أنه متى كان الذي وقع عليه الصلح أدون من حقه قدراً أو وصفاً أو في أنه إحداهما فهو إسقاط للبعض واستيفاء للباقي، وإن كان أزيد منه بمعنى دخل فيه ما لا يستحق من وصف أو ما هو بمعنى الوصف كتعجيل المؤجل وعن اختلاف الجنس فهو معاوضة. ويجوز الصلح بدراهم عن دراهم مجهولة في الذمة ومن له على آخر ألف فقال أد غداً نصفه على أنك بريء من الفضل ففعل برئ وإلا لا وكذا لو قال وأنت بريء من الزيادة على أنك إن لم تدفعها إلي غداً فلا تبرأ عن الباقي، ولو قال أبرأتك كذا على أن عن تعطيني كذا فإنه يبرأ وإن لم يؤد غداً، وكذا لو قال أد إلي كذا على أنك بريء من باقيه ولم يوقت، ولو قال إن أديت إلي خمسمائة أو إذا أديت أو متى أديت فأنت بريء من الباقي لم مطلقاً صحة تعليق البراءة بصريح الشرط بخلاف ما إذا كان بمعناه (ومن قال لعدم لآخر لا أقر لك بمالك حتى تؤخره عني أو تحط بعضه ففعل صح) إن قال ذلك سراً وإن قال علانية يؤخذ به ولو ادعى ألفاً فجحده فقال اقرر لي بها على أن أحط منها مائة أو على أن حططت منها مائة فأقر جاز بخلاف قوله على أن أعطيك مائة لأن الإقرار لا يستحق به البدل، ولو قال إن أقررت لي حططت منها مائة فأقر صح الإقرار لا الحط؛ كذا في المجتبى والله أعلم.
قوله : (وإن صالحه على جنسه خير الشريك الخ ) ولا خيار للمصالح لأنه كقبض بعض الدين
Section on shared debt: A debt between two people. One of them compromises his share for a garment: the partner may follow the debtor for half of it. Or for black dirhams against white dirhams... (examples). And the principle is... And his saying: 'If he compromises with him for its kind, the partner has an option...' but there is no option for the one who compromised because it is like receiving part of the debt.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the scenario where a debt is owed jointly to two creditors. If one creditor makes a compromise with the debtor on his share for a specific item (like a garment), the other creditor has the right to claim from the debtor half of that item. The text then repeats some examples from the previous section. The key point at the end: the partner who made the compromise has no khiyar (option) because he is considered to have simply received part of the debt, not undergone an exchange. This is consistent with the Hanafi view that compromise in debt is essentially collection of a portion of one's right, not a sale.

Key Terms
الشريك (al-sharik): The other partner in the shared debt, who has not made the compromise.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option to rescind the compromise; not available to the compromising partner here.
الصُّلْح (al-sulh): Compromise or settlement; in this context, a creditor accepting less than the full debt in satisfaction.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Remission or release of a debt; here it applies when the compromise is for less or equal without extra benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two people are owed a debt jointly, and one partner compromises his share for a specific item from the debtor, what right does the other partner have?
A) He may demand the full item from the compromising partner.
B) He may follow the debtor for half of that item.
C) He has no claim because the compromise settled the debt.
D) He must accept the remaining cash debt from the debtor.

Key Takeaway
When one creditor compromises his share of a joint debt for a specific item, the other creditor retains the right to claim his share of that item from the debtor, not from the compromising partner.

Rulings on Settlement Among Joint Creditors and Heirs
Compilation of Hanafi rulings on sulh for debts and among heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on settlement among joint creditors and distinguish between ibra and mubadala in heirs' settlement.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, settlement among joint creditors gives the non-settling partner a choice, and settlement among heirs is treated as exchange, not discharge.

Settlement (sulh) is a versatile contract. When multiple creditors share a claim, a settlement by one affects the others. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the options and liabilities. It also explains the key nuance in heirs' settlements: whether it is an exchange (sale) or a discharge, which determines its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of settlement
Determine if the settlement involves joint creditors (multiple claimants on a single debt) or heirs (distributing an estate). Each has different rules.
2
Apply the partner's choice rule
For joint creditors, note the options given to the non-settling partner: either pursue the debtor for his share or pursue the settling partner. Then note the settling partner's options upon being pursued.
The settling partner may either give the non-settling partner a share of what he received or guarantee a portion.
3
Apply the ibra vs mubadala distinction
For heirs' settlements, identify whether the transaction is a discharge (ibra) or an exchange (mubadala). The text clarifies that ibra from a specific asset is ineffective for ownership transfer, but mubadala is valid as a sale.
The relied-upon view treats heirs' settlements as exchange, not ibra.
4
Derive the consequences
Based on the choice of action, determine the legal consequences: if the non-settling partner opts to pursue the debtor and the debtor becomes insolvent, the settling partner's liability is triggered. For heirs, if the settlement is ibra from ayn, the other heirs' claims are not affected.

Book of Settlement, Chapter on Settlement in Debt: He takes half the garment from his partner unless he guarantees him a quarter of the debt. If he takes possession of his share, the partner shares in it, and both claim the remainder from the debtor. If he buys something with his share, he guarantees a quarter of the debt. The settlement of one of the two parties to a salam contract from his share for what was paid is void. And if the heirs bring out one of them regarding a commodity or real estate for money, or gold for silver, or vice versa, it is valid, little or much. From cash and other than them with one of the two currencies, detail: price or value of a joint consumable asset, or substitute of a loan, or an inherited debt; one of them settles regarding his share. If it is not of the same type as the debt, the partner has the choice: if he wishes, he can pursue the debtor for his share, or pursue his partner. If he chooses to pursue his partner, the settling party has the choice: if he wishes, he can give him his share from what was settled for, and if he wishes, he can guarantee him a quarter of the debt. There is no difference whether the settlement is based on acknowledgment, silence, or denial. The stratagem to prevent the partner from recourse is that the debtor gifts him the amount of his debt and then discharges him from his debt, or the claimant sells him a small item worth his share, then discharges him from the debt and takes the price of the sold item. And if he settles with him on the same type, the partner has the choice: if he wishes, he can pursue the debtor or participate with him. Then both claim the remainder from the debtor as if he had taken possession. So if he chooses to pursue the debtor and then his share is lost, such as the debtor dies insolvent, the receiver (of the settlement) is liable for half of what he received, even if from someone else. And if he buys something with his share, he guarantees a quarter of the debt if he wishes, like hiring with his share. And the occurrence of a debt owed by the pursued party to one of them such that they meet as a setoff (qisas) is like possession, like his marriage to the female debtor for divorced dirhams, and like one of them usurping an asset from the debtor and it perishes in his possession, or a corrupt purchase similarly. Conversely, a prior debt owed by one of them before the joint claim becomes due, when it becomes a setoff, is not possession, like his marriage to the female debtor for his share, and like one of them destroying the debtor's goods and settling for deliberate injury. And like one of them discharging (the debtor) from his share or part of it and they divide the remainder according to their shares — this is not possession, so he does not guarantee anything to his partner. And if one of them grants a deferment: if it was not obligatory by a contract of each of them, such as they inherit a deferred debt, the deferment is void. If it was obligatory by a loan by one of them: if they are partners in an 'inan company, then if the one who managed the loan grants the deferment, his deferment is valid regarding the entire debt; if the one who did not manage it grants the deferment, it is not valid even regarding his own share. If they are partners in a mufawada company and one of them grants a deferment, whichever one grants it, his deferment is valid like the deferment of an agent authorized to sell the price. And if one of them reduces (the debt): if he was a contracting party, his reduction is valid partially or entirely, and he guarantees his partner's share if he reduces the entire debt. And if one of them settles for a specific asset that he exclusively possesses — even if he was not a contracting party — it is permissible regarding his share, not regarding his partner's share. This is in Fatawa Qadikhan. And if one of the two parties to a salam contract settles regarding the jointly owned... as in his statement: (and even if from someone else) al-Ramli said: i.e., other than what he took possession of. I say: al-Zayla'i's expression is: he has recourse against him as in hawala, but he cannot have recourse against the very dirhams he received because his right in them has fallen by delivery, so his right does not return to them by the loss, and it reverts to his liability in their like. Then he said: Chapter on Settlement in Debt: except when what is given is more than his share. And if in the estate there is a debt owed by people, and they bring it out so that the debt becomes for them, it is void. And if they stipulate that the debtors are discharged from it, it is valid. And if the deceased has a debt that encompasses the estate, the settlement and division are void. Between them is a special partnership regarding his share based on what he paid from the capital — it is suspended upon the permission of his partner. If he rejects, it is void entirely and the subject of salam remains as is. If he permits, it is binding on both, so half the capital is between them and the rest of the food is between them, whether the capital was mixed or not. And if they are mufawada partners, it is permissible even regarding the whole. And if 'inan, it is suspended also if it is not from their trade. And Allah knows best. Section on Settlement of Heirs: (And if the heirs bring out one of them regarding a commodity or real estate for money, or gold for silver, or vice versa, it is valid, little or much, based on exchange (mubadala), not discharge (ibra'), because settlement regarding specific goods is void. Thus the commentators have stated absolutely, but what the expressions of the well-known books give is detail: if the discharge (ibra') regarding them is by way of creation (insha'), then it is either regarding the specific asset or regarding the claim. If it is regarding the asset, it is void from the perspective that he can claim it against the addressee and others, but valid from the perspective of discharging from the description of guarantee. That is why it is said in al-Dhakhira: they said: If a man has a slave in his hand and another man says to him, 'I am free of him,' he becomes free of him. And if he says to him, 'I declare you free of him,' he can still claim him; he only discharged him from his guarantee. And if it is regarding the claim, if it is in a specific way, such as he discharges him from the claim to this specific asset, then his claim is not heard regarding the addressee, but it is heard regarding others. That is why al-Walwalji said in his Fatawa, before the Book of Acknowledgment: A man claimed a house or a slave from a man, then the claimant said to the defendant, 'I discharged you from the house,' or 'from my litigation regarding this house,' or 'from a claim regarding this house' — all this is void, even if he claims that, it is heard. That is, it is valid if he claims it against someone other than the addressee, based on the subsequent reasoning. The essence is that if the discharge is in a specific way...
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary clarifying numerous rulings on settlement (sulh) in the Hanafi school. The chapter first discusses the case of joint creditors: when one creditor settles with a common debtor for a specific item or partial amount, various options arise for the other creditor. The core principle is that settlement is valid but the non-settling partner has a choice to either pursue his share from the debtor or from his partner (the settling creditor). The settling creditor then either hands over the goods received or guarantees a portion. The passage is rich in detail, covering scenarios where the settlement is on the same type of debt or a different type, loss of property, setoff, deferral, reduction, and specific cases involving salam (advance sale). It also discusses stratagems (hiyal) to avoid complications. The latter part transitions to settlement among heirs: here the key distinction is whether the transaction is a true exchange (mubadala) or a discharge (ibra'). According to the relied-upon view, settlement among heirs is valid as a sale or exchange, but ibra from a specific asset (ayn) is invalid in the sense of transferring ownership, though it can waive the right to claim compensation. The text cites al-Dhakhira and al-Walwalji to elaborate this distinction. The commentator emphasizes that the ruling depends on whether the ibra concerns the asset itself or the right to claim.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract between parties to settle a dispute or claim, often by giving up part of the right in exchange for immediate benefit.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): A unilateral discharge or release from a right or claim.
المبادلة (al-mubadala): An exchange or barter, considered a type of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if one creditor settles with a common debtor for an item of a different type than the debt, what options does the other creditor have?
A) He can only pursue the debtor for his full share.
B) He can either pursue the debtor for his share or hold the settling creditor liable for half the debt.
C) He can either pursue the debtor for his share or hold the settling creditor liable for a quarter of the debt.
D) He has no options and must accept the settlement.

Key Takeaway
Settlement among joint creditors creates a choice for non-settling parties, and settlement among heirs is valid as exchange but not as discharge of a specific asset.

Ibra' from Specific Assets: Liability vs. Ownership in Hanafi Fiqh
This passage analyzes the validity of discharging someone from specific assets or lawsuits, distinguishing between ibra' from the asset itself and from the claim to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ibra' from an asset (al-'ayn) and ibra' from a claim (al-da'wa), and state the Hanafi ruling on each, including the effect on liability and ownership.

Core Idea
Ibra' from an asset releases liability but does not transfer ownership; ibra' from a claim bars suit against the addressee.

When someone says "I discharge you from this specific thing," what is the legal effect? Does it mean you now own it? Or that you are no longer liable for it? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer by separating the asset itself from the right to claim it. This lesson unpacks Ibn Nujaym's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, clarifying when ibra' is valid and what it actually accomplishes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of ibra'
First, determine whether the discharge is by way of creation (insha') or information (ikhbar). Insha' creates new legal effects (e.g., 'I discharge you'), while ikhbar informs about an existing state (e.g., 'He is free of any right I have'). Focus on insha'.
The passage states that ikhbar is valid and covers both debts and assets, barring all claims.
2
Distinguish between ibra' from the asset ('ayn) and ibra' from the claim (da'wa)
If the insha' is directed at the asset itself (e.g., 'I discharge you from this slave'), it is invalid for transferring ownership but valid for dropping liability. If directed at the claim (e.g., 'I discharge you from my claim to this house'), further subdivision applies.
The key distinction: ibra' from 'ayn never transfers ownership; ibra' from da'wa only bars suit against the addressee if particular.
3
Analyze ibra' from the claim (da'wa): particular vs. general
If the ibra' is particular (khosus) — specifying a particular claim — the claimant cannot sue the addressee for that asset, but can sue others. If general (ta'mim) — e.g., 'all claims' — it is understood to cover only debts, not assets, so the claimant can still sue the addressee for the asset.
This is based on the example in al-Qunyah regarding divorcing spouses: general ibra' does not include standing assets.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar
Zufar held that ibra' from a usurped asset is invalid even for liability (daman). The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that it is valid for releasing liability but not for transferring ownership. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under majority, if the asset is destroyed, the recipient is not liable for its value; under Zufar, he remains liable.

فلا تسمع الدعوى، وكذا إذا قال لا ملك لي في هذه العين ذكره في المبسوط والمحيط. فعلم أن قوله لا أستحق قبله حقاً مطلقاً ولا أيضاً أي عن دعوى عين مخصوصة، فإن أضاف البراءة عن العين إلى المخاطب لا تسمع العين المخصوصة . ينبغي أن إبراؤه عنها في صيغة التعميم إذ لا فرق يظهر بل قد يدعي الأولوية في التعميم كيف وهو مخالف لقولهم الإبراء عن دعوى الأعيان. صحيح بخلاف الإبراء عن الأعيان نفسها. وفي القنية : لو Chapter on Settlement of Heirs: (If the heirs give one of them property or real estate in exchange for money, or gold for silver, or vice versa, it is valid whether little or much, based on exchange; he is not discharged, because it is invalid regarding specific assets. Thus the commentators have stated absolutely, but what is given by the expressions of the famous books is detail: if the discharge regarding them is by way of creation (insha'), then either it is regarding the asset itself or regarding the claim to it. If it is regarding the asset itself, it is invalid from the aspect that he has the right to claim it from the addressee and others, but valid from the aspect of discharge from the description of liability. For this reason, it is said in al-Dhakhira: They said: If a person says to a man who has a slave in his hand, 'You are free of it,' he is free of it. But if he says to him, 'I discharge you from it,' he can claim it, and he only discharged him from its liability. And if it is regarding the claim, then if it is by way of particularity, such as when he discharges him from the claim to this specific asset, then his claim is not heard regarding the addressee but is heard regarding others. For this reason, al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa before the book on iqrar: A man claimed a house or a slave from a man, then the claimant said to the defendant, 'I discharge you from the house, or from my litigation regarding this house, or from the claim regarding this house,' all of this is invalid, even if he then claims that, it is heard, and if he produces evidence, it is accepted, unlike if he said, 'I am free of it,' his evidence is not accepted afterwards. Similarly, if he says, 'I am free of this slave, or I am out,' he cannot claim afterwards, because his saying 'I discharge you from my litigation regarding this house' addresses the individual, so he can litigate with others, unlike his saying 'I am free' because he attributed the discharge to himself absolutely, so he is free. His statement: (And for this reason it is said in al-Dhakhira, etc.) And in al-Khaniyya: Discharge from a usurped asset is a discharge from its liability as a trust in the hand of the usurper. Zufar said: The discharge is not valid, and he is free from the liability for its value. In al-Khulasa: A person who establishes evidence that he was discharged from the usurped asset does not constitute a discharge from the value of the usurped asset; rather it is a discharge from the liability to return, not from the liability for the value, because while the asset exists, returning it is obligatory, not its value, so it is a discharge from what is not obligatory. That means it is not obligatory now while the asset exists, so if he prevents it after demand or consumes it after the discharge, he is liable. In al-Ashbah: Their statement that discharge from specific assets is invalid means it does not become ownership by the discharge; otherwise, the discharge is for dropping liability or is interpreted as a trust. His statement: (Even if he claims that, it is heard) means it is valid if he claims it from other than the addressee, as indicated by the subsequent reasoning. The summary is that if the discharge is by way of particularity. (And if it is by way of generality, he can claim from the addressee and others. For this reason, it is said in al-Qunyah: The spouses separated and each discharged the other from all claims; the husband has standing assets; the wife is not discharged from them, and he can claim them, because discharge applies only to debts, not assets. And if the discharge is by way of information (ikhbar), such as his saying, 'He is free of any right I have against him,' it is valid and includes debt and asset, so the claim is not heard. Similarly, if he says, 'I have no ownership in this asset,' as mentioned in al-Mabsut and al-Muhit. So it is known that his saying 'I have no right against him absolutely' means also regarding a claim to a specific asset. If he attributes the discharge from the asset to the addressee, his claim against him is not heard, but it is heard against others. If he attributes it to himself, the claim is not heard from anyone. His statement: (And if it is by way of generality) is conjuncted to his statement 'if it is by way of particularity,' meaning the creation of discharge from claims to assets if it is by a way that is not valid, such as him saying, 'I discharge you from every claim,' this includes asset and other things; so he can claim the asset from the addressee and others, unlike if he discharged him from a claim to a specific asset, then he cannot claim it from the addressee. And it is not hidden from you that since the discharge of the addressee from the claim to a specific asset is valid, his discharge from it in the form of generality should also be valid, as there is no apparent difference; rather, one might claim precedence for generality. How can it be otherwise, as it contradicts their statement that discharge from claims to assets is valid, unlike discharge from the assets themselves. And in al-Qunyah: If he discharges him after the settlement from all his claims and litigations, it is valid even if the validity of the settlement was not judged. Similarly in Hawi al-Hasiri. As for what the author quoted from al-Qunyah as evidence for his claim, it can be interpreted, because the expression of al-Qunyah regarding debts is: The spouses separated and each discharged the other from all claims, and the husband had seeds in her land and standing assets; the harvest and standing assets are not included in the discharge from all claims. Its interpretation is that this is based on one of two opinions, and the preponderant view is the opposite, or that the wife acknowledged the harvest and assets as belonging to the husband, so it is said that they are not included in the discharge, meaning they do not become the property of the wife, and she is ordered to hand them over to the husband. This is supported by what is in al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa: The lessee discharged the lessor from all claims, then the crop was realized; the lessee came after the lessor had removed the produce and claimed the produce. It was said it is heard, but the more appropriate view is that it is not heard. If the lessor removed the produce first and then the lessee discharged him from the claims, his claim is not heard. This applies if the lessor denies that the crop belongs to the lessee; if he acknowledges that it belongs to the lessee, he is ordered to hand it over to him. So if the wife denies, it is based on the weaker view; if she acknowledges, there is no problem, and it is supported that she acknowledges that. His statement, 'and the husband had seeds and assets,' indicates that she surrendered to that; otherwise, the appropriate wording would be 'and the husband claimed seeds and assets.' So ponder that. His statement: (So it is known that his saying 'I have no right against him absolutely,' etc.) indicates that this is from the discharge by way of information covering.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis from the Hanafi school regarding the validity and effect of ibra' (discharge/acquittance) when directed at specific assets (a'yan) versus claims (da'awa) regarding those assets. The author, Ibn Nujaym, is commenting on a matn (likely Kanz al-Daqa'iq) which stated that discharging someone from a specific asset is invalid. The commentary clarifies that this is not absolute but requires detail.

The passage begins by distinguishing between two modes of ibra': insha' (creating a new legal effect) and ikhbar (informing about an existing state). If the ibra' is by way of insha', it can be directed either at the asset itself ('ayn) or at the claim to it (da'wa). Ibra' from the asset itself is invalid in the sense that it does not transfer ownership; thus, the one who made the discharge can still claim the asset from the recipient or anyone else. However, it is valid as a waiver of the recipient's liability (daman) for the asset's value or compensation. This is supported by a quote from al-Dhakhira: if someone says 'You are free of this slave' to a possessor, the possessor is free from liability, but if he says 'I discharge you from this slave,' he can still claim the slave.

Ibra' from the claim to an asset is further subdivided. If it is particular (khosus) — e.g., 'I discharge you from my claim to this house' — then the claimant cannot sue the recipient regarding that asset, but can still sue others. If the ibra' is general (ta'mim) — e.g., 'I discharge you from all claims' — then the claimant can sue anyone, including the recipient, for the asset itself, because the general ibra' is understood to cover only debts, not assets. This is based on al-Qunyah's example of divorcing spouses.

If the ibra' is by way of ikhbar (informing) — e.g., 'He is free of any right I have against him' — it is valid and covers both debts and assets, so the claimant cannot sue anyone. Similarly, saying 'I have no ownership in this asset' is considered ikhbar and is valid.

The passage also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Zufar holds that ibra' from a usurped asset is invalid even regarding liability, while the majority view (including Abu Hanifa and his disciples) is that it is valid for releasing liability. The text supports the majority view.

Finally, the author clarifies a quote from al-Qunyah regarding spouses, explaining that if the wife acknowledges the husband's ownership of assets, they are not included in the general ibra', but if she denies, the ruling is based on the weaker opinion. The overall principle is that ibra' from assets themselves does not transfer ownership but can release from liability.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): A unilateral legal act by which a creditor relinquishes a right or claim against a debtor, whether for a debt or an asset.
العين (al-'ayn): A specific, tangible asset (e.g., a slave, a house) as opposed to a debt or claim.
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit asserting a right to a specific asset or debt.
الإنشاء (al-insha'): A speech act that creates a new legal effect (e.g., 'I discharge you') rather than merely reporting an existing state.
الإخبار (al-ikhbar): A speech act that informs about an existing state of affairs (e.g., 'He is free of any right I have') without creating a new legal effect.
الخصوص (al-khusus): Particularity: when the ibra' is directed at a specific claim or asset.
التعميم (al-ta'mim): Generality: when the ibra' is expressed in general terms (e.g., 'all claims').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of saying 'I discharge you from this slave' (أبرأتك من هذا العبد)?
A) The recipient becomes the owner of the slave.
B) The recipient is free from liability for the slave's value, but the slave can still be claimed.
C) The discharge is completely invalid and has no effect.
D) The slave becomes a trust in the hands of the recipient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ibra' from a specific asset (al-'ayn) only releases the recipient from liability for its value; it does not transfer ownership.

The Invalidity of a Second Compromise (Sulh Ba'd al-Sulh)
Ruling statement on second compromise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a second compromise is void and explain the legal reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
A second compromise is void because the first compromise finalizes the claim.

When parties reach a compromise (sulh) over a claim, the dispute is legally settled. In Hanafi fiqh, this settlement is binding and cannot be reopened. Therefore, any subsequent compromise agreement on the same matter is automatically void. The text from Bahr al-Raiq makes this explicit: 'Every compromise after a compromise, the second is void.' This lesson explores the rationale and confirms the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific ruling
Locate the phrase in the source that states the ruling. Look for 'وكل صلح بعد صلح فالثاني باطل'. Understand that this is a general statement covering all compromises.
2
Understand the nature of sulh
Recall that in Hanafi madhhab, sulh is a binding contract that transfers ownership or settles a claim, similar to a sale. Once concluded, the rights are transferred, and the claim is extinguished.
3
Apply the principle of finality
Since the first compromise effectively ends the dispute, any subsequent agreement attempting to alter it is invalid. The second 'compromise' lacks a valid subject matter because the claim no longer exists.
This is analogous to selling something you no longer own.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح باب الصلح في الدين إذا كان صادقاً. وفي الأجناس لا يصح لأن فيه معنى البيع وبيع الوقف لا يصح وكل صلح بعد صلح فالثاني باطل، وكذا الصلح بعد الشراء والشراء بعد الشراء جائز .
Book of Compromise, Chapter of Compromise regarding Debt. If he is truthful. And in different species it is not valid because it contains the meaning of sale, and the sale of a waqf is not valid. And every compromise after a compromise, the second is void. And likewise the compromise after a purchase, and a purchase after a purchase is permissible.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kitab al-Sulh. It begins with the chapter heading 'Compromise regarding Debt'. The text first mentions a condition 'if he is truthful' which likely refers to the debtor being truthful about his inability to pay, but the full context is from earlier. It then states that compromise in different species (jins) is invalid because it resembles a sale, and since selling a waqf property is invalid, such a compromise is also invalid. The key ruling is that a second compromise after a prior compromise is void; the first compromise stands and any subsequent agreement is ineffective. Similarly, a compromise after a purchase is also void, whereas a purchase after a purchase is valid. The reasoning is that compromise is a settling of a claim, and once settled, it cannot be reopened. This is based on the principle that compromise has the effect of finalizing rights, similar to a sale.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract to settle a claim by mutual agreement, often involving a payment to drop the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a compromise after a prior compromise according to the text?
A) Valid if both parties consent
B) Void
C) Valid only if a new dispute arises
D) Disliked (makruh)

Key Takeaway
A second compromise is void in Hanafi law because the first compromise settles the claim permanently.

Compromise After Purchase and Purchase After Purchase
Ruling statement contrasting compromise after purchase and purchase after purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on compromise after purchase (void) and purchase after purchase (valid) and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
A compromise after purchase is void, but a purchase after purchase is valid.

The passage from Bahr al-Raiq presents two seemingly related rulings: compromise after purchase is void, while purchase after purchase is valid. This lesson examines why the two differ. The key lies in the nature of compromise (sulh) as a claim-settlement tool versus a purchase as a property transfer. Understanding this distinction clarifies the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate in the source: 'وكذا الصلح بعد الشراء' and 'والشراء بعد الشراء جائز'. Notice that the first is void, the second is permissible.
2
Understand 'compromise after purchase'
This refers to a situation where someone buys an item and then later compromises with the seller over the price or terms. Since the sale is already complete, a subsequent compromise is like attempting to alter a settled transaction.
3
Understand 'purchase after purchase'
A second purchase of the same item from the original seller is permissible because the first purchase gives ownership; a second purchase would be a new contract. The text says it is permissible, not invalid. The difference is that a purchase transfers ownership, while a compromise settles a claim. After a sale, there is no claim to settle, so compromise is misplaced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح باب الصلح في الدين إذا كان صادقاً. وفي الأجناس لا يصح لأن فيه معنى البيع وبيع الوقف لا يصح وكل صلح بعد صلح فالثاني باطل، وكذا الصلح بعد الشراء والشراء بعد الشراء جائز .
Book of Compromise, Chapter of Compromise regarding Debt. If he is truthful. And in different species it is not valid because it contains the meaning of sale, and the sale of a waqf is not valid. And every compromise after a compromise, the second is void. And likewise the compromise after a purchase, and a purchase after a purchase is permissible.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Kitab al-Sulh. It begins with the chapter heading 'Compromise regarding Debt'. The text first mentions a condition 'if he is truthful' which likely refers to the debtor being truthful about his inability to pay, but the full context is from earlier. It then states that compromise in different species (jins) is invalid because it resembles a sale, and since selling a waqf property is invalid, such a compromise is also invalid. The key ruling is that a second compromise after a prior compromise is void; the first compromise stands and any subsequent agreement is ineffective. Similarly, a compromise after a purchase is also void, whereas a purchase after a purchase is valid. The reasoning is that compromise is a settling of a claim, and once settled, it cannot be reopened. This is based on the principle that compromise has the effect of finalizing rights, similar to a sale.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract to settle a claim by mutual agreement, often involving a payment to drop the claim.
شراء (shira'): Purchase, a contract of sale where ownership is transferred in exchange for a price.
باطل (batil): Void, a contract that has no legal effect from its inception.
جائز (ja'iz): Permissible, valid and enforceable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a purchase after a purchase according to the text?
A) Valid (ja'iz)
B) Void (batil)
C) Disliked (makruh)
D) Conditionally valid

Key Takeaway
Compromise after purchase is void because it attempts to settle a claim after the transaction is complete; purchase after purchase is valid as a new sale.
